Give a warning whenever a function is used before being declared. In
C99 mode (B<-std=c99> or B<-std=gnu99>), this warning is
enabled by default and it is made into an error by
B<-pedantic-errors>. This warning is also enabled by
B<-Wall>.


=item B<-Wimplicit> (C and Objective-C only)

Same as B<-Wimplicit-int> and B<-Wimplicit-function-declaration>.
This warning is enabled by B<-Wall>.


=item B<-Wignored-qualifiers> (C and C++ only)

Warn if the return type of a function has a type qualifier
such as C<const>.  For ISO C such a type qualifier has no effect,
since the value returned by a function is not an lvalue.
For C++, the warning is only emitted for scalar types or C<void>.
ISO C prohibits qualified C<void> return types on function
definitions, so such return types always receive a warning
even without this option.

This warning is also enabled by B<-Wextra>.


=item B<-Wmain>

Warn if the type of B<main> is suspicious.  B<main> should be
a function with external linkage, returning int, taking either zero
arguments, two, or three arguments of appropriate types.  This warning
is enabled by default in C++ and is enabled by either B<-Wall>
or B<-pedantic>.


=item B<-Wmissing-braces>

Warn if an aggregate or union initializer is not fully bracketed.  In
the following example, the initializer for B<a> is not fully
bracketed, but that for B<b> is fully bracketed.

	
	int a[2][2] = { 0, 1, 2, 3 };
	int b[2][2] = { { 0, 1 }, { 2, 3 } };


This warning is enabled by B<-Wall>.


=item B<-Wmissing-include-dirs> (C, C++, Objective-C and Objective-C++ only)

Warn if a user-supplied include directory does not exist.


=item B<-Wparentheses>

Warn if parentheses are omitted in certain contexts, such
as when there is an assignment in a context where a truth value
is expected, or when operators are nested whose precedence people
often get confused about.

Also warn if a comparison like B<xE<lt>=yE<lt>=z> appears; this is
equivalent to B<(xE<lt>=y ? 1 : 0) E<lt>= z>, which is a different
interpretation from that of ordinary mathematical notation.

Also warn about constructions where there may be confusion to which
C<if> statement an C<else> branch belongs.  Here is an example of
such a case:

	
	{
	  if (a)
	    if (b)
	      foo ();
	  else
	    bar ();
	}


In C/C++, every C<else> branch belongs to the innermost possible
C<if> statement, which in this example is C<if (b)>.  This is
often not what the programmer expected, as illustrated in the above
example by indentation the programmer chose.  When there is the
potential for this confusion, GCC will issue a warning when this flag
is specified.  To eliminate the warning, add explicit braces around
the innermost C<if> statement so there is no way the C<else>
could belong to the enclosing C<if>.  The resulting code would
look like this:

	
	{
	  if (a)
	    {
	      if (b)
	        foo ();
	      else
	        bar ();
	    }
	}


Also warn for dangerous uses of the 
?: with omitted middle operand GNU extension. When the condition
in the ?: operator is a boolean expression the omitted value will
be always 1. Often the user expects it to be a value computed
inside the conditional expression instead. 

This warning is enabled by B<-Wall>.


=item B<-Wsequence-point>

Warn about code that may have undefined semantics because of violations
of sequence point rules in the C and C++ standards.

The C and C++ standards defines the order in which expressions in a C/C++
program are evaluated in terms of I<sequence points>, which represent
a partial ordering between the execution of parts of the program: those
executed before the sequence point, and those executed after it.  These
occur after the evaluation of a full expression (one which is not part
of a larger expression), after the evaluation of the first operand of a
C<&&>, C<||>, C<? :> or C<,> (comma) operator, before a
function is called (but after the evaluation of its arguments and the
expression denoting the called function), and in certain other places.
Other than as expressed by the sequence point rules, the order of
evaluation of subexpressions of an expression is not specified.  All
these rules describe only a partial order rather than a total order,
since, for example, if two functions are called within one expression
with no sequence point between them, the order in which the functions
are called is not specified.  However, the standards committee have
ruled that function calls do not overlap.

It is not specified when between sequence points modifications to the
values of objects take effect.  Programs whose behavior depends on this
have undefined behavior; the C and C++ standards specify that "Between
the previous and next sequence point an object shall have its stored
value modified at most once by the evaluation of an expression.
Furthermore, the prior value shall be read only to determine the value
to be stored.".  If a program breaks these rules, the results on any
particular implementation are entirely unpredictable.

Examples of code with undefined behavior are C<a = a++;>, C<a[n]
= b[n++]> and C<a[i++] = i;>.  Some more complicated cases are not
diagnosed by this option, and it may give an occasional false positive
result, but in general it has been found fairly effective at detecting
this sort of problem in programs.

The standard is worded confusingly, therefore there is some debate
over the precise meaning of the sequence point rules in subtle cases.
Links to discussions of the problem, including proposed formal
definitions, may be found on the GCC readings page, at
E<lt>B<http://gcc.gnu.org/readings.html>E<gt>.

This warning is enabled by B<-Wall> for C and C++.


=item B<-Wreturn-type>

Warn whenever a function is defined with a return-type that defaults
to C<int>.  Also warn about any C<return> statement with no
return-value in a function whose return-type is not C<void>
(falling off the end of the function body is considered returning
without a value), and about a C<return> statement with an
expression in a function whose return-type is C<void>.

For C++, a function without return type always produces a diagnostic
message, even when B<-Wno-return-type> is specified.  The only
exceptions are B<main> and functions defined in system headers.

This warning is enabled by B<-Wall>.


=item B<-Wswitch>

Warn whenever a C<switch> statement has an index of enumerated type
and lacks a C<case> for one or more of the named codes of that
enumeration.  (The presence of a C<default> label prevents this
warning.)  C<case> labels outside the enumeration range also
provoke warnings when this option is used (even if there is a
C<default> label).
This warning is enabled by B<-Wall>.


=item B<-Wswitch-default>

Warn whenever a C<switch> statement does not have a C<default>
case.


=item B<-Wswitch-enum>

Warn whenever a C<switch> statement has an index of enumerated type
and lacks a C<case> for one or more of the named codes of that
enumeration.  C<case> labels outside the enumeration range also
provoke warnings when this option is used.  The only difference
between B<-Wswitch> and this option is that this option gives a
warning about an omitted enumeration code even if there is a
C<default> label.


=item B<-Wsync-nand> (C and C++ only)

Warn when C<__sync_fetch_and_nand> and C<__sync_nand_and_fetch>
built-in functions are used.  These functions changed semantics in GCC 4.4.


=item B<-Wtrigraphs>

Warn if any trigraphs are encountered that might change the meaning of
the program (trigraphs within comments are not warned about).
This warning is enabled by B<-Wall>.


=item B<-Wunused-but-set-parameter>

Warn whenever a function parameter is assigned to, but otherwise unused
(aside from its declaration).

To suppress this warning use the B<unused> attribute.

This warning is also enabled by B<-Wunused> together with
B<-Wextra>.


=item B<-Wunused-but-set-variable>

Warn whenever a local variable is assigned to, but otherwise unused
(aside from its declaration).
This warning is enabled by B<-Wall>.

To suppress this warning use the B<unused> attribute.

This warning is also enabled by B<-Wunused>, which is enabled
by B<-Wall>.


=item B<-Wunused-function>

Warn whenever a static function is declared but not defined or a
non-inline static function is unused.
This warning is enabled by B<-Wall>.


=item B<-Wunused-label>

Warn whenever a label is declared but not used.
This warning is enabled by B<-Wall>.

To suppress this warning use the B<unused> attribute.


=item B<-Wunused-parameter>

Warn whenever a function parameter is unused aside from its declaration.

To suppress this warning use the B<unused> attribute.


=item B<-Wno-unused-result>

Do not warn if a caller of a function marked with attribute
C<warn_unused_result> does not use
its return value. The default is B<-Wunused-result>.


=item B<-Wunused-variable>

Warn whenever a local variable or non-constant static variable is unused
aside from its declaration.
This warning is enabled by B<-Wall>.

To suppress this warning use the B<unused> attribute.


=item B<-Wunused-value>

Warn whenever a statement computes a result that is explicitly not
used. To suppress this warning cast the unused expression to
B<void>. This includes an expression-statement or the left-hand
side of a comma expression that contains no side effects. For example,
an expression such as B<x[i,j]> will cause a warning, while
B<x[(void)i,j]> will not.

This warning is enabled by B<-Wall>.


=item B<-Wunused>

All the above B<-Wunused> options combined.

In order to get a warning about an unused function parameter, you must
either specify B<-Wextra -Wunused> (note that B<-Wall> implies
B<-Wunused>), or separately specify B<-Wunused-parameter>.


=item B<-Wuninitialized>

Warn if an automatic variable is used without first being initialized
or if a variable may be clobbered by a C<setjmp> call. In C++,
warn if a non-static reference or non-static B<const> member
appears in a class without constructors.

If you want to warn about code which uses the uninitialized value of the
variable in its own initializer, use the B<-Winit-self> option.

These warnings occur for individual uninitialized or clobbered
elements of structure, union or array variables as well as for
variables which are uninitialized or clobbered as a whole.  They do
not occur for variables or elements declared C<volatile>.  Because
these warnings depend on optimization, the exact variables or elements
for which there are warnings will depend on the precise optimization
options and version of GCC used.

Note that there may be no warning about a variable that is used only
to compute a value that itself is never used, because such
computations may be deleted by data flow analysis before the warnings
are printed.

These warnings are made optional because GCC is not smart
enough to see all the reasons why the code might be correct
despite appearing to have an error.  Here is one example of how
this can happen:

	
	{
	  int x;
	  switch (y)
	    {
	    case 1: x = 1;
	      break;
	    case 2: x = 4;
	      break;
	    case 3: x = 5;
	    }
	  foo (x);
	}


If the value of C<y> is always 1, 2 or 3, then C<x> is
always initialized, but GCC doesn't know this.  Here is
another common case:

	
	{
	  int save_y;
	  if (change_y) save_y = y, y = new_y;
	  ...
	  if (change_y) y = save_y;
	}


This has no bug because C<save_y> is used only if it is set.

This option also warns when a non-volatile automatic variable might be
changed by a call to C<longjmp>.  These warnings as well are possible
only in optimizing compilation.

The compiler sees only the calls to C<setjmp>.  It cannot know
where C<longjmp> will be called; in fact, a signal handler could
call it at any point in the code.  As a result, you may get a warning
even when there is in fact no problem because C<longjmp> cannot
in fact be called at the place which would cause a problem.

Some spurious warnings can be avoided if you declare all the functions
you use that never return as C<noreturn>.  

This warning is enabled by B<-Wall> or B<-Wextra>.


=item B<-Wunknown-pragmas>

Warn when a #pragma directive is encountered which is not understood by
GCC.  If this command line option is used, warnings will even be issued
for unknown pragmas in system header files.  This is not the case if
the warnings were only enabled by the B<-Wall> command line option.


=item B<-Wno-pragmas>

Do not warn about misuses of pragmas, such as incorrect parameters,
invalid syntax, or conflicts between pragmas.  See also
B<-Wunknown-pragmas>.


=item B<-Wstrict-aliasing>

This option is only active when B<-fstrict-aliasing> is active.
It warns about code which might break the strict aliasing rules that the
compiler is using for optimization.  The warning does not catch all
cases, but does attempt to catch the more common pitfalls.  It is
included in B<-Wall>.
It is equivalent to B<-Wstrict-aliasing=3>


=item B<-Wstrict-aliasing=n>

This option is only active when B<-fstrict-aliasing> is active.
It warns about code which might break the strict aliasing rules that the
compiler is using for optimization.
Higher levels correspond to higher accuracy (fewer false positives).
Higher levels also correspond to more effort, similar to the way -O works.
B<-Wstrict-aliasing> is equivalent to B<-Wstrict-aliasing=n>,
with n=3.

Level 1: Most aggressive, quick, least accurate.
Possibly useful when higher levels
do not warn but -fstrict-aliasing still breaks the code, as it has very few
false negatives.  However, it has many false positives.
Warns for all pointer conversions between possibly incompatible types,
even if never dereferenced.  Runs in the frontend only.

Level 2: Aggressive, quick, not too precise.
May still have many false positives (not as many as level 1 though),
and few false negatives (but possibly more than level 1).
Unlike level 1, it only warns when an address is taken.  Warns about
incomplete types.  Runs in the frontend only.

Level 3 (default for B<-Wstrict-aliasing>):
Should have very few false positives and few false
negatives.  Slightly slower than levels 1 or 2 when optimization is enabled.
Takes care of the common pun+dereference pattern in the frontend:
C<*(int*)&some_float>.
If optimization is enabled, it also runs in the backend, where it deals
with multiple statement cases using flow-sensitive points-to information.
Only warns when the converted pointer is dereferenced.
Does not warn about incomplete types.


=item B<-Wstrict-overflow>


=item B<-Wstrict-overflow=>I<n>

This option is only active when B<-fstrict-overflow> is active.
It warns about cases where the compiler optimizes based on the
assumption that signed overflow does not occur.  Note that it does not
warn about all cases where the code might overflow: it only warns
about cases where the compiler implements some optimization.  Thus
this warning depends on the optimization level.

An optimization which assumes that signed overflow does not occur is
perfectly safe if the values of the variables involved are such that
overflow never does, in fact, occur.  Therefore this warning can
easily give a false positive: a warning about code which is not
actually a problem.  To help focus on important issues, several
warning levels are defined.  No warnings are issued for the use of
undefined signed overflow when estimating how many iterations a loop
will require, in particular when determining whether a loop will be
executed at all.


=over 4


=item B<-Wstrict-overflow=1>

Warn about cases which are both questionable and easy to avoid.  For
example: C<x + 1 E<gt> x>; with B<-fstrict-overflow>, the
compiler will simplify this to C<1>.  This level of
B<-Wstrict-overflow> is enabled by B<-Wall>; higher levels
are not, and must be explicitly requested.


=item B<-Wstrict-overflow=2>

Also warn about other cases where a comparison is simplified to a
constant.  For example: C<abs (x) E<gt>= 0>.  This can only be
simplified when B<-fstrict-overflow> is in effect, because
C<abs (INT_MIN)> overflows to C<INT_MIN>, which is less than
zero.  B<-Wstrict-overflow> (with no level) is the same as
B<-Wstrict-overflow=2>.


=item B<-Wstrict-overflow=3>

Also warn about other cases where a comparison is simplified.  For
example: C<x + 1 E<gt> 1> will be simplified to C<x E<gt> 0>.


=item B<-Wstrict-overflow=4>

Also warn about other simplifications not covered by the above cases.
For example: C<(x * 10) / 5> will be simplified to C<x * 2>.


=item B<-Wstrict-overflow=5>

Also warn about cases where the compiler reduces the magnitude of a
constant involved in a comparison.  For example: C<x + 2 E<gt> y> will
be simplified to C<x + 1 E<gt>= y>.  This is reported only at the
highest warning level because this simplification applies to many
comparisons, so this warning level will give a very large number of
false positives.

=back



=item B<-Wsuggest-attribute=>[B<pure>|B<const>|B<noreturn>]

Warn for cases where adding an attribute may be beneficial. The
attributes currently supported are listed below.


=over 4


=item B<-Wsuggest-attribute=pure>


=item B<-Wsuggest-attribute=const>


=item B<-Wsuggest-attribute=noreturn>


Warn about functions which might be candidates for attributes
C<pure>, C<const> or C<noreturn>.  The compiler only warns for
functions visible in other compilation units or (in the case of C<pure> and
C<const>) if it cannot prove that the function returns normally. A function
returns normally if it doesn't contain an infinite loop nor returns abnormally
by throwing, calling C<abort()> or trapping.  This analysis requires option
B<-fipa-pure-const>, which is enabled by default at B<-O> and
higher.  Higher optimization levels improve the accuracy of the analysis.

=back



=item B<-Warray-bounds>

This option is only active when B<-ftree-vrp> is active
(default for B<-O2> and above). It warns about subscripts to arrays
that are always out of bounds. This warning is enabled by B<-Wall>.


=item B<-Wno-div-by-zero>

Do not warn about compile-time integer division by zero.  Floating point
division by zero is not warned about, as it can be a legitimate way of
obtaining infinities and NaNs.


=item B<-Wsystem-headers>

Print warning messages for constructs found in system header files.
Warnings from system headers are normally suppressed, on the assumption
that they usually do not indicate real problems and would only make the
compiler output harder to read.  Using this command line option tells
GCC to emit warnings from system headers as if they occurred in user
code.  However, note that using B<-Wall> in conjunction with this
option will I<not> warn about unknown pragmas in system
headers---for that, B<-Wunknown-pragmas> must also be used.


=item B<-Wtrampolines>

 Warn about trampolines generated for pointers to nested functions.
 
 A trampoline is a small piece of data or code that is created at run
 time on the stack when the address of a nested function is taken, and
 is used to call the nested function indirectly.  For some targets, it
 is made up of data only and thus requires no special treatment.  But,
 for most targets, it is made up of code and thus requires the stack
 to be made executable in order for the program to work properly.


=item B<-Wfloat-equal>

Warn if floating point values are used in equality comparisons.

The idea behind this is that sometimes it is convenient (for the
programmer) to consider floating-point values as approximations to
infinitely precise real numbers.  If you are doing this, then you need
to compute (by analyzing the code, or in some other way) the maximum or
likely maximum error that the computation introduces, and allow for it
when performing comparisons (and when producing output, but that's a
different problem).  In particular, instead of testing for equality, you
would check to see whether the two values have ranges that overlap; and
this is done with the relational operators, so equality comparisons are
probably mistaken.


=item B<-Wtraditional> (C and Objective-C only)

Warn about certain constructs that behave differently in traditional and
ISO C.  Also warn about ISO C constructs that have no traditional C
equivalent, and/or problematic constructs which should be avoided.


=over 4


=item *

Macro parameters that appear within string literals in the macro body.
In traditional C macro replacement takes place within string literals,
but does not in ISO C.


=item *

In traditional C, some preprocessor directives did not exist.
Traditional preprocessors would only consider a line to be a directive
if the B<#> appeared in column 1 on the line.  Therefore
B<-Wtraditional> warns about directives that traditional C
understands but would ignore because the B<#> does not appear as the
first character on the line.  It also suggests you hide directives like
B<#pragma> not understood by traditional C by indenting them.  Some
traditional implementations would not recognize B<#elif>, so it
suggests avoiding it altogether.


=item *

A function-like macro that appears without arguments.


=item *

The unary plus operator.


=item *

The B<U> integer constant suffix, or the B<F> or B<L> floating point
constant suffixes.  (Traditional C does support the B<L> suffix on integer
constants.)  Note, these suffixes appear in macros defined in the system
headers of most modern systems, e.g. the B<_MIN>/B<_MAX> macros in C<E<lt>limits.hE<gt>>.
Use of these macros in user code might normally lead to spurious
warnings, however GCC's integrated preprocessor has enough context to
avoid warning in these cases.


=item *

A function declared external in one block and then used after the end of
the block.


=item *

A C<switch> statement has an operand of type C<long>.


=item *

A non-C<static> function declaration follows a C<static> one.
This construct is not accepted by some traditional C compilers.


=item *

The ISO type of an integer constant has a different width or
signedness from its traditional type.  This warning is only issued if
the base of the constant is ten.  I.e. hexadecimal or octal values, which
typically represent bit patterns, are not warned about.


=item *

Usage of ISO string concatenation is detected.


=item *

Initialization of automatic aggregates.


=item *

Identifier conflicts with labels.  Traditional C lacks a separate
namespace for labels.


=item *

Initialization of unions.  If the initializer is zero, the warning is
omitted.  This is done under the assumption that the zero initializer in
user code appears conditioned on e.g. C<__STDC__> to avoid missing
initializer warnings and relies on default initialization to zero in the
traditional C case.


=item *

Conversions by prototypes between fixed/floating point values and vice
versa.  The absence of these prototypes when compiling with traditional
C would cause serious problems.  This is a subset of the possible
conversion warnings, for the full set use B<-Wtraditional-conversion>.


=item *

Use of ISO C style function definitions.  This warning intentionally is
I<not> issued for prototype declarations or variadic functions
because these ISO C features will appear in your code when using
libiberty's traditional C compatibility macros, C<PARAMS> and
C<VPARAMS>.  This warning is also bypassed for nested functions
because that feature is already a GCC extension and thus not relevant to
traditional C compatibility.

=back



=item B<-Wtraditional-conversion> (C and Objective-C only)

Warn if a prototype causes a type conversion that is different from what
would happen to the same argument in the absence of a prototype.  This
includes conversions of fixed point to floating and vice versa, and
conversions changing the width or signedness of a fixed point argument
except when the same as the default promotion.


=item B<-Wdeclaration-after-statement> (C and Objective-C only)

Warn when a declaration is found after a statement in a block.  This
construct, known from C++, was introduced with ISO C99 and is by default
allowed in GCC.  It is not supported by ISO C90 and was not supported by
GCC versions before GCC 3.0.  


=item B<-Wundef>

Warn if an undefined identifier is evaluated in an B<#if> directive.


=item B<-Wno-endif-labels>

Do not warn whenever an B<#else> or an B<#endif> are followed by text.


=item B<-Wshadow>

Warn whenever a local variable or type declaration shadows another variable,
parameter, type, or class member (in C++), or whenever a built-in function
is shadowed. Note that in C++, the compiler will not warn if a local variable
shadows a struct/class/enum, but will warn if it shadows an explicit typedef.


=item B<-Wlarger-than=>I<len>

Warn whenever an object of larger than I<len> bytes is defined.


=item B<-Wframe-larger-than=>I<len>

Warn if the size of a function frame is larger than I<len> bytes.
The computation done to determine the stack frame size is approximate
and not conservative.
The actual requirements may be somewhat greater than I<len>
even if you do not get a warning.  In addition, any space allocated
via C<alloca>, variable-length arrays, or related constructs
is not included by the compiler when determining
whether or not to issue a warning.


=item B<-Wunsafe-loop-optimizations>

Warn if the loop cannot be optimized because the compiler could not
assume anything on the bounds of the loop indices.  With
B<-funsafe-loop-optimizations> warn if the compiler made
such assumptions.


=item B<-Wno-pedantic-ms-format> (MinGW targets only)

Disables the warnings about non-ISO C<printf> / C<scanf> format
width specifiers C<I32>, C<I64>, and C<I> used on Windows targets
depending on the MS runtime, when you are using the options B<-Wformat>
and B<-pedantic> without gnu-extensions.


=item B<-Wpointer-arith>

Warn about anything that depends on the "size of" a function type or
of C<void>.  GNU C assigns these types a size of 1, for
convenience in calculations with C<void *> pointers and pointers
to functions.  In C++, warn also when an arithmetic operation involves
C<NULL>.  This warning is also enabled by B<-pedantic>.


=item B<-Wtype-limits>

Warn if a comparison is always true or always false due to the limited
range of the data type, but do not warn for constant expressions.  For
example, warn if an unsigned variable is compared against zero with
B<E<lt>> or B<E<gt>=>.  This warning is also enabled by
B<-Wextra>.


=item B<-Wbad-function-cast> (C and Objective-C only)

Warn whenever a function call is cast to a non-matching type.
For example, warn if C<int malloc()> is cast to C<anything *>.


=item B<-Wc++-compat> (C and Objective-C only)

Warn about ISO C constructs that are outside of the common subset of
ISO C and ISO C++, e.g. request for implicit conversion from
C<void *> to a pointer to non-C<void> type.


=item B<-Wc++0x-compat> (C++ and Objective-C++ only)

Warn about C++ constructs whose meaning differs between ISO C++ 1998 and
ISO C++ 200x, e.g., identifiers in ISO C++ 1998 that will become keywords
in ISO C++ 200x.  This warning is enabled by B<-Wall>.


=item B<-Wcast-qual>

Warn whenever a pointer is cast so as to remove a type qualifier from
the target type.  For example, warn if a C<const char *> is cast
to an ordinary C<char *>.

Also warn when making a cast which introduces a type qualifier in an
unsafe way.  For example, casting C<char **> to C<const char **>
is unsafe, as in this example:

	
	  /* p is char ** value.  */
	  const char **q = (const char **) p;
	  /* Assignment of readonly string to const char * is OK.  */
	  *q = "string";
	  /* Now char** pointer points to read-only memory.  */
	  **p = 'b';



=item B<-Wcast-align>

Warn whenever a pointer is cast such that the required alignment of the
target is increased.  For example, warn if a C<char *> is cast to
an C<int *> on machines where integers can only be accessed at
two- or four-byte boundaries.


=item B<-Wwrite-strings>

When compiling C, give string constants the type C<const
char[I<length>]> so that copying the address of one into a
non-C<const> C<char *> pointer will get a warning.  These
warnings will help you find at compile time code that can try to write
into a string constant, but only if you have been very careful about
using C<const> in declarations and prototypes.  Otherwise, it will
just be a nuisance. This is why we did not make B<-Wall> request
these warnings.

When compiling C++, warn about the deprecated conversion from string
literals to C<char *>.  This warning is enabled by default for C++
programs.


=item B<-Wclobbered>

Warn for variables that might be changed by B<longjmp> or
B<vfork>.  This warning is also enabled by B<-Wextra>.


=item B<-Wconversion>

Warn for implicit conversions that may alter a value. This includes
conversions between real and integer, like C<abs (x)> when
C<x> is C<double>; conversions between signed and unsigned,
like C<unsigned ui = -1>; and conversions to smaller types, like
C<sqrtf (M_PI)>. Do not warn for explicit casts like C<abs
((int) x)> and C<ui = (unsigned) -1>, or if the value is not
changed by the conversion like in C<abs (2.0)>.  Warnings about
conversions between signed and unsigned integers can be disabled by
using B<-Wno-sign-conversion>.

For C++, also warn for confusing overload resolution for user-defined
conversions; and conversions that will never use a type conversion
operator: conversions to C<void>, the same type, a base class or a
reference to them. Warnings about conversions between signed and
unsigned integers are disabled by default in C++ unless
B<-Wsign-conversion> is explicitly enabled.


=item B<-Wno-conversion-null> (C++ and Objective-C++ only)

Do not warn for conversions between C<NULL> and non-pointer
types. B<-Wconversion-null> is enabled by default.


=item B<-Wempty-body>

Warn if an empty body occurs in an B<if>, B<else> or B<do
while> statement.  This warning is also enabled by B<-Wextra>.


=item B<-Wenum-compare>

Warn about a comparison between values of different enum types. In C++
this warning is enabled by default.  In C this warning is enabled by
B<-Wall>.


=item B<-Wjump-misses-init> (C, Objective-C only)

Warn if a C<goto> statement or a C<switch> statement jumps
forward across the initialization of a variable, or jumps backward to a
label after the variable has been initialized.  This only warns about
variables which are initialized when they are declared.  This warning is
only supported for C and Objective C; in C++ this sort of branch is an
error in any case.

B<-Wjump-misses-init> is included in B<-Wc++-compat>.  It
can be disabled with the B<-Wno-jump-misses-init> option.


=item B<-Wsign-compare>

Warn when a comparison between signed and unsigned values could produce
an incorrect result when the signed value is converted to unsigned.
This warning is also enabled by B<-Wextra>; to get the other warnings
of B<-Wextra> without this warning, use B<-Wextra -Wno-sign-compare>.


=item B<-Wsign-conversion>

Warn for implicit conversions that may change the sign of an integer
value, like assigning a signed integer expression to an unsigned
integer variable. An explicit cast silences the warning. In C, this
option is enabled also by B<-Wconversion>.


=item B<-Waddress>

Warn about suspicious uses of memory addresses. These include using
the address of a function in a conditional expression, such as
C<void func(void); if (func)>, and comparisons against the memory
address of a string literal, such as C<if (x == "abc")>.  Such
uses typically indicate a programmer error: the address of a function
always evaluates to true, so their use in a conditional usually
indicate that the programmer forgot the parentheses in a function
call; and comparisons against string literals result in unspecified
behavior and are not portable in C, so they usually indicate that the
programmer intended to use C<strcmp>.  This warning is enabled by
B<-Wall>.


=item B<-Wlogical-op>

Warn about suspicious uses of logical operators in expressions.
This includes using logical operators in contexts where a
bit-wise operator is likely to be expected.


=item B<-Waggregate-return>

Warn if any functions that return structures or unions are defined or
called.  (In languages where you can return an array, this also elicits
a warning.)


=item B<-Wno-attributes>

Do not warn if an unexpected C<__attribute__> is used, such as
unrecognized attributes, function attributes applied to variables,
etc.  This will not stop errors for incorrect use of supported
attributes.


=item B<-Wno-builtin-macro-redefined>

Do not warn if certain built-in macros are redefined.  This suppresses
warnings for redefinition of C<__TIMESTAMP__>, C<__TIME__>,
C<__DATE__>, C<__FILE__>, and C<__BASE_FILE__>.


=item B<-Wstrict-prototypes> (C and Objective-C only)

Warn if a function is declared or defined without specifying the
argument types.  (An old-style function definition is permitted without
a warning if preceded by a declaration which specifies the argument
types.)


=item B<-Wold-style-declaration> (C and Objective-C only)

Warn for obsolescent usages, according to the C Standard, in a
declaration. For example, warn if storage-class specifiers like
C<static> are not the first things in a declaration.  This warning
is also enabled by B<-Wextra>.


=item B<-Wold-style-definition> (C and Objective-C only)

Warn if an old-style function definition is used.  A warning is given
even if there is a previous prototype.


=item B<-Wmissing-parameter-type> (C and Objective-C only)

A function parameter is declared without a type specifier in K&R-style
functions:

	
	void foo(bar) { }


This warning is also enabled by B<-Wextra>.


=item B<-Wmissing-prototypes> (C and Objective-C only)

Warn if a global function is defined without a previous prototype
declaration.  This warning is issued even if the definition itself
provides a prototype.  The aim is to detect global functions that fail
to be declared in header files.


=item B<-Wmissing-declarations>

Warn if a global function is defined without a previous declaration.
Do so even if the definition itself provides a prototype.
Use this option to detect global functions that are not declared in
header files.  In C++, no warnings are issued for function templates,
or for inline functions, or for functions in anonymous namespaces.


=item B<-Wmissing-field-initializers>

Warn if a structure's initializer has some fields missing.  For
example, the following code would cause such a warning, because
C<x.h> is implicitly zero:

	
	struct s { int f, g, h; };
	struct s x = { 3, 4 };


This option does not warn about designated initializers, so the following
modification would not trigger a warning:

	
	struct s { int f, g, h; };
	struct s x = { .f = 3, .g = 4 };


This warning is included in B<-Wextra>.  To get other B<-Wextra>
warnings without this one, use B<-Wextra -Wno-missing-field-initializers>.


=item B<-Wmissing-format-attribute>

Warn about function pointers which might be candidates for C<format>
attributes.  Note these are only possible candidates, not absolute ones.
GCC will guess that function pointers with C<format> attributes that
are used in assignment, initialization, parameter passing or return
statements should have a corresponding C<format> attribute in the
resulting type.  I.e. the left-hand side of the assignment or
initialization, the type of the parameter variable, or the return type
of the containing function respectively should also have a C<format>
attribute to avoid the warning.

GCC will also warn about function definitions which might be
candidates for C<format> attributes.  Again, these are only
possible candidates.  GCC will guess that C<format> attributes
might be appropriate for any function that calls a function like
C<vprintf> or C<vscanf>, but this might not always be the
case, and some functions for which C<format> attributes are
appropriate may not be detected.


=item B<-Wno-multichar>

Do not warn if a multicharacter constant (B<'FOOF'>) is used.
Usually they indicate a typo in the user's code, as they have
implementation-defined values, and should not be used in portable code.


=item B<-Wnormalized=E<lt>none|id|nfc|nfkcE<gt>>

In ISO C and ISO C++, two identifiers are different if they are
different sequences of characters.  However, sometimes when characters
outside the basic ASCII character set are used, you can have two
different character sequences that look the same.  To avoid confusion,
the ISO 10646 standard sets out some I<normalization rules> which
when applied ensure that two sequences that look the same are turned into
the same sequence.  GCC can warn you if you are using identifiers which
have not been normalized; this option controls that warning.

There are four levels of warning that GCC supports.  The default is
B<-Wnormalized=nfc>, which warns about any identifier which is
not in the ISO 10646 "C" normalized form, I<NFC>.  NFC is the
recommended form for most uses.

Unfortunately, there are some characters which ISO C and ISO C++ allow
in identifiers that when turned into NFC aren't allowable as
identifiers.  That is, there's no way to use these symbols in portable
ISO C or C++ and have all your identifiers in NFC.
B<-Wnormalized=id> suppresses the warning for these characters.
It is hoped that future versions of the standards involved will correct
this, which is why this option is not the default.

You can switch the warning off for all characters by writing
B<-Wnormalized=none>.  You would only want to do this if you
were using some other normalization scheme (like "D"), because
otherwise you can easily create bugs that are literally impossible to see.

Some characters in ISO 10646 have distinct meanings but look identical
in some fonts or display methodologies, especially once formatting has
been applied.  For instance C<\u207F>, "SUPERSCRIPT LATIN SMALL
LETTER N", will display just like a regular C<n> which has been
placed in a superscript.  ISO 10646 defines the I<NFKC>
normalization scheme to convert all these into a standard form as
well, and GCC will warn if your code is not in NFKC if you use
B<-Wnormalized=nfkc>.  This warning is comparable to warning
about every identifier that contains the letter O because it might be
confused with the digit 0, and so is not the default, but may be
useful as a local coding convention if the programming environment is
unable to be fixed to display these characters distinctly.


=item B<-Wno-deprecated>

Do not warn about usage of deprecated features.  


=item B<-Wno-deprecated-declarations>

Do not warn about uses of functions,
variables, and types marked as deprecated by using the C<deprecated>
attribute.


=item B<-Wno-overflow>

Do not warn about compile-time overflow in constant expressions.


=item B<-Woverride-init> (C and Objective-C only)

Warn if an initialized field without side effects is overridden when
using designated initializers.

This warning is included in B<-Wextra>.  To get other
B<-Wextra> warnings without this one, use B<-Wextra
-Wno-override-init>.


=item B<-Wpacked>

Warn if a structure is given the packed attribute, but the packed
attribute has no effect on the layout or size of the structure.
Such structures may be mis-aligned for little benefit.  For
instance, in this code, the variable C<f.x> in C<struct bar>
will be misaligned even though C<struct bar> does not itself
have the packed attribute:

	
	struct foo {
	  int x;
	  char a, b, c, d;
	} __attribute__((packed));
	struct bar {
	  char z;
	  struct foo f;
	};



=item B<-Wpacked-bitfield-compat>

The 4.1, 4.2 and 4.3 series of GCC ignore the C<packed> attribute
on bit-fields of type C<char>.  This has been fixed in GCC 4.4 but
the change can lead to differences in the structure layout.  GCC
informs you when the offset of such a field has changed in GCC 4.4.
For example there is no longer a 4-bit padding between field C<a>
and C<b> in this structure:

	
	struct foo
	{
	  char a:4;
	  char b:8;
	} __attribute__ ((packed));


This warning is enabled by default.  Use
B<-Wno-packed-bitfield-compat> to disable this warning.


=item B<-Wpadded>

Warn if padding is included in a structure, either to align an element
of the structure or to align the whole structure.  Sometimes when this
happens it is possible to rearrange the fields of the structure to
reduce the padding and so make the structure smaller.


=item B<-Wredundant-decls>

Warn if anything is declared more than once in the same scope, even in
cases where multiple declaration is valid and changes nothing.


=item B<-Wnested-externs> (C and Objective-C only)

Warn if an C<extern> declaration is encountered within a function.


=item B<-Winline>

Warn if a function can not be inlined and it was declared as inline.
Even with this option, the compiler will not warn about failures to
inline functions declared in system headers.

The compiler uses a variety of heuristics to determine whether or not
to inline a function.  For example, the compiler takes into account
the size of the function being inlined and the amount of inlining
that has already been done in the current function.  Therefore,
seemingly insignificant changes in the source program can cause the
warnings produced by B<-Winline> to appear or disappear.


=item B<-Wno-invalid-offsetof> (C++ and Objective-C++ only)

Suppress warnings from applying the B<offsetof> macro to a non-POD
type.  According to the 1998 ISO C++ standard, applying B<offsetof>
to a non-POD type is undefined.  In existing C++ implementations,
however, B<offsetof> typically gives meaningful results even when
applied to certain kinds of non-POD types. (Such as a simple
B<struct> that fails to be a POD type only by virtue of having a
constructor.)  This flag is for users who are aware that they are
writing nonportable code and who have deliberately chosen to ignore the
warning about it.

The restrictions on B<offsetof> may be relaxed in a future version
of the C++ standard.


=item B<-Wno-int-to-pointer-cast>

Suppress warnings from casts to pointer type of an integer of a
different size. In C++, casting to a pointer type of smaller size is
an error. B<Wint-to-pointer-cast> is enabled by default.



=item B<-Wno-pointer-to-int-cast> (C and Objective-C only)

Suppress warnings from casts from a pointer to an integer type of a
different size.


=item B<-Winvalid-pch>

Warn if a precompiled header is found in
the search path but can't be used.


=item B<-Wlong-long>

Warn if B<long long> type is used.  This is enabled by either
B<-pedantic> or B<-Wtraditional> in ISO C90 and C++98
modes.  To inhibit the warning messages, use B<-Wno-long-long>.


=item B<-Wvariadic-macros>

Warn if variadic macros are used in pedantic ISO C90 mode, or the GNU
alternate syntax when in pedantic ISO C99 mode.  This is default.
To inhibit the warning messages, use B<-Wno-variadic-macros>.


=item B<-Wvla>

Warn if variable length array is used in the code.
B<-Wno-vla> will prevent the B<-pedantic> warning of
the variable length array.


=item B<-Wvolatile-register-var>

Warn if a register variable is declared volatile.  The volatile
modifier does not inhibit all optimizations that may eliminate reads
and/or writes to register variables.  This warning is enabled by
B<-Wall>.


=item B<-Wdisabled-optimization>

Warn if a requested optimization pass is disabled.  This warning does
not generally indicate that there is anything wrong with your code; it
merely indicates that GCC's optimizers were unable to handle the code
effectively.  Often, the problem is that your code is too big or too
complex; GCC will refuse to optimize programs when the optimization
itself is likely to take inordinate amounts of time.


=item B<-Wpointer-sign> (C and Objective-C only)

Warn for pointer argument passing or assignment with different signedness.
This option is only supported for C and Objective-C.  It is implied by
B<-Wall> and by B<-pedantic>, which can be disabled with
B<-Wno-pointer-sign>.


=item B<-Wstack-protector>

This option is only active when B<-fstack-protector> is active.  It
warns about functions that will not be protected against stack smashing.


=item B<-Wno-mudflap>

Suppress warnings about constructs that cannot be instrumented by
B<-fmudflap>.


=item B<-Woverlength-strings>

Warn about string constants which are longer than the "minimum
maximum" length specified in the C standard.  Modern compilers
generally allow string constants which are much longer than the
standard's minimum limit, but very portable programs should avoid
using longer strings.

The limit applies I<after> string constant concatenation, and does
not count the trailing NUL.  In C90, the limit was 509 characters; in
C99, it was raised to 4095.  C++98 does not specify a normative
minimum maximum, so we do not diagnose overlength strings in C++.

This option is implied by B<-pedantic>, and can be disabled with
B<-Wno-overlength-strings>.


=item B<-Wunsuffixed-float-constants> (C and Objective-C only)


GCC will issue a warning for any floating constant that does not have
a suffix.  When used together with B<-Wsystem-headers> it will
warn about such constants in system header files.  This can be useful
when preparing code to use with the C<FLOAT_CONST_DECIMAL64> pragma
from the decimal floating-point extension to C99.

=back



=head2 Options for Debugging Your Program or GCC


GCC has various special options that are used for debugging
either your program or GCC:


=over 4


=item B<-g>

Produce debugging information in the operating system's native format
(stabs, COFF, XCOFF, or DWARF 2).  GDB can work with this debugging
information.

On most systems that use stabs format, B<-g> enables use of extra
debugging information that only GDB can use; this extra information
makes debugging work better in GDB but will probably make other debuggers
crash or
refuse to read the program.  If you want to control for certain whether
to generate the extra information, use B<-gstabs+>, B<-gstabs>,
B<-gxcoff+>, B<-gxcoff>, or B<-gvms> (see below).

GCC allows you to use B<-g> with
B<-O>.  The shortcuts taken by optimized code may occasionally
produce surprising results: some variables you declared may not exist
at all; flow of control may briefly move where you did not expect it;
some statements may not be executed because they compute constant
results or their values were already at hand; some statements may
execute in different places because they were moved out of loops.

Nevertheless it proves possible to debug optimized output.  This makes
it reasonable to use the optimizer for programs that might have bugs.

The following options are useful when GCC is generated with the
capability for more than one debugging format.


=item B<-ggdb>

Produce debugging information for use by GDB.  This means to use the
most expressive format available (DWARF 2, stabs, or the native format
if neither of those are supported), including GDB extensions if at all
possible.


=item B<-gstabs>

Produce debugging information in stabs format (if that is supported),
without GDB extensions.  This is the format used by DBX on most BSD
systems.  On MIPS, Alpha and System V Release 4 systems this option
produces stabs debugging output which is not understood by DBX or SDB.
On System V Release 4 systems this option requires the GNU assembler.


=item B<-feliminate-unused-debug-symbols>

Produce debugging information in stabs format (if that is supported),
for only symbols that are actually used.


=item B<-femit-class-debug-always>

Instead of emitting debugging information for a C++ class in only one
object file, emit it in all object files using the class.  This option
should be used only with debuggers that are unable to handle the way GCC
normally emits debugging information for classes because using this
option will increase the size of debugging information by as much as a
factor of two.


=item B<-gstabs+>

Produce debugging information in stabs format (if that is supported),
using GNU extensions understood only by the GNU debugger (GDB).  The
use of these extensions is likely to make other debuggers crash or
refuse to read the program.


=item B<-gcoff>

Produce debugging information in COFF format (if that is supported).
This is the format used by SDB on most System V systems prior to
System V Release 4.


=item B<-gxcoff>

Produce debugging information in XCOFF format (if that is supported).
This is the format used by the DBX debugger on IBM RS/6000 systems.


=item B<-gxcoff+>

Produce debugging information in XCOFF format (if that is supported),
using GNU extensions understood only by the GNU debugger (GDB).  The
use of these extensions is likely to make other debuggers crash or
refuse to read the program, and may cause assemblers other than the GNU
assembler (GAS) to fail with an error.


=item B<-gdwarf->I<version>

Produce debugging information in DWARF format (if that is
supported).  This is the format used by DBX on IRIX 6.  The value
of I<version> may be either 2, 3 or 4; the default version is 2.

Note that with DWARF version 2 some ports require, and will always
use, some non-conflicting DWARF 3 extensions in the unwind tables.

Version 4 may require GDB 7.0 and B<-fvar-tracking-assignments>
for maximum benefit.


=item B<-gstrict-dwarf>

Disallow using extensions of later DWARF standard version than selected
with B<-gdwarf->I<version>.  On most targets using non-conflicting
DWARF extensions from later standard versions is allowed.


=item B<-gno-strict-dwarf>

Allow using extensions of later DWARF standard version than selected with
B<-gdwarf->I<version>.


=item B<-gvms>

Produce debugging information in VMS debug format (if that is
supported).  This is the format used by DEBUG on VMS systems.


=item B<-g>I<level>


=item B<-ggdb>I<level>


=item B<-gstabs>I<level>


=item B<-gcoff>I<level>


=item B<-gxcoff>I<level>


=item B<-gvms>I<level>

Request debugging information and also use I<level> to specify how
much information.  The default level is 2.

Level 0 produces no debug information at all.  Thus, B<-g0> negates
B<-g>.

Level 1 produces minimal information, enough for making backtraces in
parts of the program that you don't plan to debug.  This includes
descriptions of functions and external variables, but no information
about local variables and no line numbers.

Level 3 includes extra information, such as all the macro definitions
present in the program.  Some debuggers support macro expansion when
you use B<-g3>.

B<-gdwarf-2> does not accept a concatenated debug level, because
GCC used to support an option B<-gdwarf> that meant to generate
debug information in version 1 of the DWARF format (which is very
different from version 2), and it would have been too confusing.  That
debug format is long obsolete, but the option cannot be changed now.
Instead use an additional B<-g>I<level> option to change the
debug level for DWARF.


=item B<-gtoggle>

Turn off generation of debug info, if leaving out this option would have
generated it, or turn it on at level 2 otherwise.  The position of this
argument in the command line does not matter, it takes effect after all
other options are processed, and it does so only once, no matter how
many times it is given.  This is mainly intended to be used with
B<-fcompare-debug>.


=item B<-fdump-final-insns>[B<=>I<file>]

Dump the final internal representation (RTL) to I<file>.  If the
optional argument is omitted (or if I<file> is C<.>), the name
of the dump file will be determined by appending C<.gkd> to the
compilation output file name.


=item B<-fcompare-debug>[B<=>I<opts>]

If no error occurs during compilation, run the compiler a second time,
adding I<opts> and B<-fcompare-debug-second> to the arguments
passed to the second compilation.  Dump the final internal
representation in both compilations, and print an error if they differ.

If the equal sign is omitted, the default B<-gtoggle> is used.

The environment variable B<GCC_COMPARE_DEBUG>, if defined, non-empty
and nonzero, implicitly enables B<-fcompare-debug>.  If
B<GCC_COMPARE_DEBUG> is defined to a string starting with a dash,
then it is used for I<opts>, otherwise the default B<-gtoggle>
is used.

B<-fcompare-debug=>, with the equal sign but without I<opts>,
is equivalent to B<-fno-compare-debug>, which disables the dumping
of the final representation and the second compilation, preventing even
B<GCC_COMPARE_DEBUG> from taking effect.

To verify full coverage during B<-fcompare-debug> testing, set
B<GCC_COMPARE_DEBUG> to say B<-fcompare-debug-not-overridden>,
which GCC will reject as an invalid option in any actual compilation
(rather than preprocessing, assembly or linking).  To get just a
warning, setting B<GCC_COMPARE_DEBUG> to B<-w%n-fcompare-debug
not overridden> will do.


=item B<-fcompare-debug-second>

This option is implicitly passed to the compiler for the second
compilation requested by B<-fcompare-debug>, along with options to
silence warnings, and omitting other options that would cause
side-effect compiler outputs to files or to the standard output.  Dump
files and preserved temporary files are renamed so as to contain the
C<.gk> additional extension during the second compilation, to avoid
overwriting those generated by the first.

When this option is passed to the compiler driver, it causes the
I<first> compilation to be skipped, which makes it useful for little
other than debugging the compiler proper.


=item B<-feliminate-dwarf2-dups>

Compress DWARF2 debugging information by eliminating duplicated
information about each symbol.  This option only makes sense when
generating DWARF2 debugging information with B<-gdwarf-2>.


=item B<-femit-struct-debug-baseonly>

Emit debug information for struct-like types
only when the base name of the compilation source file
matches the base name of file in which the struct was defined.

This option substantially reduces the size of debugging information,
but at significant potential loss in type information to the debugger.
See B<-femit-struct-debug-reduced> for a less aggressive option.
See B<-femit-struct-debug-detailed> for more detailed control.

This option works only with DWARF 2.


=item B<-femit-struct-debug-reduced>

Emit debug information for struct-like types
only when the base name of the compilation source file
matches the base name of file in which the type was defined,
unless the struct is a template or defined in a system header.

This option significantly reduces the size of debugging information,
with some potential loss in type information to the debugger.
See B<-femit-struct-debug-baseonly> for a more aggressive option.
See B<-femit-struct-debug-detailed> for more detailed control.

This option works only with DWARF 2.


=item B<-femit-struct-debug-detailed>[B<=>I<spec-list>]

Specify the struct-like types
for which the compiler will generate debug information.
The intent is to reduce duplicate struct debug information
between different object files within the same program.

This option is a detailed version of
B<-femit-struct-debug-reduced> and B<-femit-struct-debug-baseonly>,
which will serve for most needs.

A specification has the syntax[B<dir:>|B<ind:>][B<ord:>|B<gen:>](B<any>|B<sys>|B<base>|B<none>)

The optional first word limits the specification to
structs that are used directly (B<dir:>) or used indirectly (B<ind:>).
A struct type is used directly when it is the type of a variable, member.
Indirect uses arise through pointers to structs.
That is, when use of an incomplete struct would be legal, the use is indirect.
An example is
B<struct one direct; struct two * indirect;>.

The optional second word limits the specification to
ordinary structs (B<ord:>) or generic structs (B<gen:>).
Generic structs are a bit complicated to explain.
For C++, these are non-explicit specializations of template classes,
or non-template classes within the above.
Other programming languages have generics,
but B<-femit-struct-debug-detailed> does not yet implement them.

The third word specifies the source files for those
structs for which the compiler will emit debug information.
The values B<none> and B<any> have the normal meaning.
The value B<base> means that
the base of name of the file in which the type declaration appears
must match the base of the name of the main compilation file.
In practice, this means that
types declared in F<foo.c> and F<foo.h> will have debug information,
but types declared in other header will not.
The value B<sys> means those types satisfying B<base>
or declared in system or compiler headers.

You may need to experiment to determine the best settings for your application.

The default is B<-femit-struct-debug-detailed=all>.

This option works only with DWARF 2.


=item B<-fenable-icf-debug>

Generate additional debug information to support identical code folding (ICF).
This option only works with DWARF version 2 or higher.


=item B<-fno-merge-debug-strings>

Direct the linker to not merge together strings in the debugging
information which are identical in different object files.  Merging is
not supported by all assemblers or linkers.  Merging decreases the size
of the debug information in the output file at the cost of increasing
link processing time.  Merging is enabled by default.


=item B<-fdebug-prefix-map=>I<old>B<=>I<new>

When compiling files in directory F<I<old>>, record debugging
information describing them as in F<I<new>> instead.


=item B<-fno-dwarf2-cfi-asm>

Emit DWARF 2 unwind info as compiler generated C<.eh_frame> section
instead of using GAS C<.cfi_*> directives.


=item B<-p>

Generate extra code to write profile information suitable for the
analysis program B<prof>.  You must use this option when compiling
the source files you want data about, and you must also use it when
linking.


=item B<-pg>

Generate extra code to write profile information suitable for the
analysis program B<gprof>.  You must use this option when compiling
the source files you want data about, and you must also use it when
linking.


=item B<-Q>

Makes the compiler print out each function name as it is compiled, and
print some statistics about each pass when it finishes.


=item B<-ftime-report>

Makes the compiler print some statistics about the time consumed by each
pass when it finishes.


=item B<-fmem-report>

Makes the compiler print some statistics about permanent memory
allocation when it finishes.


=item B<-fpre-ipa-mem-report>


=item B<-fpost-ipa-mem-report>

Makes the compiler print some statistics about permanent memory
allocation before or after interprocedural optimization.


=item B<-fstack-usage>

Makes the compiler output stack usage information for the program, on a
per-function basis.  The filename for the dump is made by appending
F<.su> to the I<auxname>.  I<auxname> is generated from the name of
the output file, if explicitly specified and it is not an executable,
otherwise it is the basename of the source file.  An entry is made up
of three fields:


=over 4


=item *

The name of the function.

=item *

A number of bytes.

=item *

One or more qualifiers: C<static>, C<dynamic>, C<bounded>.

=back


The qualifier C<static> means that the function manipulates the stack
statically: a fixed number of bytes are allocated for the frame on function
entry and released on function exit; no stack adjustments are otherwise made
in the function.  The second field is this fixed number of bytes.

The qualifier C<dynamic> means that the function manipulates the stack
dynamically: in addition to the static allocation described above, stack
adjustments are made in the body of the function, for example to push/pop
arguments around function calls.  If the qualifier C<bounded> is also
present, the amount of these adjustments is bounded at compile-time and
the second field is an upper bound of the total amount of stack used by
the function.  If it is not present, the amount of these adjustments is
not bounded at compile-time and the second field only represents the
bounded part.


=item B<-fprofile-arcs>

Add code so that program flow I<arcs> are instrumented.  During
execution the program records how many times each branch and call is
executed and how many times it is taken or returns.  When the compiled
program exits it saves this data to a file called
F<I<auxname>.gcda> for each source file.  The data may be used for
profile-directed optimizations (B<-fbranch-probabilities>), or for
test coverage analysis (B<-ftest-coverage>).  Each object file's
I<auxname> is generated from the name of the output file, if
explicitly specified and it is not the final executable, otherwise it is
the basename of the source file.  In both cases any suffix is removed
(e.g. F<foo.gcda> for input file F<dir/foo.c>, or
F<dir/foo.gcda> for output file specified as B<-o dir/foo.o>).



=item B<--coverage>


This option is used to compile and link code instrumented for coverage
analysis.  The option is a synonym for B<-fprofile-arcs>
B<-ftest-coverage> (when compiling) and B<-lgcov> (when
linking).  See the documentation for those options for more details.


=over 4



=item *

Compile the source files with B<-fprofile-arcs> plus optimization
and code generation options.  For test coverage analysis, use the
additional B<-ftest-coverage> option.  You do not need to profile
every source file in a program.


=item *

Link your object files with B<-lgcov> or B<-fprofile-arcs>
(the latter implies the former).


=item *

Run the program on a representative workload to generate the arc profile
information.  This may be repeated any number of times.  You can run
concurrent instances of your program, and provided that the file system
supports locking, the data files will be correctly updated.  Also
C<fork> calls are detected and correctly handled (double counting
will not happen).


=item *

For profile-directed optimizations, compile the source files again with
the same optimization and code generation options plus
B<-fbranch-probabilities>.


=item *

For test coverage analysis, use B<gcov> to produce human readable
information from the F<.gcno> and F<.gcda> files.  Refer to the
B<gcov> documentation for further information.


=back


With B<-fprofile-arcs>, for each function of your program GCC
creates a program flow graph, then finds a spanning tree for the graph.
Only arcs that are not on the spanning tree have to be instrumented: the
compiler adds code to count the number of times that these arcs are
executed.  When an arc is the only exit or only entrance to a block, the
instrumentation code can be added to the block; otherwise, a new basic
block must be created to hold the instrumentation code.


=item B<-ftest-coverage>

Produce a notes file that the B<gcov> code-coverage utility can use to
show program coverage.  Each source file's note file is called
F<I<auxname>.gcno>.  Refer to the B<-fprofile-arcs> option
above for a description of I<auxname> and instructions on how to
generate test coverage data.  Coverage data will match the source files
more closely, if you do not optimize.


=item B<-fdbg-cnt-list>

Print the name and the counter upper bound for all debug counters.


=item B<-fdbg-cnt=>I<counter-value-list>

Set the internal debug counter upper bound.  I<counter-value-list>
is a comma-separated list of I<name>:I<value> pairs
which sets the upper bound of each debug counter I<name> to I<value>.
All debug counters have the initial upper bound of I<UINT_MAX>,
thus dbg_cnt() returns true always unless the upper bound is set by this option.
e.g. With -fdbg-cnt=dce:10,tail_call:0
dbg_cnt(dce) will return true only for first 10 invocations
and dbg_cnt(tail_call) will return false always.


=item B<-d>I<letters>


=item B<-fdump-rtl->I<pass>

Says to make debugging dumps during compilation at times specified by
I<letters>.  This is used for debugging the RTL-based passes of the
compiler.  The file names for most of the dumps are made by appending
a pass number and a word to the I<dumpname>, and the files are
created in the directory of the output file.  Note that the pass
number is computed statically as passes get registered into the pass
manager.  Thus the numbering is not related to the dynamic order of
execution of passes.  In particular, a pass installed by a plugin
could have a number over 200 even if it executed quite early.
I<dumpname> is generated from the name of the output file, if
explicitly specified and it is not an executable, otherwise it is the
basename of the source file. These switches may have different effects
when B<-E> is used for preprocessing.

Debug dumps can be enabled with a B<-fdump-rtl> switch or some
B<-d> option I<letters>.  Here are the possible
letters for use in I<pass> and I<letters>, and their meanings:


=over 4



=item B<-fdump-rtl-alignments>

Dump after branch alignments have been computed.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-asmcons>

Dump after fixing rtl statements that have unsatisfied in/out constraints.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-auto_inc_dec>

Dump after auto-inc-dec discovery.  This pass is only run on
architectures that have auto inc or auto dec instructions.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-barriers>

Dump after cleaning up the barrier instructions.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-bbpart>

Dump after partitioning hot and cold basic blocks.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-bbro>

Dump after block reordering.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-btl1>


=item B<-fdump-rtl-btl2>

B<-fdump-rtl-btl1> and B<-fdump-rtl-btl2> enable dumping
after the two branch
target load optimization passes.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-bypass>

Dump after jump bypassing and control flow optimizations.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-combine>

Dump after the RTL instruction combination pass.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-compgotos>

Dump after duplicating the computed gotos.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-ce1>


=item B<-fdump-rtl-ce2>


=item B<-fdump-rtl-ce3>

B<-fdump-rtl-ce1>, B<-fdump-rtl-ce2>, and
B<-fdump-rtl-ce3> enable dumping after the three
if conversion passes.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-cprop_hardreg>

Dump after hard register copy propagation.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-csa>

Dump after combining stack adjustments.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-cse1>


=item B<-fdump-rtl-cse2>

B<-fdump-rtl-cse1> and B<-fdump-rtl-cse2> enable dumping after
the two common sub-expression elimination passes.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-dce>

Dump after the standalone dead code elimination passes.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-dbr>

Dump after delayed branch scheduling.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-dce1>


=item B<-fdump-rtl-dce2>

B<-fdump-rtl-dce1> and B<-fdump-rtl-dce2> enable dumping after
the two dead store elimination passes.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-eh>

Dump after finalization of EH handling code.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-eh_ranges>

Dump after conversion of EH handling range regions.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-expand>

Dump after RTL generation.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-fwprop1>


=item B<-fdump-rtl-fwprop2>

B<-fdump-rtl-fwprop1> and B<-fdump-rtl-fwprop2> enable
dumping after the two forward propagation passes.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-gcse1>


=item B<-fdump-rtl-gcse2>

B<-fdump-rtl-gcse1> and B<-fdump-rtl-gcse2> enable dumping
after global common subexpression elimination.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-init-regs>

Dump after the initialization of the registers.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-initvals>

Dump after the computation of the initial value sets.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-into_cfglayout>

Dump after converting to cfglayout mode.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-ira>

Dump after iterated register allocation.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-jump>

Dump after the second jump optimization.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-loop2>

B<-fdump-rtl-loop2> enables dumping after the rtl
loop optimization passes.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-mach>

Dump after performing the machine dependent reorganization pass, if that
pass exists.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-mode_sw>

Dump after removing redundant mode switches.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-rnreg>

Dump after register renumbering.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-outof_cfglayout>

Dump after converting from cfglayout mode.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-peephole2>

Dump after the peephole pass.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-postreload>

Dump after post-reload optimizations.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-pro_and_epilogue>

Dump after generating the function pro and epilogues.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-regmove>

Dump after the register move pass.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-sched1>


=item B<-fdump-rtl-sched2>

B<-fdump-rtl-sched1> and B<-fdump-rtl-sched2> enable dumping
after the basic block scheduling passes.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-see>

Dump after sign extension elimination.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-seqabstr>

Dump after common sequence discovery.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-shorten>

Dump after shortening branches.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-sibling>

Dump after sibling call optimizations.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-split1>


=item B<-fdump-rtl-split2>


=item B<-fdump-rtl-split3>


=item B<-fdump-rtl-split4>


=item B<-fdump-rtl-split5>

B<-fdump-rtl-split1>, B<-fdump-rtl-split2>,
B<-fdump-rtl-split3>, B<-fdump-rtl-split4> and
B<-fdump-rtl-split5> enable dumping after five rounds of
instruction splitting.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-sms>

Dump after modulo scheduling.  This pass is only run on some
architectures.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-stack>

Dump after conversion from GCC's "flat register file" registers to the
x87's stack-like registers.  This pass is only run on x86 variants.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-subreg1>


=item B<-fdump-rtl-subreg2>

B<-fdump-rtl-subreg1> and B<-fdump-rtl-subreg2> enable dumping after
the two subreg expansion passes.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-unshare>

Dump after all rtl has been unshared.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-vartrack>

Dump after variable tracking.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-vregs>

Dump after converting virtual registers to hard registers.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-web>

Dump after live range splitting.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-regclass>


=item B<-fdump-rtl-subregs_of_mode_init>


=item B<-fdump-rtl-subregs_of_mode_finish>


=item B<-fdump-rtl-dfinit>


=item B<-fdump-rtl-dfinish>

These dumps are defined but always produce empty files.


=item B<-fdump-rtl-all>

Produce all the dumps listed above.


=item B<-dA>

Annotate the assembler output with miscellaneous debugging information.


=item B<-dD>

Dump all macro definitions, at the end of preprocessing, in addition to
normal output.


=item B<-dH>

Produce a core dump whenever an error occurs.


=item B<-dm>

Print statistics on memory usage, at the end of the run, to
standard error.


=item B<-dp>

Annotate the assembler output with a comment indicating which
pattern and alternative was used.  The length of each instruction is
also printed.


=item B<-dP>

Dump the RTL in the assembler output as a comment before each instruction.
Also turns on B<-dp> annotation.


=item B<-dv>

For each of the other indicated dump files (B<-fdump-rtl->I<pass>),
dump a representation of the control flow graph suitable for viewing with VCG
to F<I<file>.I<pass>.vcg>.


=item B<-dx>

Just generate RTL for a function instead of compiling it.  Usually used
with B<-fdump-rtl-expand>.

=back



=item B<-fdump-noaddr>

When doing debugging dumps, suppress address output.  This makes it more
feasible to use diff on debugging dumps for compiler invocations with
different compiler binaries and/or different
text / bss / data / heap / stack / dso start locations.


=item B<-fdump-unnumbered>

When doing debugging dumps, suppress instruction numbers and address output.
This makes it more feasible to use diff on debugging dumps for compiler
invocations with different options, in particular with and without
B<-g>.


=item B<-fdump-unnumbered-links>

When doing debugging dumps (see B<-d> option above), suppress
instruction numbers for the links to the previous and next instructions
in a sequence.


=item B<-fdump-translation-unit> (C++ only)


=item B<-fdump-translation-unit->I<options>B< >(C++ only)

Dump a representation of the tree structure for the entire translation
unit to a file.  The file name is made by appending F<.tu> to the
source file name, and the file is created in the same directory as the
output file.  If the B<->I<options> form is used, I<options>
controls the details of the dump as described for the
B<-fdump-tree> options.


=item B<-fdump-class-hierarchy> (C++ only)


=item B<-fdump-class-hierarchy->I<options>B< >(C++ only)

Dump a representation of each class's hierarchy and virtual function
table layout to a file.  The file name is made by appending
F<.class> to the source file name, and the file is created in the
same directory as the output file.  If the B<->I<options> form
is used, I<options> controls the details of the dump as described
for the B<-fdump-tree> options.


=item B<-fdump-ipa->I<switch>

Control the dumping at various stages of inter-procedural analysis
language tree to a file.  The file name is generated by appending a
switch specific suffix to the source file name, and the file is created
in the same directory as the output file.  The following dumps are
possible:


=over 4


=item B<all>

Enables all inter-procedural analysis dumps.


=item B<cgraph>

Dumps information about call-graph optimization, unused function removal,
and inlining decisions.


=item B<inline>

Dump after function inlining.


=back



=item B<-fdump-statistics->I<option>

Enable and control dumping of pass statistics in a separate file.  The
file name is generated by appending a suffix ending in
B<.statistics> to the source file name, and the file is created in
the same directory as the output file.  If the B<->I<option>
form is used, B<-stats> will cause counters to be summed over the
whole compilation unit while B<-details> will dump every event as
the passes generate them.  The default with no option is to sum
counters for each function compiled.


=item B<-fdump-tree->I<switch>


=item B<-fdump-tree->I<switch>B<->I<options>

Control the dumping at various stages of processing the intermediate
language tree to a file.  The file name is generated by appending a
switch specific suffix to the source file name, and the file is
created in the same directory as the output file.  If the
B<->I<options> form is used, I<options> is a list of
B<-> separated options that control the details of the dump.  Not
all options are applicable to all dumps, those which are not
meaningful will be ignored.  The following options are available


=over 4


=item B<address>

Print the address of each node.  Usually this is not meaningful as it
changes according to the environment and source file.  Its primary use
is for tying up a dump file with a debug environment.

=item B<asmname>

If C<DECL_ASSEMBLER_NAME> has been set for a given decl, use that
in the dump instead of C<DECL_NAME>.  Its primary use is ease of
use working backward from mangled names in the assembly file.

=item B<slim>

Inhibit dumping of members of a scope or body of a function merely
because that scope has been reached.  Only dump such items when they
are directly reachable by some other path.  When dumping pretty-printed
trees, this option inhibits dumping the bodies of control structures.

=item B<raw>

Print a raw representation of the tree.  By default, trees are
pretty-printed into a C-like representation.

=item B<details>

Enable more detailed dumps (not honored by every dump option).

=item B<stats>

Enable dumping various statistics about the pass (not honored by every dump
option).

=item B<blocks>

Enable showing basic block boundaries (disabled in raw dumps).

=item B<vops>

Enable showing virtual operands for every statement.

=item B<lineno>

Enable showing line numbers for statements.

=item B<uid>

Enable showing the unique ID (C<DECL_UID>) for each variable.

=item B<verbose>

Enable showing the tree dump for each statement.

=item B<eh>

Enable showing the EH region number holding each statement.

=item B<all>

Turn on all options, except B<raw>, B<slim>, B<verbose>
and B<lineno>.

=back


The following tree dumps are possible:

=over 4



=item B<original>

Dump before any tree based optimization, to F<I<file>.original>.


=item B<optimized>

Dump after all tree based optimization, to F<I<file>.optimized>.


=item B<gimple>

Dump each function before and after the gimplification pass to a file.  The
file name is made by appending F<.gimple> to the source file name.


=item B<cfg>

Dump the control flow graph of each function to a file.  The file name is
made by appending F<.cfg> to the source file name.


=item B<vcg>

Dump the control flow graph of each function to a file in VCG format.  The
file name is made by appending F<.vcg> to the source file name.  Note
that if the file contains more than one function, the generated file cannot
be used directly by VCG.  You will need to cut and paste each function's
graph into its own separate file first.


=item B<ch>

Dump each function after copying loop headers.  The file name is made by
appending F<.ch> to the source file name.


=item B<ssa>

Dump SSA related information to a file.  The file name is made by appending
F<.ssa> to the source file name.


=item B<alias>

Dump aliasing information for each function.  The file name is made by
appending F<.alias> to the source file name.


=item B<ccp>

Dump each function after CCP.  The file name is made by appending
F<.ccp> to the source file name.


=item B<storeccp>

Dump each function after STORE-CCP.  The file name is made by appending
F<.storeccp> to the source file name.


=item B<pre>

Dump trees after partial redundancy elimination.  The file name is made
by appending F<.pre> to the source file name.


=item B<fre>

Dump trees after full redundancy elimination.  The file name is made
by appending F<.fre> to the source file name.


=item B<copyprop>

Dump trees after copy propagation.  The file name is made
by appending F<.copyprop> to the source file name.


=item B<store_copyprop>

Dump trees after store copy-propagation.  The file name is made
by appending F<.store_copyprop> to the source file name.


=item B<dce>

Dump each function after dead code elimination.  The file name is made by
appending F<.dce> to the source file name.


=item B<mudflap>

Dump each function after adding mudflap instrumentation.  The file name is
made by appending F<.mudflap> to the source file name.


=item B<sra>

Dump each function after performing scalar replacement of aggregates.  The
file name is made by appending F<.sra> to the source file name.


=item B<sink>

Dump each function after performing code sinking.  The file name is made
by appending F<.sink> to the source file name.


=item B<dom>

Dump each function after applying dominator tree optimizations.  The file
name is made by appending F<.dom> to the source file name.


=item B<dse>

Dump each function after applying dead store elimination.  The file
name is made by appending F<.dse> to the source file name.


=item B<phiopt>

Dump each function after optimizing PHI nodes into straightline code.  The file
name is made by appending F<.phiopt> to the source file name.


=item B<forwprop>

Dump each function after forward propagating single use variables.  The file
name is made by appending F<.forwprop> to the source file name.


=item B<copyrename>

Dump each function after applying the copy rename optimization.  The file
name is made by appending F<.copyrename> to the source file name.


=item B<nrv>

Dump each function after applying the named return value optimization on
generic trees.  The file name is made by appending F<.nrv> to the source
file name.


=item B<vect>

Dump each function after applying vectorization of loops.  The file name is
made by appending F<.vect> to the source file name.


=item B<slp>

Dump each function after applying vectorization of basic blocks.  The file name
is made by appending F<.slp> to the source file name.


=item B<vrp>

Dump each function after Value Range Propagation (VRP).  The file name
is made by appending F<.vrp> to the source file name.


=item B<all>

Enable all the available tree dumps with the flags provided in this option.

=back



=item B<-ftree-vectorizer-verbose=>I<n>
