libc.  These entry points should be supplied through some other
mechanism when this option is specified.

One of the standard libraries bypassed by B<-nostdlib> and
B<-nodefaultlibs> is F<libgcc.a>, a library of internal subroutines
that GCC uses to overcome shortcomings of particular machines, or special
needs for some languages.

In most cases, you need F<libgcc.a> even when you want to avoid
other standard libraries.  In other words, when you specify B<-nostdlib>
or B<-nodefaultlibs> you should usually specify B<-lgcc> as well.
This ensures that you have no unresolved references to internal GCC
library subroutines.  (For example, B<__main>, used to ensure C++
constructors will be called.)


=item B<-pie>

Produce a position independent executable on targets which support it.
For predictable results, you must also specify the same set of options
that were used to generate code (B<-fpie>, B<-fPIE>,
or model suboptions) when you specify this option.


=item B<-rdynamic>

Pass the flag B<-export-dynamic> to the ELF linker, on targets
that support it. This instructs the linker to add all symbols, not
only used ones, to the dynamic symbol table. This option is needed
for some uses of C<dlopen> or to allow obtaining backtraces
from within a program.


=item B<-s>

Remove all symbol table and relocation information from the executable.


=item B<-static>

On systems that support dynamic linking, this prevents linking with the shared
libraries.  On other systems, this option has no effect.


=item B<-shared>

Produce a shared object which can then be linked with other objects to
form an executable.  Not all systems support this option.  For predictable
results, you must also specify the same set of options that were used to
generate code (B<-fpic>, B<-fPIC>, or model suboptions)
when you specify this option.[1]


=item B<-shared-libgcc>


=item B<-static-libgcc>

On systems that provide F<libgcc> as a shared library, these options
force the use of either the shared or static version respectively.
If no shared version of F<libgcc> was built when the compiler was
configured, these options have no effect.

There are several situations in which an application should use the
shared F<libgcc> instead of the static version.  The most common
of these is when the application wishes to throw and catch exceptions
across different shared libraries.  In that case, each of the libraries
as well as the application itself should use the shared F<libgcc>.

Therefore, the G++ and GCJ drivers automatically add
B<-shared-libgcc> whenever you build a shared library or a main
executable, because C++ and Java programs typically use exceptions, so
this is the right thing to do.

If, instead, you use the GCC driver to create shared libraries, you may
find that they will not always be linked with the shared F<libgcc>.
If GCC finds, at its configuration time, that you have a non-GNU linker
or a GNU linker that does not support option B<--eh-frame-hdr>,
it will link the shared version of F<libgcc> into shared libraries
by default.  Otherwise, it will take advantage of the linker and optimize
away the linking with the shared version of F<libgcc>, linking with
the static version of libgcc by default.  This allows exceptions to
propagate through such shared libraries, without incurring relocation
costs at library load time.

However, if a library or main executable is supposed to throw or catch
exceptions, you must link it using the G++ or GCJ driver, as appropriate
for the languages used in the program, or using the option
B<-shared-libgcc>, such that it is linked with the shared
F<libgcc>.


=item B<-static-libstdc++>

When the B<g++> program is used to link a C++ program, it will
normally automatically link against B<libstdc++>.  If
F<libstdc++> is available as a shared library, and the
B<-static> option is not used, then this will link against the
shared version of F<libstdc++>.  That is normally fine.  However, it
is sometimes useful to freeze the version of F<libstdc++> used by
the program without going all the way to a fully static link.  The
B<-static-libstdc++> option directs the B<g++> driver to
link F<libstdc++> statically, without necessarily linking other
libraries statically.


=item B<-symbolic>

Bind references to global symbols when building a shared object.  Warn
about any unresolved references (unless overridden by the link editor
option B<-Xlinker -z -Xlinker defs>).  Only a few systems support
this option.


=item B<-T> I<script>

Use I<script> as the linker script.  This option is supported by most
systems using the GNU linker.  On some targets, such as bare-board
targets without an operating system, the B<-T> option may be required
when linking to avoid references to undefined symbols.


=item B<-Xlinker> I<option>

Pass I<option> as an option to the linker.  You can use this to
supply system-specific linker options which GCC does not know how to
recognize.

If you want to pass an option that takes a separate argument, you must use
B<-Xlinker> twice, once for the option and once for the argument.
For example, to pass B<-assert definitions>, you must write
B<-Xlinker -assert -Xlinker definitions>.  It does not work to write
B<-Xlinker "-assert definitions">, because this passes the entire
string as a single argument, which is not what the linker expects.

When using the GNU linker, it is usually more convenient to pass
arguments to linker options using the I<option>B<=>I<value>
syntax than as separate arguments.  For example, you can specify
B<-Xlinker -Map=output.map> rather than
B<-Xlinker -Map -Xlinker output.map>.  Other linkers may not support
this syntax for command-line options.


=item B<-Wl,>I<option>

Pass I<option> as an option to the linker.  If I<option> contains
commas, it is split into multiple options at the commas.  You can use this
syntax to pass an argument to the option.
For example, B<-Wl,-Map,output.map> passes B<-Map output.map> to the
linker.  When using the GNU linker, you can also get the same effect with
B<-Wl,-Map=output.map>.


=item B<-u> I<symbol>

Pretend the symbol I<symbol> is undefined, to force linking of
library modules to define it.  You can use B<-u> multiple times with
different symbols to force loading of additional library modules.

=back



=head2 Options for Directory Search


These options specify directories to search for header files, for
libraries and for parts of the compiler:


=over 4


=item B<-I>I<dir>

Add the directory I<dir> to the head of the list of directories to be
searched for header files.  This can be used to override a system header
file, substituting your own version, since these directories are
searched before the system header file directories.  However, you should
not use this option to add directories that contain vendor-supplied
system header files (use B<-isystem> for that).  If you use more than
one B<-I> option, the directories are scanned in left-to-right
order; the standard system directories come after.

If a standard system include directory, or a directory specified with
B<-isystem>, is also specified with B<-I>, the B<-I>
option will be ignored.  The directory will still be searched but as a
system directory at its normal position in the system include chain.
This is to ensure that GCC's procedure to fix buggy system headers and
the ordering for the include_next directive are not inadvertently changed.
If you really need to change the search order for system directories,
use the B<-nostdinc> and/or B<-isystem> options.


=item B<-iplugindir=>I<dir>

Set the directory to search for plugins which are passed
by B<-fplugin=>I<name> instead of
B<-fplugin=>I<path>B</>I<name>B<.so>.  This option is not meant
to be used by the user, but only passed by the driver.


=item B<-iquote>I<dir>

Add the directory I<dir> to the head of the list of directories to
be searched for header files only for the case of B<#include
">I<file>B<">; they are not searched for B<#include E<lt>>I<file>B<E<gt>>,
otherwise just like B<-I>.


=item B<-L>I<dir>

Add directory I<dir> to the list of directories to be searched
for B<-l>.


=item B<-B>I<prefix>

This option specifies where to find the executables, libraries,
include files, and data files of the compiler itself.

The compiler driver program runs one or more of the subprograms
F<cpp>, F<cc1>, F<as> and F<ld>.  It tries
I<prefix> as a prefix for each program it tries to run, both with and
without I<machine>B</>I<version>B</>.

For each subprogram to be run, the compiler driver first tries the
B<-B> prefix, if any.  If that name is not found, or if B<-B>
was not specified, the driver tries two standard prefixes, which are
F</usr/lib/gcc/> and F</usr/local/lib/gcc/>.  If neither of
those results in a file name that is found, the unmodified program
name is searched for using the directories specified in your
B<PATH> environment variable.

The compiler will check to see if the path provided by the B<-B>
refers to a directory, and if necessary it will add a directory
separator character at the end of the path.

B<-B> prefixes that effectively specify directory names also apply
to libraries in the linker, because the compiler translates these
options into B<-L> options for the linker.  They also apply to
includes files in the preprocessor, because the compiler translates these
options into B<-isystem> options for the preprocessor.  In this case,
the compiler appends B<include> to the prefix.

The run-time support file F<libgcc.a> can also be searched for using
the B<-B> prefix, if needed.  If it is not found there, the two
standard prefixes above are tried, and that is all.  The file is left
out of the link if it is not found by those means.

Another way to specify a prefix much like the B<-B> prefix is to use
the environment variable B<GCC_EXEC_PREFIX>.  

As a special kludge, if the path provided by B<-B> is
F<[dir/]stageI<N>/>, where I<N> is a number in the range 0 to
9, then it will be replaced by F<[dir/]include>.  This is to help
with boot-strapping the compiler.


=item B<-specs=>I<file>

Process I<file> after the compiler reads in the standard F<specs>
file, in order to override the defaults that the F<gcc> driver
program uses when determining what switches to pass to F<cc1>,
F<cc1plus>, F<as>, F<ld>, etc.  More than one
B<-specs=>I<file> can be specified on the command line, and they
are processed in order, from left to right.


=item B<--sysroot=>I<dir>

Use I<dir> as the logical root directory for headers and libraries.
For example, if the compiler would normally search for headers in
F</usr/include> and libraries in F</usr/lib>, it will instead
search F<I<dir>/usr/include> and F<I<dir>/usr/lib>.

If you use both this option and the B<-isysroot> option, then
the B<--sysroot> option will apply to libraries, but the
B<-isysroot> option will apply to header files.

The GNU linker (beginning with version 2.16) has the necessary support
for this option.  If your linker does not support this option, the
header file aspect of B<--sysroot> will still work, but the
library aspect will not.


=item B<-I->

This option has been deprecated.  Please use B<-iquote> instead for
B<-I> directories before the B<-I-> and remove the B<-I->.
Any directories you specify with B<-I> options before the B<-I->
option are searched only for the case of B<#include ">I<file>B<">;
they are not searched for B<#include E<lt>>I<file>B<E<gt>>.

If additional directories are specified with B<-I> options after
the B<-I->, these directories are searched for all B<#include>
directives.  (Ordinarily I<all> B<-I> directories are used
this way.)

In addition, the B<-I-> option inhibits the use of the current
directory (where the current input file came from) as the first search
directory for B<#include ">I<file>B<">.  There is no way to
override this effect of B<-I->.  With B<-I.> you can specify
searching the directory which was current when the compiler was
invoked.  That is not exactly the same as what the preprocessor does
by default, but it is often satisfactory.

B<-I-> does not inhibit the use of the standard system directories
for header files.  Thus, B<-I-> and B<-nostdinc> are
independent.

=back




=head2 Specifying Target Machine and Compiler Version


The usual way to run GCC is to run the executable called B<gcc>, or
I<machine>B<-gcc> when cross-compiling, or
I<machine>B<-gcc->I<version> to run a version other than the
one that was installed last.


=head2 Hardware Models and Configurations


Each target machine types can have its own
special options, starting with B<-m>, to choose among various
hardware models or configurations---for example, 68010 vs 68020,
floating coprocessor or none.  A single installed version of the
compiler can compile for any model or configuration, according to the
options specified.

Some configurations of the compiler also support additional special
options, usually for compatibility with other compilers on the same
platform.




=head3 ARC Options


These options are defined for ARC implementations:


=over 4


=item B<-EL>

Compile code for little endian mode.  This is the default.


=item B<-EB>

Compile code for big endian mode.


=item B<-mmangle-cpu>

Prepend the name of the CPU to all public symbol names.
In multiple-processor systems, there are many ARC variants with different
instruction and register set characteristics.  This flag prevents code
compiled for one CPU to be linked with code compiled for another.
No facility exists for handling variants that are "almost identical".
This is an all or nothing option.


=item B<-mcpu=>I<cpu>

Compile code for ARC variant I<cpu>.
Which variants are supported depend on the configuration.
All variants support B<-mcpu=base>, this is the default.


=item B<-mtext=>I<text-section>


=item B<-mdata=>I<data-section>


=item B<-mrodata=>I<readonly-data-section>

Put functions, data, and readonly data in I<text-section>,
I<data-section>, and I<readonly-data-section> respectively
by default.  This can be overridden with the C<section> attribute.



=back



=head3 ARM Options


These B<-m> options are defined for Advanced RISC Machines (ARM)
architectures:


=over 4


=item B<-mabi=>I<name>

Generate code for the specified ABI.  Permissible values are: B<apcs-gnu>,
B<atpcs>, B<aapcs>, B<aapcs-linux> and B<iwmmxt>.


=item B<-mapcs-frame>

Generate a stack frame that is compliant with the ARM Procedure Call
Standard for all functions, even if this is not strictly necessary for
correct execution of the code.  Specifying B<-fomit-frame-pointer>
with this option will cause the stack frames not to be generated for
leaf functions.  The default is B<-mno-apcs-frame>.


=item B<-mapcs>

This is a synonym for B<-mapcs-frame>.



=item B<-mthumb-interwork>

Generate code which supports calling between the ARM and Thumb
instruction sets.  Without this option the two instruction sets cannot
be reliably used inside one program.  The default is
B<-mno-thumb-interwork>, since slightly larger code is generated
when B<-mthumb-interwork> is specified.


=item B<-mno-sched-prolog>

Prevent the reordering of instructions in the function prolog, or the
merging of those instruction with the instructions in the function's
body.  This means that all functions will start with a recognizable set
of instructions (or in fact one of a choice from a small set of
different function prologues), and this information can be used to
locate the start if functions inside an executable piece of code.  The
default is B<-msched-prolog>.


=item B<-mfloat-abi=>I<name>

Specifies which floating-point ABI to use.  Permissible values
are: B<soft>, B<softfp> and B<hard>.

Specifying B<soft> causes GCC to generate output containing
library calls for floating-point operations.
B<softfp> allows the generation of code using hardware floating-point
instructions, but still uses the soft-float calling conventions.
B<hard> allows generation of floating-point instructions
and uses FPU-specific calling conventions.

The default depends on the specific target configuration.  Note that
the hard-float and soft-float ABIs are not link-compatible; you must
compile your entire program with the same ABI, and link with a
compatible set of libraries.


=item B<-mhard-float>

Equivalent to B<-mfloat-abi=hard>.


=item B<-msoft-float>

Equivalent to B<-mfloat-abi=soft>.


=item B<-mlittle-endian>

Generate code for a processor running in little-endian mode.  This is
the default for all standard configurations.


=item B<-mbig-endian>

Generate code for a processor running in big-endian mode; the default is
to compile code for a little-endian processor.


=item B<-mwords-little-endian>

This option only applies when generating code for big-endian processors.
Generate code for a little-endian word order but a big-endian byte
order.  That is, a byte order of the form B<32107654>.  Note: this
option should only be used if you require compatibility with code for
big-endian ARM processors generated by versions of the compiler prior to
2.8.


=item B<-mcpu=>I<name>

This specifies the name of the target ARM processor.  GCC uses this name
to determine what kind of instructions it can emit when generating
assembly code.  Permissible names are: B<arm2>, B<arm250>,
B<arm3>, B<arm6>, B<arm60>, B<arm600>, B<arm610>,
B<arm620>, B<arm7>, B<arm7m>, B<arm7d>, B<arm7dm>,
B<arm7di>, B<arm7dmi>, B<arm70>, B<arm700>,
B<arm700i>, B<arm710>, B<arm710c>, B<arm7100>,
B<arm720>,
B<arm7500>, B<arm7500fe>, B<arm7tdmi>, B<arm7tdmi-s>,
B<arm710t>, B<arm720t>, B<arm740t>,
B<strongarm>, B<strongarm110>, B<strongarm1100>,
B<strongarm1110>,
B<arm8>, B<arm810>, B<arm9>, B<arm9e>, B<arm920>,
B<arm920t>, B<arm922t>, B<arm946e-s>, B<arm966e-s>,
B<arm968e-s>, B<arm926ej-s>, B<arm940t>, B<arm9tdmi>,
B<arm10tdmi>, B<arm1020t>, B<arm1026ej-s>,
B<arm10e>, B<arm1020e>, B<arm1022e>,
B<arm1136j-s>, B<arm1136jf-s>, B<mpcore>, B<mpcorenovfp>,
B<arm1156t2-s>, B<arm1156t2f-s>, B<arm1176jz-s>, B<arm1176jzf-s>,
B<cortex-a5>, B<cortex-a8>, B<cortex-a9>, B<cortex-a15>,
B<cortex-r4>, B<cortex-r4f>, B<cortex-m4>, B<cortex-m3>,
B<cortex-m1>,
B<cortex-m0>,
B<xscale>, B<iwmmxt>, B<iwmmxt2>, B<ep9312>.


=item B<-mtune=>I<name>

This option is very similar to the B<-mcpu=> option, except that
instead of specifying the actual target processor type, and hence
restricting which instructions can be used, it specifies that GCC should
tune the performance of the code as if the target were of the type
specified in this option, but still choosing the instructions that it
will generate based on the CPU specified by a B<-mcpu=> option.
For some ARM implementations better performance can be obtained by using
this option.


=item B<-march=>I<name>

This specifies the name of the target ARM architecture.  GCC uses this
name to determine what kind of instructions it can emit when generating
assembly code.  This option can be used in conjunction with or instead
of the B<-mcpu=> option.  Permissible names are: B<armv2>,
B<armv2a>, B<armv3>, B<armv3m>, B<armv4>, B<armv4t>,
B<armv5>, B<armv5t>, B<armv5e>, B<armv5te>,
B<armv6>, B<armv6j>,
B<armv6t2>, B<armv6z>, B<armv6zk>, B<armv6-m>,
B<armv7>, B<armv7-a>, B<armv7-r>, B<armv7-m>,
B<iwmmxt>, B<iwmmxt2>, B<ep9312>.


=item B<-mfpu=>I<name>


=item B<-mfpe=>I<number>


=item B<-mfp=>I<number>

This specifies what floating point hardware (or hardware emulation) is
available on the target.  Permissible names are: B<fpa>, B<fpe2>,
B<fpe3>, B<maverick>, B<vfp>, B<vfpv3>, B<vfpv3-fp16>,
B<vfpv3-d16>, B<vfpv3-d16-fp16>, B<vfpv3xd>, B<vfpv3xd-fp16>,
B<neon>, B<neon-fp16>, B<vfpv4>, B<vfpv4-d16>,
B<fpv4-sp-d16> and B<neon-vfpv4>.
B<-mfp> and B<-mfpe> are synonyms for
B<-mfpu>=B<fpe>I<number>, for compatibility with older versions
of GCC.

If B<-msoft-float> is specified this specifies the format of
floating point values.

If the selected floating-point hardware includes the NEON extension
(e.g. B<-mfpu>=B<neon>), note that floating-point
operations will not be used by GCC's auto-vectorization pass unless
B<-funsafe-math-optimizations> is also specified.  This is
because NEON hardware does not fully implement the IEEE 754 standard for
floating-point arithmetic (in particular denormal values are treated as
zero), so the use of NEON instructions may lead to a loss of precision.


=item B<-mfp16-format=>I<name>

Specify the format of the C<__fp16> half-precision floating-point type.
Permissible names are B<none>, B<ieee>, and B<alternative>;
the default is B<none>, in which case the C<__fp16> type is not
defined.  


=item B<-mstructure-size-boundary=>I<n>

The size of all structures and unions will be rounded up to a multiple
of the number of bits set by this option.  Permissible values are 8, 32
and 64.  The default value varies for different toolchains.  For the COFF
targeted toolchain the default value is 8.  A value of 64 is only allowed
if the underlying ABI supports it.

Specifying the larger number can produce faster, more efficient code, but
can also increase the size of the program.  Different values are potentially
incompatible.  Code compiled with one value cannot necessarily expect to
work with code or libraries compiled with another value, if they exchange
information using structures or unions.


=item B<-mabort-on-noreturn>

Generate a call to the function C<abort> at the end of a
C<noreturn> function.  It will be executed if the function tries to
return.


=item B<-mlong-calls>


=item B<-mno-long-calls>

Tells the compiler to perform function calls by first loading the
address of the function into a register and then performing a subroutine
call on this register.  This switch is needed if the target function
will lie outside of the 64 megabyte addressing range of the offset based
version of subroutine call instruction.

Even if this switch is enabled, not all function calls will be turned
into long calls.  The heuristic is that static functions, functions
which have the B<short-call> attribute, functions that are inside
the scope of a B<#pragma no_long_calls> directive and functions whose
definitions have already been compiled within the current compilation
unit, will not be turned into long calls.  The exception to this rule is
that weak function definitions, functions with the B<long-call>
attribute or the B<section> attribute, and functions that are within
the scope of a B<#pragma long_calls> directive, will always be
turned into long calls.

This feature is not enabled by default.  Specifying
B<-mno-long-calls> will restore the default behavior, as will
placing the function calls within the scope of a B<#pragma
long_calls_off> directive.  Note these switches have no effect on how
the compiler generates code to handle function calls via function
pointers.


=item B<-msingle-pic-base>

Treat the register used for PIC addressing as read-only, rather than
loading it in the prologue for each function.  The run-time system is
responsible for initializing this register with an appropriate value
before execution begins.


=item B<-mpic-register=>I<reg>

Specify the register to be used for PIC addressing.  The default is R10
unless stack-checking is enabled, when R9 is used.


=item B<-mcirrus-fix-invalid-insns>

Insert NOPs into the instruction stream to in order to work around
problems with invalid Maverick instruction combinations.  This option
is only valid if the B<-mcpu=ep9312> option has been used to
enable generation of instructions for the Cirrus Maverick floating
point co-processor.  This option is not enabled by default, since the
problem is only present in older Maverick implementations.  The default
can be re-enabled by use of the B<-mno-cirrus-fix-invalid-insns>
switch.


=item B<-mpoke-function-name>

Write the name of each function into the text section, directly
preceding the function prologue.  The generated code is similar to this:

	
	     t0
	         .ascii "arm_poke_function_name", 0
	         .align
	     t1
	         .word 0xff000000 + (t1 - t0)
	     arm_poke_function_name
	         mov     ip, sp
	         stmfd   sp!, {fp, ip, lr, pc}
	         sub     fp, ip, #4


When performing a stack backtrace, code can inspect the value of
C<pc> stored at C<fp + 0>.  If the trace function then looks at
location C<pc - 12> and the top 8 bits are set, then we know that
there is a function name embedded immediately preceding this location
and has length C<((pc[-3]) & 0xff000000)>.


=item B<-mthumb>

Generate code for the Thumb instruction set.  The default is to
use the 32-bit ARM instruction set.
This option automatically enables either 16-bit Thumb-1 or
mixed 16/32-bit Thumb-2 instructions based on the B<-mcpu=>I<name>
and B<-march=>I<name> options.  This option is not passed to the
assembler. If you want to force assembler files to be interpreted as Thumb code,
either add a B<.thumb> directive to the source or pass the B<-mthumb>
option directly to the assembler by prefixing it with B<-Wa>.


=item B<-mtpcs-frame>

Generate a stack frame that is compliant with the Thumb Procedure Call
Standard for all non-leaf functions.  (A leaf function is one that does
not call any other functions.)  The default is B<-mno-tpcs-frame>.


=item B<-mtpcs-leaf-frame>

Generate a stack frame that is compliant with the Thumb Procedure Call
Standard for all leaf functions.  (A leaf function is one that does
not call any other functions.)  The default is B<-mno-apcs-leaf-frame>.


=item B<-mcallee-super-interworking>

Gives all externally visible functions in the file being compiled an ARM
instruction set header which switches to Thumb mode before executing the
rest of the function.  This allows these functions to be called from
non-interworking code.  This option is not valid in AAPCS configurations
because interworking is enabled by default.


=item B<-mcaller-super-interworking>

Allows calls via function pointers (including virtual functions) to
execute correctly regardless of whether the target code has been
compiled for interworking or not.  There is a small overhead in the cost
of executing a function pointer if this option is enabled.  This option
is not valid in AAPCS configurations because interworking is enabled
by default.


=item B<-mtp=>I<name>

Specify the access model for the thread local storage pointer.  The valid
models are B<soft>, which generates calls to C<__aeabi_read_tp>,
B<cp15>, which fetches the thread pointer from C<cp15> directly
(supported in the arm6k architecture), and B<auto>, which uses the
best available method for the selected processor.  The default setting is
B<auto>.


=item B<-mword-relocations>

Only generate absolute relocations on word sized values (i.e. R_ARM_ABS32).
This is enabled by default on targets (uClinux, SymbianOS) where the runtime
loader imposes this restriction, and when B<-fpic> or B<-fPIC>
is specified.


=item B<-mfix-cortex-m3-ldrd>

Some Cortex-M3 cores can cause data corruption when C<ldrd> instructions
with overlapping destination and base registers are used.  This option avoids
generating these instructions.  This option is enabled by default when
B<-mcpu=cortex-m3> is specified.


=back



=head3 AVR Options


These options are defined for AVR implementations:


=over 4


=item B<-mmcu=>I<mcu>

Specify ATMEL AVR instruction set or MCU type.

Instruction set avr1 is for the minimal AVR core, not supported by the C
compiler, only for assembler programs (MCU types: at90s1200, attiny10,
attiny11, attiny12, attiny15, attiny28).

Instruction set avr2 (default) is for the classic AVR core with up to
8K program memory space (MCU types: at90s2313, at90s2323, attiny22,
at90s2333, at90s2343, at90s4414, at90s4433, at90s4434, at90s8515,
at90c8534, at90s8535).

Instruction set avr3 is for the classic AVR core with up to 128K program
memory space (MCU types: atmega103, atmega603, at43usb320, at76c711).

Instruction set avr4 is for the enhanced AVR core with up to 8K program
memory space (MCU types: atmega8, atmega83, atmega85).

Instruction set avr5 is for the enhanced AVR core with up to 128K program
memory space (MCU types: atmega16, atmega161, atmega163, atmega32, atmega323,
atmega64, atmega128, at43usb355, at94k).


=item B<-mno-interrupts>

Generated code is not compatible with hardware interrupts.
Code size will be smaller.


=item B<-mcall-prologues>

Functions prologues/epilogues expanded as call to appropriate
subroutines.  Code size will be smaller.


=item B<-mtiny-stack>

Change only the low 8 bits of the stack pointer.


=item B<-mint8>

Assume int to be 8 bit integer.  This affects the sizes of all types: A
char will be 1 byte, an int will be 1 byte, a long will be 2 bytes
and long long will be 4 bytes.  Please note that this option does not
comply to the C standards, but it will provide you with smaller code
size.

=back



=head4 C<EIND> and Devices with more than 128k Bytes of Flash


Pointers in the implementation are 16 bits wide.
The address of a function or label is represented as word address so
that indirect jumps and calls can address any code address in the
range of 64k words.

In order to faciliate indirect jump on devices with more than 128k
bytes of program memory space, there is a special function register called
C<EIND> that serves as most significant part of the target address
when C<EICALL> or C<EIJMP> instructions are used.

Indirect jumps and calls on these devices are handled as follows and
are subject to some limitations:


=over 4



=item *

The compiler never sets C<EIND>.


=item *

The startup code from libgcc never sets C<EIND>.
Notice that startup code is a blend of code from libgcc and avr-libc.
For the impact of avr-libc on C<EIND>, see the
S<avr-libc user manual> (C<http://nongnu.org/avr-libc/user-manual>).


=item *

The compiler uses C<EIND> implicitely in C<EICALL>/C<EIJMP>
instructions or might read C<EIND> directly.


=item *

The compiler assumes that C<EIND> never changes during the startup
code or run of the application. In particular, C<EIND> is not
saved/restored in function or interrupt service routine
prologue/epilogue.


=item *

It is legitimate for user-specific startup code to set up C<EIND>
early, for example by means of initialization code located in
section C<.init3>, and thus prior to general startup code that
initializes RAM and calls constructors.


=item *

For indirect calls to functions and computed goto, the linker will
generate I<stubs>. Stubs are jump pads sometimes also called
I<trampolines>. Thus, the indirect call/jump will jump to such a stub.
The stub contains a direct jump to the desired address.


=item *

Stubs will be generated automatically by the linker if
the following two conditions are met:

=over 4



=item -<The address of a label is taken by means of the C<gs> modifier>

(short for I<generate stubs>) like so:
	
	LDI r24, lo8(gs(<func>))
	LDI r25, hi8(gs(<func>))


=item -<The final location of that label is in a code segment>

I<outside> the segment where the stubs are located.

=back



=item *

The compiler will emit such C<gs> modifiers for code labels in the
following situations:

=over 4


=item -<Taking address of a function or code label.>


=item -<Computed goto.>


=item -<If prologue-save function is used, see B<-mcall-prologues>>

command line option.

=item -<Switch/case dispatch tables. If you do not want such dispatch>

tables you can specify the B<-fno-jump-tables> command line option.

=item -<C and C++ constructors/destructors called during startup/shutdown.>


=item -<If the tools hit a C<gs()> modifier explained above.>


=back



=item *

The default linker script is arranged for code with C<EIND = 0>.
If code is supposed to work for a setup with C<EIND != 0>, a custom
linker script has to be used in order to place the sections whose
name start with C<.trampolines> into the segment where C<EIND>
points to.


=item *

Jumping to non-symbolic addresses like so is I<not> supported:

	
	int main (void)
	{
	    /* Call function at word address 0x2 */
	    return ((int(*)(void)) 0x2)();
	}


Instead, a stub has to be set up:

	
	int main (void)
	{
	    extern int func_4 (void);
	
	    /* Call function at byte address 0x4 */
	    return func_4();
	}


and the application be linked with C<-Wl,--defsym,func_4=0x4>.
Alternatively, C<func_4> can be defined in the linker script.

=back



=head3 Blackfin Options



=over 4


=item B<-mcpu=>I<cpu>[B<->I<sirevision>]

Specifies the name of the target Blackfin processor.  Currently, I<cpu>
can be one of B<bf512>, B<bf514>, B<bf516>, B<bf518>,
B<bf522>, B<bf523>, B<bf524>, B<bf525>, B<bf526>,
B<bf527>, B<bf531>, B<bf532>, B<bf533>,
B<bf534>, B<bf536>, B<bf537>, B<bf538>, B<bf539>,
B<bf542>, B<bf544>, B<bf547>, B<bf548>, B<bf549>,
B<bf542m>, B<bf544m>, B<bf547m>, B<bf548m>, B<bf549m>,
B<bf561>.
The optional I<sirevision> specifies the silicon revision of the target
Blackfin processor.  Any workarounds available for the targeted silicon revision
will be enabled.  If I<sirevision> is B<none>, no workarounds are enabled.
If I<sirevision> is B<any>, all workarounds for the targeted processor
will be enabled.  The C<__SILICON_REVISION__> macro is defined to two
hexadecimal digits representing the major and minor numbers in the silicon
revision.  If I<sirevision> is B<none>, the C<__SILICON_REVISION__>
is not defined.  If I<sirevision> is B<any>, the
C<__SILICON_REVISION__> is defined to be C<0xffff>.
If this optional I<sirevision> is not used, GCC assumes the latest known
silicon revision of the targeted Blackfin processor.

Support for B<bf561> is incomplete.  For B<bf561>,
Only the processor macro is defined.
Without this option, B<bf532> is used as the processor by default.
The corresponding predefined processor macros for I<cpu> is to
be defined.  And for B<bfin-elf> toolchain, this causes the hardware BSP
provided by libgloss to be linked in if B<-msim> is not given.


=item B<-msim>

Specifies that the program will be run on the simulator.  This causes
the simulator BSP provided by libgloss to be linked in.  This option
has effect only for B<bfin-elf> toolchain.
Certain other options, such as B<-mid-shared-library> and
B<-mfdpic>, imply B<-msim>.


=item B<-momit-leaf-frame-pointer>

Don't keep the frame pointer in a register for leaf functions.  This
avoids the instructions to save, set up and restore frame pointers and
makes an extra register available in leaf functions.  The option
B<-fomit-frame-pointer> removes the frame pointer for all functions
which might make debugging harder.


=item B<-mspecld-anomaly>

When enabled, the compiler will ensure that the generated code does not
contain speculative loads after jump instructions. If this option is used,
C<__WORKAROUND_SPECULATIVE_LOADS> is defined.


=item B<-mno-specld-anomaly>

Don't generate extra code to prevent speculative loads from occurring.


=item B<-mcsync-anomaly>

When enabled, the compiler will ensure that the generated code does not
contain CSYNC or SSYNC instructions too soon after conditional branches.
If this option is used, C<__WORKAROUND_SPECULATIVE_SYNCS> is defined.


=item B<-mno-csync-anomaly>

Don't generate extra code to prevent CSYNC or SSYNC instructions from
occurring too soon after a conditional branch.


=item B<-mlow-64k>

When enabled, the compiler is free to take advantage of the knowledge that
the entire program fits into the low 64k of memory.


=item B<-mno-low-64k>

Assume that the program is arbitrarily large.  This is the default.


=item B<-mstack-check-l1>

Do stack checking using information placed into L1 scratchpad memory by the
uClinux kernel.


=item B<-mid-shared-library>

Generate code that supports shared libraries via the library ID method.
This allows for execute in place and shared libraries in an environment
without virtual memory management.  This option implies B<-fPIC>.
With a B<bfin-elf> target, this option implies B<-msim>.


=item B<-mno-id-shared-library>

Generate code that doesn't assume ID based shared libraries are being used.
This is the default.


=item B<-mleaf-id-shared-library>

Generate code that supports shared libraries via the library ID method,
but assumes that this library or executable won't link against any other
ID shared libraries.  That allows the compiler to use faster code for jumps
and calls.


=item B<-mno-leaf-id-shared-library>

Do not assume that the code being compiled won't link against any ID shared
libraries.  Slower code will be generated for jump and call insns.


=item B<-mshared-library-id=n>

Specified the identification number of the ID based shared library being
compiled.  Specifying a value of 0 will generate more compact code, specifying
other values will force the allocation of that number to the current
library but is no more space or time efficient than omitting this option.


=item B<-msep-data>

Generate code that allows the data segment to be located in a different
area of memory from the text segment.  This allows for execute in place in
an environment without virtual memory management by eliminating relocations
against the text section.


=item B<-mno-sep-data>

Generate code that assumes that the data segment follows the text segment.
This is the default.


=item B<-mlong-calls>


=item B<-mno-long-calls>

Tells the compiler to perform function calls by first loading the
address of the function into a register and then performing a subroutine
call on this register.  This switch is needed if the target function
will lie outside of the 24 bit addressing range of the offset based
version of subroutine call instruction.

This feature is not enabled by default.  Specifying
B<-mno-long-calls> will restore the default behavior.  Note these
switches have no effect on how the compiler generates code to handle
function calls via function pointers.


=item B<-mfast-fp>

Link with the fast floating-point library. This library relaxes some of
the IEEE floating-point standard's rules for checking inputs against
Not-a-Number (NAN), in the interest of performance.


=item B<-minline-plt>

Enable inlining of PLT entries in function calls to functions that are
not known to bind locally.  It has no effect without B<-mfdpic>.


=item B<-mmulticore>

Build standalone application for multicore Blackfin processor. Proper
start files and link scripts will be used to support multicore.
This option defines C<__BFIN_MULTICORE>. It can only be used with
B<-mcpu=bf561>[B<->I<sirevision>]. It can be used with
B<-mcorea> or B<-mcoreb>. If it's used without
B<-mcorea> or B<-mcoreb>, single application/dual core
programming model is used. In this model, the main function of Core B
should be named as coreb_main. If it's used with B<-mcorea> or
B<-mcoreb>, one application per core programming model is used.
If this option is not used, single core application programming
model is used.


=item B<-mcorea>

Build standalone application for Core A of BF561 when using
one application per core programming model. Proper start files
and link scripts will be used to support Core A. This option
defines C<__BFIN_COREA>. It must be used with B<-mmulticore>.


=item B<-mcoreb>

Build standalone application for Core B of BF561 when using
one application per core programming model. Proper start files
and link scripts will be used to support Core B. This option
defines C<__BFIN_COREB>. When this option is used, coreb_main
should be used instead of main. It must be used with
B<-mmulticore>.


=item B<-msdram>

Build standalone application for SDRAM. Proper start files and
link scripts will be used to put the application into SDRAM.
Loader should initialize SDRAM before loading the application
into SDRAM. This option defines C<__BFIN_SDRAM>.


=item B<-micplb>

Assume that ICPLBs are enabled at runtime.  This has an effect on certain
anomaly workarounds.  For Linux targets, the default is to assume ICPLBs
are enabled; for standalone applications the default is off.

=back



=head3 CRIS Options


These options are defined specifically for the CRIS ports.


=over 4


=item B<-march=>I<architecture-type>


=item B<-mcpu=>I<architecture-type>

Generate code for the specified architecture.  The choices for
I<architecture-type> are B<v3>, B<v8> and B<v10> for
respectively ETRAXS< >4, ETRAXS< >100, and ETRAXS< >100S< >LX.
Default is B<v0> except for cris-axis-linux-gnu, where the default is
B<v10>.


=item B<-mtune=>I<architecture-type>

Tune to I<architecture-type> everything applicable about the generated
code, except for the ABI and the set of available instructions.  The
choices for I<architecture-type> are the same as for
B<-march=>I<architecture-type>.


=item B<-mmax-stack-frame=>I<n>

Warn when the stack frame of a function exceeds I<n> bytes.


=item B<-metrax4>


=item B<-metrax100>

The options B<-metrax4> and B<-metrax100> are synonyms for
B<-march=v3> and B<-march=v8> respectively.


=item B<-mmul-bug-workaround>


=item B<-mno-mul-bug-workaround>

Work around a bug in the C<muls> and C<mulu> instructions for CPU
models where it applies.  This option is active by default.


=item B<-mpdebug>

Enable CRIS-specific verbose debug-related information in the assembly
code.  This option also has the effect to turn off the B<#NO_APP>
formatted-code indicator to the assembler at the beginning of the
assembly file.


=item B<-mcc-init>

Do not use condition-code results from previous instruction; always emit
compare and test instructions before use of condition codes.


=item B<-mno-side-effects>

Do not emit instructions with side-effects in addressing modes other than
post-increment.


=item B<-mstack-align>


=item B<-mno-stack-align>


=item B<-mdata-align>


=item B<-mno-data-align>


=item B<-mconst-align>


=item B<-mno-const-align>

These options (no-options) arranges (eliminate arrangements) for the
stack-frame, individual data and constants to be aligned for the maximum
single data access size for the chosen CPU model.  The default is to
arrange for 32-bit alignment.  ABI details such as structure layout are
not affected by these options.


=item B<-m32-bit>


=item B<-m16-bit>


=item B<-m8-bit>

Similar to the stack- data- and const-align options above, these options
arrange for stack-frame, writable data and constants to all be 32-bit,
16-bit or 8-bit aligned.  The default is 32-bit alignment.


=item B<-mno-prologue-epilogue>


=item B<-mprologue-epilogue>

With B<-mno-prologue-epilogue>, the normal function prologue and
epilogue that sets up the stack-frame are omitted and no return
instructions or return sequences are generated in the code.  Use this
option only together with visual inspection of the compiled code: no
warnings or errors are generated when call-saved registers must be saved,
or storage for local variable needs to be allocated.


=item B<-mno-gotplt>


=item B<-mgotplt>

With B<-fpic> and B<-fPIC>, don't generate (do generate)
instruction sequences that load addresses for functions from the PLT part
of the GOT rather than (traditional on other architectures) calls to the
PLT.  The default is B<-mgotplt>.


=item B<-melf>

Legacy no-op option only recognized with the cris-axis-elf and
cris-axis-linux-gnu targets.


=item B<-mlinux>

Legacy no-op option only recognized with the cris-axis-linux-gnu target.


=item B<-sim>

This option, recognized for the cris-axis-elf arranges
to link with input-output functions from a simulator library.  Code,
initialized data and zero-initialized data are allocated consecutively.


=item B<-sim2>

Like B<-sim>, but pass linker options to locate initialized data at
0x40000000 and zero-initialized data at 0x80000000.

=back



=head3 CRX Options


These options are defined specifically for the CRX ports.


=over 4



=item B<-mmac>

Enable the use of multiply-accumulate instructions. Disabled by default.


=item B<-mpush-args>

Push instructions will be used to pass outgoing arguments when functions
are called. Enabled by default.

=back



=head3 Darwin Options


These options are defined for all architectures running the Darwin operating
system.

FSF GCC on Darwin does not create "fat" object files; it will create
an object file for the single architecture that it was built to
target.  Apple's GCC on Darwin does create "fat" files if multiple
B<-arch> options are used; it does so by running the compiler or
linker multiple times and joining the results together with
F<lipo>.

The subtype of the file created (like B<ppc7400> or B<ppc970> or
B<i686>) is determined by the flags that specify the ISA
that GCC is targetting, like B<-mcpu> or B<-march>.  The
B<-force_cpusubtype_ALL> option can be used to override this.

The Darwin tools vary in their behavior when presented with an ISA
mismatch.  The assembler, F<as>, will only permit instructions to
be used that are valid for the subtype of the file it is generating,
so you cannot put 64-bit instructions in a B<ppc750> object file.
The linker for shared libraries, F</usr/bin/libtool>, will fail
and print an error if asked to create a shared library with a less
restrictive subtype than its input files (for instance, trying to put
a B<ppc970> object file in a B<ppc7400> library).  The linker
for executables, F<ld>, will quietly give the executable the most
restrictive subtype of any of its input files.


=over 4


=item B<-F>I<dir>

Add the framework directory I<dir> to the head of the list of
directories to be searched for header files.  These directories are
interleaved with those specified by B<-I> options and are
scanned in a left-to-right order.

A framework directory is a directory with frameworks in it.  A
framework is a directory with a B<"Headers"> and/or
B<"PrivateHeaders"> directory contained directly in it that ends
in B<".framework">.  The name of a framework is the name of this
directory excluding the B<".framework">.  Headers associated with
the framework are found in one of those two directories, with
B<"Headers"> being searched first.  A subframework is a framework
directory that is in a framework's B<"Frameworks"> directory.
Includes of subframework headers can only appear in a header of a
framework that contains the subframework, or in a sibling subframework
header.  Two subframeworks are siblings if they occur in the same
framework.  A subframework should not have the same name as a
framework, a warning will be issued if this is violated.  Currently a
subframework cannot have subframeworks, in the future, the mechanism
may be extended to support this.  The standard frameworks can be found
in B<"/System/Library/Frameworks"> and
B<"/Library/Frameworks">.  An example include looks like
C<#include E<lt>Framework/header.hE<gt>>, where B<Framework> denotes
the name of the framework and header.h is found in the
B<"PrivateHeaders"> or B<"Headers"> directory.


=item B<-iframework>I<dir>

Like B<-F> except the directory is a treated as a system
directory.  The main difference between this B<-iframework> and
B<-F> is that with B<-iframework> the compiler does not
warn about constructs contained within header files found via
I<dir>.  This option is valid only for the C family of languages.


=item B<-gused>

Emit debugging information for symbols that are used.  For STABS
debugging format, this enables B<-feliminate-unused-debug-symbols>.
This is by default ON.


=item B<-gfull>

Emit debugging information for all symbols and types.


=item B<-mmacosx-version-min=>I<version>

The earliest version of MacOS X that this executable will run on
is I<version>.  Typical values of I<version> include C<10.1>,
C<10.2>, and C<10.3.9>.

If the compiler was built to use the system's headers by default,
then the default for this option is the system version on which the
compiler is running, otherwise the default is to make choices which
are compatible with as many systems and code bases as possible.


=item B<-mkernel>

Enable kernel development mode.  The B<-mkernel> option sets
B<-static>, B<-fno-common>, B<-fno-cxa-atexit>,
B<-fno-exceptions>, B<-fno-non-call-exceptions>,
B<-fapple-kext>, B<-fno-weak> and B<-fno-rtti> where
applicable.  This mode also sets B<-mno-altivec>,
B<-msoft-float>, B<-fno-builtin> and
B<-mlong-branch> for PowerPC targets.


=item B<-mone-byte-bool>

Override the defaults for B<bool> so that B<sizeof(bool)==1>.
By default B<sizeof(bool)> is B<4> when compiling for
Darwin/PowerPC and B<1> when compiling for Darwin/x86, so this
option has no effect on x86.

B<Warning:> The B<-mone-byte-bool> switch causes GCC
to generate code that is not binary compatible with code generated
without that switch.  Using this switch may require recompiling all
other modules in a program, including system libraries.  Use this
switch to conform to a non-default data model.


=item B<-mfix-and-continue>


=item B<-ffix-and-continue>


=item B<-findirect-data>

Generate code suitable for fast turn around development.  Needed to
enable gdb to dynamically load C<.o> files into already running
programs.  B<-findirect-data> and B<-ffix-and-continue>
are provided for backwards compatibility.


=item B<-all_load>

Loads all members of static archive libraries.
See man ld(1) for more information.


=item B<-arch_errors_fatal>

Cause the errors having to do with files that have the wrong architecture
to be fatal.


=item B<-bind_at_load>

Causes the output file to be marked such that the dynamic linker will
bind all undefined references when the file is loaded or launched.


=item B<-bundle>

Produce a Mach-o bundle format file.
See man ld(1) for more information.


=item B<-bundle_loader> I<executable>

This option specifies the I<executable> that will be loading the build
output file being linked.  See man ld(1) for more information.


=item B<-dynamiclib>

When passed this option, GCC will produce a dynamic library instead of
an executable when linking, using the Darwin F<libtool> command.


=item B<-force_cpusubtype_ALL>

This causes GCC's output file to have the I<ALL> subtype, instead of
one controlled by the B<-mcpu> or B<-march> option.


=item B<-allowable_client>  I<client_name>


=item B<-client_name>


=item B<-compatibility_version>


=item B<-current_version>


=item B<-dead_strip>


=item B<-dependency-file>


=item B<-dylib_file>


=item B<-dylinker_install_name>


=item B<-dynamic>


=item B<-exported_symbols_list>


=item B<-filelist>


=item B<-flat_namespace>


=item B<-force_flat_namespace>


=item B<-headerpad_max_install_names>


=item B<-image_base>


=item B<-init>


=item B<-install_name>


=item B<-keep_private_externs>


=item B<-multi_module>


=item B<-multiply_defined>


=item B<-multiply_defined_unused>


=item B<-noall_load>


=item B<-no_dead_strip_inits_and_terms>


=item B<-nofixprebinding>


=item B<-nomultidefs>


=item B<-noprebind>


=item B<-noseglinkedit>


=item B<-pagezero_size>


=item B<-prebind>


=item B<-prebind_all_twolevel_modules>


=item B<-private_bundle>


=item B<-read_only_relocs>


=item B<-sectalign>


=item B<-sectobjectsymbols>


=item B<-whyload>


=item B<-seg1addr>


=item B<-sectcreate>


=item B<-sectobjectsymbols>


=item B<-sectorder>


=item B<-segaddr>


=item B<-segs_read_only_addr>


=item B<-segs_read_write_addr>


=item B<-seg_addr_table>


=item B<-seg_addr_table_filename>


=item B<-seglinkedit>


=item B<-segprot>


=item B<-segs_read_only_addr>


=item B<-segs_read_write_addr>


=item B<-single_module>


=item B<-static>


=item B<-sub_library>


=item B<-sub_umbrella>


=item B<-twolevel_namespace>


=item B<-umbrella>


=item B<-undefined>


=item B<-unexported_symbols_list>


=item B<-weak_reference_mismatches>


=item B<-whatsloaded>

These options are passed to the Darwin linker.  The Darwin linker man page
describes them in detail.

=back



=head3 DEC Alpha Options


These B<-m> options are defined for the DEC Alpha implementations:


=over 4


=item B<-mno-soft-float>


=item B<-msoft-float>

Use (do not use) the hardware floating-point instructions for
floating-point operations.  When B<-msoft-float> is specified,
functions in F<libgcc.a> will be used to perform floating-point
operations.  Unless they are replaced by routines that emulate the
floating-point operations, or compiled in such a way as to call such
emulations routines, these routines will issue floating-point
operations.   If you are compiling for an Alpha without floating-point
operations, you must ensure that the library is built so as not to call
them.

Note that Alpha implementations without floating-point operations are
required to have floating-point registers.


=item B<-mfp-reg>


=item B<-mno-fp-regs>

Generate code that uses (does not use) the floating-point register set.
B<-mno-fp-regs> implies B<-msoft-float>.  If the floating-point
register set is not used, floating point operands are passed in integer
registers as if they were integers and floating-point results are passed
in C<$0> instead of C<$f0>.  This is a non-standard calling sequence,
so any function with a floating-point argument or return value called by code
compiled with B<-mno-fp-regs> must also be compiled with that
option.

A typical use of this option is building a kernel that does not use,
and hence need not save and restore, any floating-point registers.


=item B<-mieee>

The Alpha architecture implements floating-point hardware optimized for
maximum performance.  It is mostly compliant with the IEEE floating
point standard.  However, for full compliance, software assistance is
required.  This option generates code fully IEEE compliant code
I<except> that the I<inexact-flag> is not maintained (see below).
If this option is turned on, the preprocessor macro C<_IEEE_FP> is
defined during compilation.  The resulting code is less efficient but is
able to correctly support denormalized numbers and exceptional IEEE
values such as not-a-number and plus/minus infinity.  Other Alpha
compilers call this option B<-ieee_with_no_inexact>.


=item B<-mieee-with-inexact>

This is like B<-mieee> except the generated code also maintains
the IEEE I<inexact-flag>.  Turning on this option causes the
generated code to implement fully-compliant IEEE math.  In addition to
C<_IEEE_FP>, C<_IEEE_FP_EXACT> is defined as a preprocessor
macro.  On some Alpha implementations the resulting code may execute
significantly slower than the code generated by default.  Since there is
very little code that depends on the I<inexact-flag>, you should
normally not specify this option.  Other Alpha compilers call this
option B<-ieee_with_inexact>.


=item B<-mfp-trap-mode=>I<trap-mode>

This option controls what floating-point related traps are enabled.
Other Alpha compilers call this option B<-fptm> I<trap-mode>.
The trap mode can be set to one of four values:


=over 4


=item B<n>

This is the default (normal) setting.  The only traps that are enabled
are the ones that cannot be disabled in software (e.g., division by zero
trap).


=item B<u>

In addition to the traps enabled by B<n>, underflow traps are enabled
as well.


=item B<su>

Like B<u>, but the instructions are marked to be safe for software
completion (see Alpha architecture manual for details).


=item B<sui>

Like B<su>, but inexact traps are enabled as well.

=back



=item B<-mfp-rounding-mode=>I<rounding-mode>

Selects the IEEE rounding mode.  Other Alpha compilers call this option
B<-fprm> I<rounding-mode>.  The I<rounding-mode> can be one
of:


=over 4


=item B<n>

Normal IEEE rounding mode.  Floating point numbers are rounded towards
the nearest machine number or towards the even machine number in case
of a tie.


=item B<m>

Round towards minus infinity.


=item B<c>

Chopped rounding mode.  Floating point numbers are rounded towards zero.


=item B<d>

Dynamic rounding mode.  A field in the floating point control register
(I<fpcr>, see Alpha architecture reference manual) controls the
rounding mode in effect.  The C library initializes this register for
rounding towards plus infinity.  Thus, unless your program modifies the
I<fpcr>, B<d> corresponds to round towards plus infinity.

=back



=item B<-mtrap-precision=>I<trap-precision>

In the Alpha architecture, floating point traps are imprecise.  This
means without software assistance it is impossible to recover from a
floating trap and program execution normally needs to be terminated.
GCC can generate code that can assist operating system trap handlers
in determining the exact location that caused a floating point trap.
Depending on the requirements of an application, different levels of
precisions can be selected:


=over 4


=item B<p>

Program precision.  This option is the default and means a trap handler
can only identify which program caused a floating point exception.


=item B<f>

Function precision.  The trap handler can determine the function that
caused a floating point exception.


=item B<i>

Instruction precision.  The trap handler can determine the exact
instruction that caused a floating point exception.

=back


Other Alpha compilers provide the equivalent options called
B<-scope_safe> and B<-resumption_safe>.


=item B<-mieee-conformant>

This option marks the generated code as IEEE conformant.  You must not
use this option unless you also specify B<-mtrap-precision=i> and either
B<-mfp-trap-mode=su> or B<-mfp-trap-mode=sui>.  Its only effect
is to emit the line B<.eflag 48> in the function prologue of the
generated assembly file.  Under DEC Unix, this has the effect that
IEEE-conformant math library routines will be linked in.


=item B<-mbuild-constants>

Normally GCC examines a 32- or 64-bit integer constant to
see if it can construct it from smaller constants in two or three
instructions.  If it cannot, it will output the constant as a literal and
generate code to load it from the data segment at runtime.

Use this option to require GCC to construct I<all> integer constants
using code, even if it takes more instructions (the maximum is six).

You would typically use this option to build a shared library dynamic
loader.  Itself a shared library, it must relocate itself in memory
before it can find the variables and constants in its own data segment.


=item B<-malpha-as>


=item B<-mgas>

Select whether to generate code to be assembled by the vendor-supplied
assembler (B<-malpha-as>) or by the GNU assembler B<-mgas>.


=item B<-mbwx>


=item B<-mno-bwx>


=item B<-mcix>


=item B<-mno-cix>


=item B<-mfix>


=item B<-mno-fix>


=item B<-mmax>


=item B<-mno-max>

Indicate whether GCC should generate code to use the optional BWX,
CIX, FIX and MAX instruction sets.  The default is to use the instruction
sets supported by the CPU type specified via B<-mcpu=> option or that
of the CPU on which GCC was built if none was specified.


=item B<-mfloat-vax>


=item B<-mfloat-ieee>

Generate code that uses (does not use) VAX F and G floating point
arithmetic instead of IEEE single and double precision.


=item B<-mexplicit-relocs>


=item B<-mno-explicit-relocs>

Older Alpha assemblers provided no way to generate symbol relocations
except via assembler macros.  Use of these macros does not allow
optimal instruction scheduling.  GNU binutils as of version 2.12
supports a new syntax that allows the compiler to explicitly mark
which relocations should apply to which instructions.  This option
is mostly useful for debugging, as GCC detects the capabilities of
the assembler when it is built and sets the default accordingly.


=item B<-msmall-data>


=item B<-mlarge-data>

When B<-mexplicit-relocs> is in effect, static data is
accessed via I<gp-relative> relocations.  When B<-msmall-data>
is used, objects 8 bytes long or smaller are placed in a I<small data area>
(the C<.sdata> and C<.sbss> sections) and are accessed via
16-bit relocations off of the C<$gp> register.  This limits the
size of the small data area to 64KB, but allows the variables to be
directly accessed via a single instruction.

The default is B<-mlarge-data>.  With this option the data area
is limited to just below 2GB.  Programs that require more than 2GB of
data must use C<malloc> or C<mmap> to allocate the data in the
heap instead of in the program's data segment.

When generating code for shared libraries, B<-fpic> implies
B<-msmall-data> and B<-fPIC> implies B<-mlarge-data>.


=item B<-msmall-text>


=item B<-mlarge-text>

When B<-msmall-text> is used, the compiler assumes that the
code of the entire program (or shared library) fits in 4MB, and is
thus reachable with a branch instruction.  When B<-msmall-data>
is used, the compiler can assume that all local symbols share the
same C<$gp> value, and thus reduce the number of instructions
required for a function call from 4 to 1.

The default is B<-mlarge-text>.


=item B<-mcpu=>I<cpu_type>

Set the instruction set and instruction scheduling parameters for
machine type I<cpu_type>.  You can specify either the B<EV>
style name or the corresponding chip number.  GCC supports scheduling
parameters for the EV4, EV5 and EV6 family of processors and will
choose the default values for the instruction set from the processor
you specify.  If you do not specify a processor type, GCC will default
to the processor on which the compiler was built.

Supported values for I<cpu_type> are


=over 4


=item B<ev4>


=item B<ev45>


=item B<21064>

Schedules as an EV4 and has no instruction set extensions.


=item B<ev5>


=item B<21164>

Schedules as an EV5 and has no instruction set extensions.


=item B<ev56>


=item B<21164a>

Schedules as an EV5 and supports the BWX extension.


=item B<pca56>


=item B<21164pc>


=item B<21164PC>

Schedules as an EV5 and supports the BWX and MAX extensions.


=item B<ev6>


=item B<21264>

Schedules as an EV6 and supports the BWX, FIX, and MAX extensions.


=item B<ev67>


=item B<21264a>

Schedules as an EV6 and supports the BWX, CIX, FIX, and MAX extensions.

=back


Native Linux/GNU toolchains also support the value B<native>,
which selects the best architecture option for the host processor.
B<-mcpu=native> has no effect if GCC does not recognize
the processor.


=item B<-mtune=>I<cpu_type>

Set only the instruction scheduling parameters for machine type
I<cpu_type>.  The instruction set is not changed.

Native Linux/GNU toolchains also support the value B<native>,
which selects the best architecture option for the host processor.
B<-mtune=native> has no effect if GCC does not recognize
the processor.


=item B<-mmemory-latency=>I<time>

Sets the latency the scheduler should assume for typical memory
references as seen by the application.  This number is highly
dependent on the memory access patterns used by the application
and the size of the external cache on the machine.

Valid options for I<time> are


=over 4


=item I<number>

A decimal number representing clock cycles.


=item B<L1>


=item B<L2>


=item B<L3>


=item B<main>

The compiler contains estimates of the number of clock cycles for
"typical" EV4 & EV5 hardware for the Level 1, 2 & 3 caches
(also called Dcache, Scache, and Bcache), as well as to main memory.
Note that L3 is only valid for EV5.


=back


=back



=head3 DEC Alpha/VMS Options


These B<-m> options are defined for the DEC Alpha/VMS implementations:


=over 4


=item B<-mvms-return-codes>

Return VMS condition codes from main.  The default is to return POSIX
style condition (e.g. error) codes.


=item B<-mdebug-main=>I<prefix>

Flag the first routine whose name starts with I<prefix> as the main
routine for the debugger.


=item B<-mmalloc64>

Default to 64bit memory allocation routines.

=back



=head3 FR30 Options


These options are defined specifically for the FR30 port.


=over 4



=item B<-msmall-model>

Use the small address space model.  This can produce smaller code, but
it does assume that all symbolic values and addresses will fit into a
20-bit range.


=item B<-mno-lsim>

Assume that run-time support has been provided and so there is no need
to include the simulator library (F<libsim.a>) on the linker
command line.


=back



=head3 FRV Options



=over 4


=item B<-mgpr-32>


Only use the first 32 general purpose registers.


=item B<-mgpr-64>


Use all 64 general purpose registers.


=item B<-mfpr-32>


Use only the first 32 floating point registers.


=item B<-mfpr-64>


Use all 64 floating point registers


=item B<-mhard-float>


Use hardware instructions for floating point operations.


=item B<-msoft-float>


Use library routines for floating point operations.


=item B<-malloc-cc>


Dynamically allocate condition code registers.


=item B<-mfixed-cc>


Do not try to dynamically allocate condition code registers, only
use C<icc0> and C<fcc0>.


=item B<-mdword>


Change ABI to use double word insns.


=item B<-mno-dword>


Do not use double word instructions.


=item B<-mdouble>


Use floating point double instructions.


=item B<-mno-double>


Do not use floating point double instructions.


=item B<-mmedia>


Use media instructions.


=item B<-mno-media>


Do not use media instructions.


=item B<-mmuladd>


Use multiply and add/subtract instructions.


=item B<-mno-muladd>


Do not use multiply and add/subtract instructions.


=item B<-mfdpic>


Select the FDPIC ABI, that uses function descriptors to represent
pointers to functions.  Without any PIC/PIE-related options, it
implies B<-fPIE>.  With B<-fpic> or B<-fpie>, it
assumes GOT entries and small data are within a 12-bit range from the
GOT base address; with B<-fPIC> or B<-fPIE>, GOT offsets
are computed with 32 bits.
With a B<bfin-elf> target, this option implies B<-msim>.


=item B<-minline-plt>


Enable inlining of PLT entries in function calls to functions that are
not known to bind locally.  It has no effect without B<-mfdpic>.
It's enabled by default if optimizing for speed and compiling for
shared libraries (i.e., B<-fPIC> or B<-fpic>), or when an
optimization option such as B<-O3> or above is present in the
command line.


=item B<-mTLS>


Assume a large TLS segment when generating thread-local code.


=item B<-mtls>


Do not assume a large TLS segment when generating thread-local code.


=item B<-mgprel-ro>


Enable the use of C<GPREL> relocations in the FDPIC ABI for data
that is known to be in read-only sections.  It's enabled by default,
except for B<-fpic> or B<-fpie>: even though it may help
make the global offset table smaller, it trades 1 instruction for 4.
With B<-fPIC> or B<-fPIE>, it trades 3 instructions for 4,
one of which may be shared by multiple symbols, and it avoids the need
for a GOT entry for the referenced symbol, so it's more likely to be a
win.  If it is not, B<-mno-gprel-ro> can be used to disable it.


=item B<-multilib-library-pic>


Link with the (library, not FD) pic libraries.  It's implied by
B<-mlibrary-pic>, as well as by B<-fPIC> and
B<-fpic> without B<-mfdpic>.  You should never have to use
it explicitly.


=item B<-mlinked-fp>


Follow the EABI requirement of always creating a frame pointer whenever
a stack frame is allocated.  This option is enabled by default and can
be disabled with B<-mno-linked-fp>.


=item B<-mlong-calls>


Use indirect addressing to call functions outside the current
compilation unit.  This allows the functions to be placed anywhere
within the 32-bit address space.


=item B<-malign-labels>


Try to align labels to an 8-byte boundary by inserting nops into the
previous packet.  This option only has an effect when VLIW packing
is enabled.  It doesn't create new packets; it merely adds nops to
existing ones.


=item B<-mlibrary-pic>


Generate position-independent EABI code.


=item B<-macc-4>


Use only the first four media accumulator registers.


=item B<-macc-8>


Use all eight media accumulator registers.


=item B<-mpack>


Pack VLIW instructions.


=item B<-mno-pack>


Do not pack VLIW instructions.


=item B<-mno-eflags>


Do not mark ABI switches in e_flags.


=item B<-mcond-move>


Enable the use of conditional-move instructions (default).

This switch is mainly for debugging the compiler and will likely be removed
in a future version.


=item B<-mno-cond-move>


Disable the use of conditional-move instructions.

This switch is mainly for debugging the compiler and will likely be removed
in a future version.


=item B<-mscc>


Enable the use of conditional set instructions (default).

This switch is mainly for debugging the compiler and will likely be removed
in a future version.


=item B<-mno-scc>


Disable the use of conditional set instructions.

This switch is mainly for debugging the compiler and will likely be removed
in a future version.


=item B<-mcond-exec>


Enable the use of conditional execution (default).

This switch is mainly for debugging the compiler and will likely be removed
in a future version.


=item B<-mno-cond-exec>


Disable the use of conditional execution.

This switch is mainly for debugging the compiler and will likely be removed
in a future version.


=item B<-mvliw-branch>


Run a pass to pack branches into VLIW instructions (default).

This switch is mainly for debugging the compiler and will likely be removed
in a future version.


=item B<-mno-vliw-branch>


Do not run a pass to pack branches into VLIW instructions.

This switch is mainly for debugging the compiler and will likely be removed
in a future version.


=item B<-mmulti-cond-exec>


Enable optimization of C<&&> and C<||> in conditional execution
(default).

This switch is mainly for debugging the compiler and will likely be removed
in a future version.


=item B<-mno-multi-cond-exec>


Disable optimization of C<&&> and C<||> in conditional execution.

This switch is mainly for debugging the compiler and will likely be removed
in a future version.


=item B<-mnested-cond-exec>


Enable nested conditional execution optimizations (default).

This switch is mainly for debugging the compiler and will likely be removed
in a future version.


=item B<-mno-nested-cond-exec>


Disable nested conditional execution optimizations.

This switch is mainly for debugging the compiler and will likely be removed
in a future version.


=item B<-moptimize-membar>


This switch removes redundant C<membar> instructions from the
compiler generated code.  It is enabled by default.


=item B<-mno-optimize-membar>


This switch disables the automatic removal of redundant C<membar>
instructions from the generated code.


=item B<-mtomcat-stats>


Cause gas to print out tomcat statistics.


=item B<-mcpu=>I<cpu>


Select the processor type for which to generate code.  Possible values are
B<frv>, B<fr550>, B<tomcat>, B<fr500>, B<fr450>,
B<fr405>, B<fr400>, B<fr300> and B<simple>.


=back



=head3 GNU/Linux Options


These B<-m> options are defined for GNU/Linux targets:


=over 4


=item B<-mglibc>

Use the GNU C library.  This is the default except
on B<*-*-linux-*uclibc*> and B<*-*-linux-*android*> targets.


=item B<-muclibc>

Use uClibc C library.  This is the default on
B<*-*-linux-*uclibc*> targets.


=item B<-mbionic>

Use Bionic C library.  This is the default on
B<*-*-linux-*android*> targets.


=item B<-mandroid>

Compile code compatible with Android platform.  This is the default on
B<*-*-linux-*android*> targets.

When compiling, this option enables B<-mbionic>, B<-fPIC>,
B<-fno-exceptions> and B<-fno-rtti> by default.  When linking,
this option makes the GCC driver pass Android-specific options to the linker.
Finally, this option causes the preprocessor macro C<__ANDROID__>
to be defined.


=item B<-tno-android-cc>

Disable compilation effects of B<-mandroid>, i.e., do not enable
B<-mbionic>, B<-fPIC>, B<-fno-exceptions> and
B<-fno-rtti> by default.


=item B<-tno-android-ld>

Disable linking effects of B<-mandroid>, i.e., pass standard Linux
linking options to the linker.


=back



=head3 H8/300 Options


These B<-m> options are defined for the H8/300 implementations:


=over 4


=item B<-mrelax>

Shorten some address references at link time, when possible; uses the
linker option B<-relax>.  


=item B<-mh>

Generate code for the H8/300H.


=item B<-ms>

Generate code for the H8S.


=item B<-mn>

Generate code for the H8S and H8/300H in the normal mode.  This switch
must be used either with B<-mh> or B<-ms>.


=item B<-ms2600>

Generate code for the H8S/2600.  This switch must be used with B<-ms>.


=item B<-mint32>

Make C<int> data 32 bits by default.


=item B<-malign-300>

On the H8/300H and H8S, use the same alignment rules as for the H8/300.
The default for the H8/300H and H8S is to align longs and floats on 4
byte boundaries.
B<-malign-300> causes them to be aligned on 2 byte boundaries.
This option has no effect on the H8/300.

=back



=head3 HPPA Options


These B<-m> options are defined for the HPPA family of computers:


=over 4


=item B<-march=>I<architecture-type>

Generate code for the specified architecture.  The choices for
I<architecture-type> are B<1.0> for PA 1.0, B<1.1> for PA
1.1, and B<2.0> for PA 2.0 processors.  Refer to
F</usr/lib/sched.models> on an HP-UX system to determine the proper
architecture option for your machine.  Code compiled for lower numbered
architectures will run on higher numbered architectures, but not the
other way around.


=item B<-mpa-risc-1-0>


=item B<-mpa-risc-1-1>


=item B<-mpa-risc-2-0>

Synonyms for B<-march=1.0>, B<-march=1.1>, and B<-march=2.0> respectively.


=item B<-mbig-switch>

Generate code suitable for big switch tables.  Use this option only if
the assembler/linker complain about out of range branches within a switch
table.


=item B<-mjump-in-delay>

Fill delay slots of function calls with unconditional jump instructions
by modifying the return pointer for the function call to be the target
of the conditional jump.


=item B<-mdisable-fpregs>

Prevent floating point registers from being used in any manner.  This is
necessary for compiling kernels which perform lazy context switching of
floating point registers.  If you use this option and attempt to perform
floating point operations, the compiler will abort.


=item B<-mdisable-indexing>

Prevent the compiler from using indexing address modes.  This avoids some
rather obscure problems when compiling MIG generated code under MACH.


=item B<-mno-space-regs>

Generate code that assumes the target has no space registers.  This allows
GCC to generate faster indirect calls and use unscaled index address modes.

Such code is suitable for level 0 PA systems and kernels.


=item B<-mfast-indirect-calls>

Generate code that assumes calls never cross space boundaries.  This
allows GCC to emit code which performs faster indirect calls.

This option will not work in the presence of shared libraries or nested
functions.


=item B<-mfixed-range=>I<register-range>

Generate code treating the given register range as fixed registers.
A fixed register is one that the register allocator can not use.  This is
useful when compiling kernel code.  A register range is specified as
two registers separated by a dash.  Multiple register ranges can be
specified separated by a comma.


=item B<-mlong-load-store>

Generate 3-instruction load and store sequences as sometimes required by
the HP-UX 10 linker.  This is equivalent to the B<+k> option to
the HP compilers.


=item B<-mportable-runtime>

Use the portable calling conventions proposed by HP for ELF systems.


=item B<-mgas>

Enable the use of assembler directives only GAS understands.


=item B<-mschedule=>I<cpu-type>

Schedule code according to the constraints for the machine type
I<cpu-type>.  The choices for I<cpu-type> are B<700>
B<7100>, B<7100LC>, B<7200>, B<7300> and B<8000>.  Refer
to F</usr/lib/sched.models> on an HP-UX system to determine the
proper scheduling option for your machine.  The default scheduling is
B<8000>.


=item B<-mlinker-opt>

Enable the optimization pass in the HP-UX linker.  Note this makes symbolic
debugging impossible.  It also triggers a bug in the HP-UX 8 and HP-UX 9
linkers in which they give bogus error messages when linking some programs.


=item B<-msoft-float>

Generate output containing library calls for floating point.
B<Warning:> the requisite libraries are not available for all HPPA
targets.  Normally the facilities of the machine's usual C compiler are
used, but this cannot be done directly in cross-compilation.  You must make
your own arrangements to provide suitable library functions for
cross-compilation.

B<-msoft-float> changes the calling convention in the output file;
therefore, it is only useful if you compile I<all> of a program with
this option.  In particular, you need to compile F<libgcc.a>, the
library that comes with GCC, with B<-msoft-float> in order for
this to work.


=item B<-msio>

Generate the predefine, C<_SIO>, for server IO.  The default is
B<-mwsio>.  This generates the predefines, C<__hp9000s700>,
C<__hp9000s700__> and C<_WSIO>, for workstation IO.  These
options are available under HP-UX and HI-UX.


=item B<-mgnu-ld>

Use GNU ld specific options.  This passes B<-shared> to ld when
building a shared library.  It is the default when GCC is configured,
explicitly or implicitly, with the GNU linker.  This option does not
have any affect on which ld is called, it only changes what parameters
are passed to that ld.  The ld that is called is determined by the
B<--with-ld> configure option, GCC's program search path, and
finally by the user's B<PATH>.  The linker used by GCC can be printed
using B<which `gcc -print-prog-name=ld`>.  This option is only available
on the 64 bit HP-UX GCC, i.e. configured with B<hppa*64*-*-hpux*>.


=item B<-mhp-ld>

Use HP ld specific options.  This passes B<-b> to ld when building
a shared library and passes B<+Accept TypeMismatch> to ld on all
links.  It is the default when GCC is configured, explicitly or
implicitly, with the HP linker.  This option does not have any affect on
which ld is called, it only changes what parameters are passed to that
ld.  The ld that is called is determined by the B<--with-ld>
configure option, GCC's program search path, and finally by the user's
B<PATH>.  The linker used by GCC can be printed using B<which
`gcc -print-prog-name=ld`>.  This option is only available on the 64 bit
HP-UX GCC, i.e. configured with B<hppa*64*-*-hpux*>.


=item B<-mlong-calls>

Generate code that uses long call sequences.  This ensures that a call
is always able to reach linker generated stubs.  The default is to generate
long calls only when the distance from the call site to the beginning
of the function or translation unit, as the case may be, exceeds a
predefined limit set by the branch type being used.  The limits for
normal calls are 7,600,000 and 240,000 bytes, respectively for the
PA 2.0 and PA 1.X architectures.  Sibcalls are always limited at
240,000 bytes.

Distances are measured from the beginning of functions when using the
B<-ffunction-sections> option, or when using the B<-mgas>
and B<-mno-portable-runtime> options together under HP-UX with
the SOM linker.

It is normally not desirable to use this option as it will degrade
performance.  However, it may be useful in large applications,
particularly when partial linking is used to build the application.

The types of long calls used depends on the capabilities of the
assembler and linker, and the type of code being generated.  The
impact on systems that support long absolute calls, and long pic
symbol-difference or pc-relative calls should be relatively small.
However, an indirect call is used on 32-bit ELF systems in pic code
and it is quite long.


=item B<-munix=>I<unix-std>

Generate compiler predefines and select a startfile for the specified
UNIX standard.  The choices for I<unix-std> are B<93>, B<95>
and B<98>.  B<93> is supported on all HP-UX versions.  B<95>
is available on HP-UX 10.10 and later.  B<98> is available on HP-UX
11.11 and later.  The default values are B<93> for HP-UX 10.00,
B<95> for HP-UX 10.10 though to 11.00, and B<98> for HP-UX 11.11
and later.

B<-munix=93> provides the same predefines as GCC 3.3 and 3.4.
B<-munix=95> provides additional predefines for C<XOPEN_UNIX>
and C<_XOPEN_SOURCE_EXTENDED>, and the startfile F<unix95.o>.
