B<-munix=98> provides additional predefines for C<_XOPEN_UNIX>,
C<_XOPEN_SOURCE_EXTENDED>, C<_INCLUDE__STDC_A1_SOURCE> and
C<_INCLUDE_XOPEN_SOURCE_500>, and the startfile F<unix98.o>.

It is I<important> to note that this option changes the interfaces
for various library routines.  It also affects the operational behavior
of the C library.  Thus, I<extreme> care is needed in using this
option.

Library code that is intended to operate with more than one UNIX
standard must test, set and restore the variable I<__xpg4_extended_mask>
as appropriate.  Most GNU software doesn't provide this capability.


=item B<-nolibdld>

Suppress the generation of link options to search libdld.sl when the
B<-static> option is specified on HP-UX 10 and later.


=item B<-static>

The HP-UX implementation of setlocale in libc has a dependency on
libdld.sl.  There isn't an archive version of libdld.sl.  Thus,
when the B<-static> option is specified, special link options
are needed to resolve this dependency.

On HP-UX 10 and later, the GCC driver adds the necessary options to
link with libdld.sl when the B<-static> option is specified.
This causes the resulting binary to be dynamic.  On the 64-bit port,
the linkers generate dynamic binaries by default in any case.  The
B<-nolibdld> option can be used to prevent the GCC driver from
adding these link options.


=item B<-threads>

Add support for multithreading with the I<dce thread> library
under HP-UX.  This option sets flags for both the preprocessor and
linker.

=back



=head3 Intel 386 and AMD x86-64 Options


These B<-m> options are defined for the i386 and x86-64 family of
computers:


=over 4


=item B<-mtune=>I<cpu-type>

Tune to I<cpu-type> everything applicable about the generated code, except
for the ABI and the set of available instructions.  The choices for
I<cpu-type> are:

=over 4


=item I<generic>

Produce code optimized for the most common IA32/AMD64/EM64T processors.
If you know the CPU on which your code will run, then you should use
the corresponding B<-mtune> option instead of
B<-mtune=generic>.  But, if you do not know exactly what CPU users
of your application will have, then you should use this option.

As new processors are deployed in the marketplace, the behavior of this
option will change.  Therefore, if you upgrade to a newer version of
GCC, the code generated option will change to reflect the processors
that were most common when that version of GCC was released.

There is no B<-march=generic> option because B<-march>
indicates the instruction set the compiler can use, and there is no
generic instruction set applicable to all processors.  In contrast,
B<-mtune> indicates the processor (or, in this case, collection of
processors) for which the code is optimized.

=item I<native>

This selects the CPU to tune for at compilation time by determining
the processor type of the compiling machine.  Using B<-mtune=native>
will produce code optimized for the local machine under the constraints
of the selected instruction set.  Using B<-march=native> will
enable all instruction subsets supported by the local machine (hence
the result might not run on different machines).

=item I<i386>

Original Intel's i386 CPU.

=item I<i486>

Intel's i486 CPU.  (No scheduling is implemented for this chip.)

=item I<i586, pentium>

Intel Pentium CPU with no MMX support.

=item I<pentium-mmx>

Intel PentiumMMX CPU based on Pentium core with MMX instruction set support.

=item I<pentiumpro>

Intel PentiumPro CPU.

=item I<i686>

Same as C<generic>, but when used as C<march> option, PentiumPro
instruction set will be used, so the code will run on all i686 family chips.

=item I<pentium2>

Intel Pentium2 CPU based on PentiumPro core with MMX instruction set support.

=item I<pentium3, pentium3m>

Intel Pentium3 CPU based on PentiumPro core with MMX and SSE instruction set
support.

=item I<pentium-m>

Low power version of Intel Pentium3 CPU with MMX, SSE and SSE2 instruction set
support.  Used by Centrino notebooks.

=item I<pentium4, pentium4m>

Intel Pentium4 CPU with MMX, SSE and SSE2 instruction set support.

=item I<prescott>

Improved version of Intel Pentium4 CPU with MMX, SSE, SSE2 and SSE3 instruction
set support.

=item I<nocona>

Improved version of Intel Pentium4 CPU with 64-bit extensions, MMX, SSE,
SSE2 and SSE3 instruction set support.

=item I<core2>

Intel Core2 CPU with 64-bit extensions, MMX, SSE, SSE2, SSE3 and SSSE3
instruction set support.

=item I<corei7>

Intel Core i7 CPU with 64-bit extensions, MMX, SSE, SSE2, SSE3, SSSE3, SSE4.1
and SSE4.2 instruction set support.

=item I<corei7-avx>

Intel Core i7 CPU with 64-bit extensions, MMX, SSE, SSE2, SSE3, SSSE3,
SSE4.1, SSE4.2, AVX, AES and PCLMUL instruction set support.

=item I<core-avx-i>

Intel Core CPU with 64-bit extensions, MMX, SSE, SSE2, SSE3, SSSE3,
SSE4.1, SSE4.2, AVX, AES, PCLMUL, FSGSBASE, RDRND and F16C instruction
set support.

=item I<atom>

Intel Atom CPU with 64-bit extensions, MMX, SSE, SSE2, SSE3 and SSSE3
instruction set support.

=item I<k6>

AMD K6 CPU with MMX instruction set support.

=item I<k6-2, k6-3>

Improved versions of AMD K6 CPU with MMX and 3DNow! instruction set support.

=item I<athlon, athlon-tbird>

AMD Athlon CPU with MMX, 3dNOW!, enhanced 3DNow! and SSE prefetch instructions
support.

=item I<athlon-4, athlon-xp, athlon-mp>

Improved AMD Athlon CPU with MMX, 3DNow!, enhanced 3DNow! and full SSE
instruction set support.

=item I<k8, opteron, athlon64, athlon-fx>

AMD K8 core based CPUs with x86-64 instruction set support.  (This supersets
MMX, SSE, SSE2, 3DNow!, enhanced 3DNow! and 64-bit instruction set extensions.)

=item I<k8-sse3, opteron-sse3, athlon64-sse3>

Improved versions of k8, opteron and athlon64 with SSE3 instruction set support.

=item I<amdfam10, barcelona>

AMD Family 10h core based CPUs with x86-64 instruction set support.  (This
supersets MMX, SSE, SSE2, SSE3, SSE4A, 3DNow!, enhanced 3DNow!, ABM and 64-bit
instruction set extensions.)

=item I<winchip-c6>

IDT Winchip C6 CPU, dealt in same way as i486 with additional MMX instruction
set support.

=item I<winchip2>

IDT Winchip2 CPU, dealt in same way as i486 with additional MMX and 3DNow!
instruction set support.

=item I<c3>

Via C3 CPU with MMX and 3DNow! instruction set support.  (No scheduling is
implemented for this chip.)

=item I<c3-2>

Via C3-2 CPU with MMX and SSE instruction set support.  (No scheduling is
implemented for this chip.)

=item I<geode>

Embedded AMD CPU with MMX and 3DNow! instruction set support.

=back


While picking a specific I<cpu-type> will schedule things appropriately
for that particular chip, the compiler will not generate any code that
does not run on the i386 without the B<-march=>I<cpu-type> option
being used.


=item B<-march=>I<cpu-type>

Generate instructions for the machine type I<cpu-type>.  The choices
for I<cpu-type> are the same as for B<-mtune>.  Moreover,
specifying B<-march=>I<cpu-type> implies B<-mtune=>I<cpu-type>.


=item B<-mcpu=>I<cpu-type>

A deprecated synonym for B<-mtune>.


=item B<-mfpmath=>I<unit>

Generate floating point arithmetics for selected unit I<unit>.  The choices
for I<unit> are:


=over 4


=item B<387>

Use the standard 387 floating point coprocessor present majority of chips and
emulated otherwise.  Code compiled with this option will run almost everywhere.
The temporary results are computed in 80bit precision instead of precision
specified by the type resulting in slightly different results compared to most
of other chips.  See B<-ffloat-store> for more detailed description.

This is the default choice for i386 compiler.


=item B<sse>

Use scalar floating point instructions present in the SSE instruction set.
This instruction set is supported by Pentium3 and newer chips, in the AMD line
by Athlon-4, Athlon-xp and Athlon-mp chips.  The earlier version of SSE
instruction set supports only single precision arithmetics, thus the double and
extended precision arithmetics is still done using 387.  Later version, present
only in Pentium4 and the future AMD x86-64 chips supports double precision
arithmetics too.

For the i386 compiler, you need to use B<-march=>I<cpu-type>, B<-msse>
or B<-msse2> switches to enable SSE extensions and make this option
effective.  For the x86-64 compiler, these extensions are enabled by default.

The resulting code should be considerably faster in the majority of cases and avoid
the numerical instability problems of 387 code, but may break some existing
code that expects temporaries to be 80bit.

This is the default choice for the x86-64 compiler.


=item B<sse,387>


=item B<sse+387>


=item B<both>

Attempt to utilize both instruction sets at once.  This effectively double the
amount of available registers and on chips with separate execution units for
387 and SSE the execution resources too.  Use this option with care, as it is
still experimental, because the GCC register allocator does not model separate
functional units well resulting in instable performance.

=back



=item B<-masm=>I<dialect>

Output asm instructions using selected I<dialect>.  Supported
choices are B<intel> or B<att> (the default one).  Darwin does
not support B<intel>.


=item B<-mieee-fp>


=item B<-mno-ieee-fp>

Control whether or not the compiler uses IEEE floating point
comparisons.  These handle correctly the case where the result of a
comparison is unordered.


=item B<-msoft-float>

Generate output containing library calls for floating point.
B<Warning:> the requisite libraries are not part of GCC.
Normally the facilities of the machine's usual C compiler are used, but
this can't be done directly in cross-compilation.  You must make your
own arrangements to provide suitable library functions for
cross-compilation.

On machines where a function returns floating point results in the 80387
register stack, some floating point opcodes may be emitted even if
B<-msoft-float> is used.


=item B<-mno-fp-ret-in-387>

Do not use the FPU registers for return values of functions.

The usual calling convention has functions return values of types
C<float> and C<double> in an FPU register, even if there
is no FPU.  The idea is that the operating system should emulate
an FPU.

The option B<-mno-fp-ret-in-387> causes such values to be returned
in ordinary CPU registers instead.


=item B<-mno-fancy-math-387>

Some 387 emulators do not support the C<sin>, C<cos> and
C<sqrt> instructions for the 387.  Specify this option to avoid
generating those instructions.  This option is the default on FreeBSD,
OpenBSD and NetBSD.  This option is overridden when B<-march>
indicates that the target CPU will always have an FPU and so the
instruction will not need emulation.  As of revision 2.6.1, these
instructions are not generated unless you also use the
B<-funsafe-math-optimizations> switch.


=item B<-malign-double>


=item B<-mno-align-double>

Control whether GCC aligns C<double>, C<long double>, and
C<long long> variables on a two word boundary or a one word
boundary.  Aligning C<double> variables on a two word boundary will
produce code that runs somewhat faster on a B<Pentium> at the
expense of more memory.

On x86-64, B<-malign-double> is enabled by default.

B<Warning:> if you use the B<-malign-double> switch,
structures containing the above types will be aligned differently than
the published application binary interface specifications for the 386
and will not be binary compatible with structures in code compiled
without that switch.


=item B<-m96bit-long-double>


=item B<-m128bit-long-double>

These switches control the size of C<long double> type.  The i386
application binary interface specifies the size to be 96 bits,
so B<-m96bit-long-double> is the default in 32 bit mode.

Modern architectures (Pentium and newer) would prefer C<long double>
to be aligned to an 8 or 16 byte boundary.  In arrays or structures
conforming to the ABI, this would not be possible.  So specifying a
B<-m128bit-long-double> will align C<long double>
to a 16 byte boundary by padding the C<long double> with an additional
32 bit zero.

In the x86-64 compiler, B<-m128bit-long-double> is the default choice as
its ABI specifies that C<long double> is to be aligned on 16 byte boundary.

Notice that neither of these options enable any extra precision over the x87
standard of 80 bits for a C<long double>.

B<Warning:> if you override the default value for your target ABI, the
structures and arrays containing C<long double> variables will change
their size as well as function calling convention for function taking
C<long double> will be modified.  Hence they will not be binary
compatible with arrays or structures in code compiled without that switch.


=item B<-mlarge-data-threshold=>I<number>

When B<-mcmodel=medium> is specified, the data greater than
I<threshold> are placed in large data section.  This value must be the
same across all object linked into the binary and defaults to 65535.


=item B<-mrtd>

Use a different function-calling convention, in which functions that
take a fixed number of arguments return with the C<ret> I<num>
instruction, which pops their arguments while returning.  This saves one
instruction in the caller since there is no need to pop the arguments
there.

You can specify that an individual function is called with this calling
sequence with the function attribute B<stdcall>.  You can also
override the B<-mrtd> option by using the function attribute
B<cdecl>.  

B<Warning:> this calling convention is incompatible with the one
normally used on Unix, so you cannot use it if you need to call
libraries compiled with the Unix compiler.

Also, you must provide function prototypes for all functions that
take variable numbers of arguments (including C<printf>);
otherwise incorrect code will be generated for calls to those
functions.

In addition, seriously incorrect code will result if you call a
function with too many arguments.  (Normally, extra arguments are
harmlessly ignored.)


=item B<-mregparm=>I<num>

Control how many registers are used to pass integer arguments.  By
default, no registers are used to pass arguments, and at most 3
registers can be used.  You can control this behavior for a specific
function by using the function attribute B<regparm>.


B<Warning:> if you use this switch, and
I<num> is nonzero, then you must build all modules with the same
value, including any libraries.  This includes the system libraries and
startup modules.


=item B<-msseregparm>

Use SSE register passing conventions for float and double arguments
and return values.  You can control this behavior for a specific
function by using the function attribute B<sseregparm>.


B<Warning:> if you use this switch then you must build all
modules with the same value, including any libraries.  This includes
the system libraries and startup modules.


=item B<-mvect8-ret-in-mem>

Return 8-byte vectors in memory instead of MMX registers.  This is the
default on Solaris@tie{}8 and 9 and VxWorks to match the ABI of the Sun
Studio compilers until version 12.  Later compiler versions (starting
with Studio 12 Update@tie{}1) follow the ABI used by other x86 targets, which
is the default on Solaris@tie{}10 and later.  I<Only> use this option if
you need to remain compatible with existing code produced by those
previous compiler versions or older versions of GCC.


=item B<-mpc32>


=item B<-mpc64>


=item B<-mpc80>


Set 80387 floating-point precision to 32, 64 or 80 bits.  When B<-mpc32>
is specified, the significands of results of floating-point operations are
rounded to 24 bits (single precision); B<-mpc64> rounds the
significands of results of floating-point operations to 53 bits (double
precision) and B<-mpc80> rounds the significands of results of
floating-point operations to 64 bits (extended double precision), which is
the default.  When this option is used, floating-point operations in higher
precisions are not available to the programmer without setting the FPU
control word explicitly.

Setting the rounding of floating-point operations to less than the default
80 bits can speed some programs by 2% or more.  Note that some mathematical
libraries assume that extended precision (80 bit) floating-point operations
are enabled by default; routines in such libraries could suffer significant
loss of accuracy, typically through so-called "catastrophic cancellation",
when this option is used to set the precision to less than extended precision.


=item B<-mstackrealign>

Realign the stack at entry.  On the Intel x86, the B<-mstackrealign>
option will generate an alternate prologue and epilogue that realigns the
runtime stack if necessary.  This supports mixing legacy codes that keep
a 4-byte aligned stack with modern codes that keep a 16-byte stack for
SSE compatibility.  See also the attribute C<force_align_arg_pointer>,
applicable to individual functions.


=item B<-mpreferred-stack-boundary=>I<num>

Attempt to keep the stack boundary aligned to a 2 raised to I<num>
byte boundary.  If B<-mpreferred-stack-boundary> is not specified,
the default is 4 (16 bytes or 128 bits).


=item B<-mincoming-stack-boundary=>I<num>

Assume the incoming stack is aligned to a 2 raised to I<num> byte
boundary.  If B<-mincoming-stack-boundary> is not specified,
the one specified by B<-mpreferred-stack-boundary> will be used.

On Pentium and PentiumPro, C<double> and C<long double> values
should be aligned to an 8 byte boundary (see B<-malign-double>) or
suffer significant run time performance penalties.  On Pentium III, the
Streaming SIMD Extension (SSE) data type C<__m128> may not work
properly if it is not 16 byte aligned.

To ensure proper alignment of this values on the stack, the stack boundary
must be as aligned as that required by any value stored on the stack.
Further, every function must be generated such that it keeps the stack
aligned.  Thus calling a function compiled with a higher preferred
stack boundary from a function compiled with a lower preferred stack
boundary will most likely misalign the stack.  It is recommended that
libraries that use callbacks always use the default setting.

This extra alignment does consume extra stack space, and generally
increases code size.  Code that is sensitive to stack space usage, such
as embedded systems and operating system kernels, may want to reduce the
preferred alignment to B<-mpreferred-stack-boundary=2>.


=item B<-mmmx>


=item B<-mno-mmx>


=item B<-msse>


=item B<-mno-sse>


=item B<-msse2>


=item B<-mno-sse2>


=item B<-msse3>


=item B<-mno-sse3>


=item B<-mssse3>


=item B<-mno-ssse3>


=item B<-msse4.1>


=item B<-mno-sse4.1>


=item B<-msse4.2>


=item B<-mno-sse4.2>


=item B<-msse4>


=item B<-mno-sse4>


=item B<-mavx>


=item B<-mno-avx>


=item B<-maes>


=item B<-mno-aes>


=item B<-mpclmul>


=item B<-mno-pclmul>


=item B<-mfsgsbase>


=item B<-mno-fsgsbase>


=item B<-mrdrnd>


=item B<-mno-rdrnd>


=item B<-mf16c>


=item B<-mno-f16c>


=item B<-msse4a>


=item B<-mno-sse4a>


=item B<-mfma4>


=item B<-mno-fma4>


=item B<-mxop>


=item B<-mno-xop>


=item B<-mlwp>


=item B<-mno-lwp>


=item B<-m3dnow>


=item B<-mno-3dnow>


=item B<-mpopcnt>


=item B<-mno-popcnt>


=item B<-mabm>


=item B<-mno-abm>


=item B<-mbmi>


=item B<-mno-bmi>


=item B<-mtbm>


=item B<-mno-tbm>

These switches enable or disable the use of instructions in the MMX,
SSE, SSE2, SSE3, SSSE3, SSE4.1, AVX, AES, PCLMUL, FSGSBASE, RDRND,
F16C, SSE4A, FMA4, XOP, LWP, ABM, BMI, or 3DNow! extended instruction sets.
These extensions are also available as built-in functions: see
B<X86 Built-in Functions>, for details of the functions enabled and
disabled by these switches.

To have SSE/SSE2 instructions generated automatically from floating-point
code (as opposed to 387 instructions), see B<-mfpmath=sse>.

GCC depresses SSEx instructions when B<-mavx> is used. Instead, it
generates new AVX instructions or AVX equivalence for all SSEx instructions
when needed.

These options will enable GCC to use these extended instructions in
generated code, even without B<-mfpmath=sse>.  Applications which
perform runtime CPU detection must compile separate files for each
supported architecture, using the appropriate flags.  In particular,
the file containing the CPU detection code should be compiled without
these options.


=item B<-mfused-madd>


=item B<-mno-fused-madd>

Do (don't) generate code that uses the fused multiply/add or multiply/subtract
instructions.  The default is to use these instructions.


=item B<-mcld>

This option instructs GCC to emit a C<cld> instruction in the prologue
of functions that use string instructions.  String instructions depend on
the DF flag to select between autoincrement or autodecrement mode.  While the
ABI specifies the DF flag to be cleared on function entry, some operating
systems violate this specification by not clearing the DF flag in their
exception dispatchers.  The exception handler can be invoked with the DF flag
set which leads to wrong direction mode, when string instructions are used.
This option can be enabled by default on 32-bit x86 targets by configuring
GCC with the B<--enable-cld> configure option.  Generation of C<cld>
instructions can be suppressed with the B<-mno-cld> compiler option
in this case.


=item B<-mvzeroupper>

This option instructs GCC to emit a C<vzeroupper> instruction
before a transfer of control flow out of the function to minimize
AVX to SSE transition penalty as well as remove unnecessary zeroupper 
intrinsics.


=item B<-mcx16>

This option will enable GCC to use CMPXCHG16B instruction in generated code.
CMPXCHG16B allows for atomic operations on 128-bit double quadword (or oword)
data types.  This is useful for high resolution counters that could be updated
by multiple processors (or cores).  This instruction is generated as part of
atomic built-in functions: see B<Atomic Builtins> for details.


=item B<-msahf>

This option will enable GCC to use SAHF instruction in generated 64-bit code.
Early Intel CPUs with Intel 64 lacked LAHF and SAHF instructions supported
by AMD64 until introduction of Pentium 4 G1 step in December 2005.  LAHF and
SAHF are load and store instructions, respectively, for certain status flags.
In 64-bit mode, SAHF instruction is used to optimize C<fmod>, C<drem>
or C<remainder> built-in functions: see B<Other Builtins> for details.


=item B<-mmovbe>

This option will enable GCC to use movbe instruction to implement
C<__builtin_bswap32> and C<__builtin_bswap64>.


=item B<-mcrc32>

This option will enable built-in functions, C<__builtin_ia32_crc32qi>,
C<__builtin_ia32_crc32hi>. C<__builtin_ia32_crc32si> and
C<__builtin_ia32_crc32di> to generate the crc32 machine instruction.


=item B<-mrecip>

This option will enable GCC to use RCPSS and RSQRTSS instructions (and their
vectorized variants RCPPS and RSQRTPS) with an additional Newton-Raphson step
to increase precision instead of DIVSS and SQRTSS (and their vectorized
variants) for single precision floating point arguments.  These instructions
are generated only when B<-funsafe-math-optimizations> is enabled
together with B<-finite-math-only> and B<-fno-trapping-math>.
Note that while the throughput of the sequence is higher than the throughput
of the non-reciprocal instruction, the precision of the sequence can be
decreased by up to 2 ulp (i.e. the inverse of 1.0 equals 0.99999994).

Note that GCC implements 1.0f/sqrtf(x) in terms of RSQRTSS (or RSQRTPS)
already with B<-ffast-math> (or the above option combination), and
doesn't need B<-mrecip>.


=item B<-mveclibabi=>I<type>

Specifies the ABI type to use for vectorizing intrinsics using an
external library.  Supported types are C<svml> for the Intel short
vector math library and C<acml> for the AMD math core library style
of interfacing.  GCC will currently emit calls to C<vmldExp2>,
C<vmldLn2>, C<vmldLog102>, C<vmldLog102>, C<vmldPow2>,
C<vmldTanh2>, C<vmldTan2>, C<vmldAtan2>, C<vmldAtanh2>,
C<vmldCbrt2>, C<vmldSinh2>, C<vmldSin2>, C<vmldAsinh2>,
C<vmldAsin2>, C<vmldCosh2>, C<vmldCos2>, C<vmldAcosh2>,
C<vmldAcos2>, C<vmlsExp4>, C<vmlsLn4>, C<vmlsLog104>,
C<vmlsLog104>, C<vmlsPow4>, C<vmlsTanh4>, C<vmlsTan4>,
C<vmlsAtan4>, C<vmlsAtanh4>, C<vmlsCbrt4>, C<vmlsSinh4>,
C<vmlsSin4>, C<vmlsAsinh4>, C<vmlsAsin4>, C<vmlsCosh4>,
C<vmlsCos4>, C<vmlsAcosh4> and C<vmlsAcos4> for corresponding
function type when B<-mveclibabi=svml> is used and C<__vrd2_sin>,
C<__vrd2_cos>, C<__vrd2_exp>, C<__vrd2_log>, C<__vrd2_log2>,
C<__vrd2_log10>, C<__vrs4_sinf>, C<__vrs4_cosf>,
C<__vrs4_expf>, C<__vrs4_logf>, C<__vrs4_log2f>,
C<__vrs4_log10f> and C<__vrs4_powf> for corresponding function type
when B<-mveclibabi=acml> is used. Both B<-ftree-vectorize> and
B<-funsafe-math-optimizations> have to be enabled. A SVML or ACML ABI
compatible library will have to be specified at link time.


=item B<-mabi=>I<name>

Generate code for the specified calling convention.  Permissible values
are: B<sysv> for the ABI used on GNU/Linux and other systems and
B<ms> for the Microsoft ABI.  The default is to use the Microsoft
ABI when targeting Windows.  On all other systems, the default is the
SYSV ABI.  You can control this behavior for a specific function by
using the function attribute B<ms_abi>/B<sysv_abi>.



=item B<-mpush-args>


=item B<-mno-push-args>

Use PUSH operations to store outgoing parameters.  This method is shorter
and usually equally fast as method using SUB/MOV operations and is enabled
by default.  In some cases disabling it may improve performance because of
improved scheduling and reduced dependencies.


=item B<-maccumulate-outgoing-args>

If enabled, the maximum amount of space required for outgoing arguments will be
computed in the function prologue.  This is faster on most modern CPUs
because of reduced dependencies, improved scheduling and reduced stack usage
when preferred stack boundary is not equal to 2.  The drawback is a notable
increase in code size.  This switch implies B<-mno-push-args>.


=item B<-mthreads>

Support thread-safe exception handling on B<Mingw32>.  Code that relies
on thread-safe exception handling must compile and link all code with the
B<-mthreads> option.  When compiling, B<-mthreads> defines
B<-D_MT>; when linking, it links in a special thread helper library
B<-lmingwthrd> which cleans up per thread exception handling data.


=item B<-mno-align-stringops>

Do not align destination of inlined string operations.  This switch reduces
code size and improves performance in case the destination is already aligned,
but GCC doesn't know about it.


=item B<-minline-all-stringops>

By default GCC inlines string operations only when destination is known to be
aligned at least to 4 byte boundary.  This enables more inlining, increase code
size, but may improve performance of code that depends on fast memcpy, strlen
and memset for short lengths.


=item B<-minline-stringops-dynamically>

For string operation of unknown size, inline runtime checks so for small
blocks inline code is used, while for large blocks library call is used.


=item B<-mstringop-strategy=>I<alg>

Overwrite internal decision heuristic about particular algorithm to inline
string operation with.  The allowed values are C<rep_byte>,
C<rep_4byte>, C<rep_8byte> for expanding using i386 C<rep> prefix
of specified size, C<byte_loop>, C<loop>, C<unrolled_loop> for
expanding inline loop, C<libcall> for always expanding library call.


=item B<-momit-leaf-frame-pointer>

Don't keep the frame pointer in a register for leaf functions.  This
avoids the instructions to save, set up and restore frame pointers and
makes an extra register available in leaf functions.  The option
B<-fomit-frame-pointer> removes the frame pointer for all functions
which might make debugging harder.


=item B<-mtls-direct-seg-refs>


=item B<-mno-tls-direct-seg-refs>

Controls whether TLS variables may be accessed with offsets from the
TLS segment register (C<%gs> for 32-bit, C<%fs> for 64-bit),
or whether the thread base pointer must be added.  Whether or not this
is legal depends on the operating system, and whether it maps the
segment to cover the entire TLS area.

For systems that use GNU libc, the default is on.


=item B<-msse2avx>


=item B<-mno-sse2avx>

Specify that the assembler should encode SSE instructions with VEX
prefix.  The option B<-mavx> turns this on by default.


=item B<-mfentry>


=item B<-mno-fentry>

If profiling is active B<-pg> put the profiling
counter call before prologue.
Note: On x86 architectures the attribute C<ms_hook_prologue>
isn't possible at the moment for B<-mfentry> and B<-pg>.


=item B<-m8bit-idiv>


=item B<-mno-8bit-idiv>

On some processors, like Intel Atom, 8bit unsigned integer divide is
much faster than 32bit/64bit integer divide.  This option will generate a
runt-time check.  If both dividend and divisor are within range of 0
to 255, 8bit unsigned integer divide will be used instead of
32bit/64bit integer divide.


=item B<-mavx256-split-unaligned-load>


=item B<-mavx256-split-unaligned-store>

Split 32-byte AVX unaligned load and store.


=back


These B<-m> switches are supported in addition to the above
on AMD x86-64 processors in 64-bit environments.


=over 4


=item B<-m32>


=item B<-m64>

Generate code for a 32-bit or 64-bit environment.
The 32-bit environment sets int, long and pointer to 32 bits and
generates code that runs on any i386 system.
The 64-bit environment sets int to 32 bits and long and pointer
to 64 bits and generates code for AMD's x86-64 architecture. For
darwin only the -m64 option turns off the B<-fno-pic> and
B<-mdynamic-no-pic> options.


=item B<-mno-red-zone>

Do not use a so called red zone for x86-64 code.  The red zone is mandated
by the x86-64 ABI, it is a 128-byte area beyond the location of the
stack pointer that will not be modified by signal or interrupt handlers
and therefore can be used for temporary data without adjusting the stack
pointer.  The flag B<-mno-red-zone> disables this red zone.


=item B<-mcmodel=small>

Generate code for the small code model: the program and its symbols must
be linked in the lower 2 GB of the address space.  Pointers are 64 bits.
Programs can be statically or dynamically linked.  This is the default
code model.


=item B<-mcmodel=kernel>

Generate code for the kernel code model.  The kernel runs in the
negative 2 GB of the address space.
This model has to be used for Linux kernel code.


=item B<-mcmodel=medium>

Generate code for the medium model: The program is linked in the lower 2
GB of the address space.  Small symbols are also placed there.  Symbols
with sizes larger than B<-mlarge-data-threshold> are put into
large data or bss sections and can be located above 2GB.  Programs can
be statically or dynamically linked.


=item B<-mcmodel=large>

Generate code for the large model: This model makes no assumptions
about addresses and sizes of sections.

=back



=head3 i386 and x86-64 Windows Options


These additional options are available for Windows targets:


=over 4


=item B<-mconsole>

This option is available for Cygwin and MinGW targets.  It
specifies that a console application is to be generated, by
instructing the linker to set the PE header subsystem type
required for console applications.
This is the default behavior for Cygwin and MinGW targets.


=item B<-mdll>

This option is available for Cygwin and MinGW targets.  It
specifies that a DLL - a dynamic link library - is to be
generated, enabling the selection of the required runtime
startup object and entry point.


=item B<-mnop-fun-dllimport>

This option is available for Cygwin and MinGW targets.  It
specifies that the dllimport attribute should be ignored.


=item B<-mthread>

This option is available for MinGW targets. It specifies
that MinGW-specific thread support is to be used.


=item B<-municode>

This option is available for mingw-w64 targets.  It specifies
that the UNICODE macro is getting pre-defined and that the
unicode capable runtime startup code is chosen.


=item B<-mwin32>

This option is available for Cygwin and MinGW targets.  It
specifies that the typical Windows pre-defined macros are to
be set in the pre-processor, but does not influence the choice
of runtime library/startup code.


=item B<-mwindows>

This option is available for Cygwin and MinGW targets.  It
specifies that a GUI application is to be generated by
instructing the linker to set the PE header subsystem type
appropriately.


=item B<-fno-set-stack-executable>

This option is available for MinGW targets. It specifies that
the executable flag for stack used by nested functions isn't
set. This is necessary for binaries running in kernel mode of
Windows, as there the user32 API, which is used to set executable
privileges, isn't available.


=item B<-mpe-aligned-commons>

This option is available for Cygwin and MinGW targets.  It
specifies that the GNU extension to the PE file format that
permits the correct alignment of COMMON variables should be
used when generating code.  It will be enabled by default if
GCC detects that the target assembler found during configuration
supports the feature.

=back


See also under B<i386 and x86-64 Options> for standard options.


=head3 IA-64 Options


These are the B<-m> options defined for the Intel IA-64 architecture.


=over 4


=item B<-mbig-endian>

Generate code for a big endian target.  This is the default for HP-UX.


=item B<-mlittle-endian>

Generate code for a little endian target.  This is the default for AIX5
and GNU/Linux.


=item B<-mgnu-as>


=item B<-mno-gnu-as>

Generate (or don't) code for the GNU assembler.  This is the default.


=item B<-mgnu-ld>


=item B<-mno-gnu-ld>

Generate (or don't) code for the GNU linker.  This is the default.


=item B<-mno-pic>

Generate code that does not use a global pointer register.  The result
is not position independent code, and violates the IA-64 ABI.


=item B<-mvolatile-asm-stop>


=item B<-mno-volatile-asm-stop>

Generate (or don't) a stop bit immediately before and after volatile asm
statements.


=item B<-mregister-names>


=item B<-mno-register-names>

Generate (or don't) B<in>, B<loc>, and B<out> register names for
the stacked registers.  This may make assembler output more readable.


=item B<-mno-sdata>


=item B<-msdata>

Disable (or enable) optimizations that use the small data section.  This may
be useful for working around optimizer bugs.


=item B<-mconstant-gp>

Generate code that uses a single constant global pointer value.  This is
useful when compiling kernel code.


=item B<-mauto-pic>

Generate code that is self-relocatable.  This implies B<-mconstant-gp>.
This is useful when compiling firmware code.


=item B<-minline-float-divide-min-latency>

Generate code for inline divides of floating point values
using the minimum latency algorithm.


=item B<-minline-float-divide-max-throughput>

Generate code for inline divides of floating point values
using the maximum throughput algorithm.


=item B<-mno-inline-float-divide>

Do not generate inline code for divides of floating point values.


=item B<-minline-int-divide-min-latency>

Generate code for inline divides of integer values
using the minimum latency algorithm.


=item B<-minline-int-divide-max-throughput>

Generate code for inline divides of integer values
using the maximum throughput algorithm.


=item B<-mno-inline-int-divide>

Do not generate inline code for divides of integer values.


=item B<-minline-sqrt-min-latency>

Generate code for inline square roots
using the minimum latency algorithm.


=item B<-minline-sqrt-max-throughput>

Generate code for inline square roots
using the maximum throughput algorithm.


=item B<-mno-inline-sqrt>

Do not generate inline code for sqrt.


=item B<-mfused-madd>


=item B<-mno-fused-madd>

Do (don't) generate code that uses the fused multiply/add or multiply/subtract
instructions.  The default is to use these instructions.


=item B<-mno-dwarf2-asm>


=item B<-mdwarf2-asm>

Don't (or do) generate assembler code for the DWARF2 line number debugging
info.  This may be useful when not using the GNU assembler.


=item B<-mearly-stop-bits>


=item B<-mno-early-stop-bits>

Allow stop bits to be placed earlier than immediately preceding the
instruction that triggered the stop bit.  This can improve instruction
scheduling, but does not always do so.


=item B<-mfixed-range=>I<register-range>

Generate code treating the given register range as fixed registers.
A fixed register is one that the register allocator can not use.  This is
useful when compiling kernel code.  A register range is specified as
two registers separated by a dash.  Multiple register ranges can be
specified separated by a comma.


=item B<-mtls-size=>I<tls-size>

Specify bit size of immediate TLS offsets.  Valid values are 14, 22, and
64.


=item B<-mtune=>I<cpu-type>

Tune the instruction scheduling for a particular CPU, Valid values are
itanium, itanium1, merced, itanium2, and mckinley.


=item B<-milp32>


=item B<-mlp64>

Generate code for a 32-bit or 64-bit environment.
The 32-bit environment sets int, long and pointer to 32 bits.
The 64-bit environment sets int to 32 bits and long and pointer
to 64 bits.  These are HP-UX specific flags.


=item B<-mno-sched-br-data-spec>


=item B<-msched-br-data-spec>

(Dis/En)able data speculative scheduling before reload.
This will result in generation of the ld.a instructions and
the corresponding check instructions (ld.c / chk.a).
The default is 'disable'.


=item B<-msched-ar-data-spec>


=item B<-mno-sched-ar-data-spec>

(En/Dis)able data speculative scheduling after reload.
This will result in generation of the ld.a instructions and
the corresponding check instructions (ld.c / chk.a).
The default is 'enable'.


=item B<-mno-sched-control-spec>


=item B<-msched-control-spec>

(Dis/En)able control speculative scheduling.  This feature is
available only during region scheduling (i.e. before reload).
This will result in generation of the ld.s instructions and
the corresponding check instructions chk.s .
The default is 'disable'.


=item B<-msched-br-in-data-spec>


=item B<-mno-sched-br-in-data-spec>

(En/Dis)able speculative scheduling of the instructions that
are dependent on the data speculative loads before reload.
This is effective only with B<-msched-br-data-spec> enabled.
The default is 'enable'.


=item B<-msched-ar-in-data-spec>


=item B<-mno-sched-ar-in-data-spec>

(En/Dis)able speculative scheduling of the instructions that
are dependent on the data speculative loads after reload.
This is effective only with B<-msched-ar-data-spec> enabled.
The default is 'enable'.


=item B<-msched-in-control-spec>


=item B<-mno-sched-in-control-spec>

(En/Dis)able speculative scheduling of the instructions that
are dependent on the control speculative loads.
This is effective only with B<-msched-control-spec> enabled.
The default is 'enable'.


=item B<-mno-sched-prefer-non-data-spec-insns>


=item B<-msched-prefer-non-data-spec-insns>

If enabled, data speculative instructions will be chosen for schedule
only if there are no other choices at the moment.  This will make
the use of the data speculation much more conservative.
The default is 'disable'.


=item B<-mno-sched-prefer-non-control-spec-insns>


=item B<-msched-prefer-non-control-spec-insns>

If enabled, control speculative instructions will be chosen for schedule
only if there are no other choices at the moment.  This will make
the use of the control speculation much more conservative.
The default is 'disable'.


=item B<-mno-sched-count-spec-in-critical-path>


=item B<-msched-count-spec-in-critical-path>

If enabled, speculative dependencies will be considered during
computation of the instructions priorities.  This will make the use of the
speculation a bit more conservative.
The default is 'disable'.


=item B<-msched-spec-ldc>

Use a simple data speculation check.  This option is on by default.


=item B<-msched-control-spec-ldc>

Use a simple check for control speculation.  This option is on by default.


=item B<-msched-stop-bits-after-every-cycle>

Place a stop bit after every cycle when scheduling.  This option is on
by default.


=item B<-msched-fp-mem-deps-zero-cost>

Assume that floating-point stores and loads are not likely to cause a conflict
when placed into the same instruction group.  This option is disabled by
default.


=item B<-msel-sched-dont-check-control-spec>

Generate checks for control speculation in selective scheduling.
This flag is disabled by default.


=item B<-msched-max-memory-insns=>I<max-insns>

Limit on the number of memory insns per instruction group, giving lower
priority to subsequent memory insns attempting to schedule in the same
instruction group. Frequently useful to prevent cache bank conflicts.
The default value is 1.


=item B<-msched-max-memory-insns-hard-limit>

Disallow more than `msched-max-memory-insns' in instruction group.
Otherwise, limit is `soft' meaning that we would prefer non-memory operations
when limit is reached but may still schedule memory operations.


=back



=head3 IA-64/VMS Options


These B<-m> options are defined for the IA-64/VMS implementations:


=over 4


=item B<-mvms-return-codes>

Return VMS condition codes from main. The default is to return POSIX
style condition (e.g. error) codes.


=item B<-mdebug-main=>I<prefix>

Flag the first routine whose name starts with I<prefix> as the main
routine for the debugger.


=item B<-mmalloc64>

Default to 64bit memory allocation routines.

=back



=head3 LM32 Options


These B<-m> options are defined for the Lattice Mico32 architecture:


=over 4


=item B<-mbarrel-shift-enabled>

Enable barrel-shift instructions.


=item B<-mdivide-enabled>

Enable divide and modulus instructions.


=item B<-mmultiply-enabled>

Enable multiply instructions.


=item B<-msign-extend-enabled>

Enable sign extend instructions.


=item B<-muser-enabled>

Enable user-defined instructions.


=back



=head3 M32C Options



=over 4


=item B<-mcpu=>I<name>

Select the CPU for which code is generated.  I<name> may be one of
B<r8c> for the R8C/Tiny series, B<m16c> for the M16C (up to
/60) series, B<m32cm> for the M16C/80 series, or B<m32c> for
the M32C/80 series.


=item B<-msim>

Specifies that the program will be run on the simulator.  This causes
an alternate runtime library to be linked in which supports, for
example, file I/O.  You must not use this option when generating
programs that will run on real hardware; you must provide your own
runtime library for whatever I/O functions are needed.


=item B<-memregs=>I<number>

Specifies the number of memory-based pseudo-registers GCC will use
during code generation.  These pseudo-registers will be used like real
registers, so there is a tradeoff between GCC's ability to fit the
code into available registers, and the performance penalty of using
memory instead of registers.  Note that all modules in a program must
be compiled with the same value for this option.  Because of that, you
must not use this option with the default runtime libraries gcc
builds.


=back



=head3 M32R/D Options


These B<-m> options are defined for Renesas M32R/D architectures:


=over 4


=item B<-m32r2>

Generate code for the M32R/2.


=item B<-m32rx>

Generate code for the M32R/X.


=item B<-m32r>

Generate code for the M32R.  This is the default.


=item B<-mmodel=small>

Assume all objects live in the lower 16MB of memory (so that their addresses
can be loaded with the C<ld24> instruction), and assume all subroutines
are reachable with the C<bl> instruction.
This is the default.

The addressability of a particular object can be set with the
C<model> attribute.


=item B<-mmodel=medium>

Assume objects may be anywhere in the 32-bit address space (the compiler
will generate C<seth/add3> instructions to load their addresses), and
assume all subroutines are reachable with the C<bl> instruction.


=item B<-mmodel=large>

Assume objects may be anywhere in the 32-bit address space (the compiler
will generate C<seth/add3> instructions to load their addresses), and
assume subroutines may not be reachable with the C<bl> instruction
(the compiler will generate the much slower C<seth/add3/jl>
instruction sequence).


=item B<-msdata=none>

Disable use of the small data area.  Variables will be put into
one of B<.data>, B<bss>, or B<.rodata> (unless the
C<section> attribute has been specified).
This is the default.

The small data area consists of sections B<.sdata> and B<.sbss>.
Objects may be explicitly put in the small data area with the
C<section> attribute using one of these sections.


=item B<-msdata=sdata>

Put small global and static data in the small data area, but do not
generate special code to reference them.


=item B<-msdata=use>

Put small global and static data in the small data area, and generate
special instructions to reference them.


=item B<-G> I<num>

Put global and static objects less than or equal to I<num> bytes
into the small data or bss sections instead of the normal data or bss
sections.  The default value of I<num> is 8.
The B<-msdata> option must be set to one of B<sdata> or B<use>
for this option to have any effect.

All modules should be compiled with the same B<-G> I<num> value.
Compiling with different values of I<num> may or may not work; if it
doesn't the linker will give an error message---incorrect code will not be
generated.


=item B<-mdebug>

Makes the M32R specific code in the compiler display some statistics
that might help in debugging programs.


=item B<-malign-loops>

Align all loops to a 32-byte boundary.


=item B<-mno-align-loops>

Do not enforce a 32-byte alignment for loops.  This is the default.


=item B<-missue-rate=>I<number>

Issue I<number> instructions per cycle.  I<number> can only be 1
or 2.


=item B<-mbranch-cost=>I<number>

I<number> can only be 1 or 2.  If it is 1 then branches will be
preferred over conditional code, if it is 2, then the opposite will
apply.


=item B<-mflush-trap=>I<number>

Specifies the trap number to use to flush the cache.  The default is
12.  Valid numbers are between 0 and 15 inclusive.


=item B<-mno-flush-trap>

Specifies that the cache cannot be flushed by using a trap.


=item B<-mflush-func=>I<name>

Specifies the name of the operating system function to call to flush
the cache.  The default is I<_flush_cache>, but a function call
will only be used if a trap is not available.


=item B<-mno-flush-func>

Indicates that there is no OS function for flushing the cache.


=back



=head3 M680x0 Options


These are the B<-m> options defined for M680x0 and ColdFire processors.
The default settings depend on which architecture was selected when
the compiler was configured; the defaults for the most common choices
are given below.


=over 4


=item B<-march=>I<arch>

Generate code for a specific M680x0 or ColdFire instruction set
architecture.  Permissible values of I<arch> for M680x0
architectures are: B<68000>, B<68010>, B<68020>,
B<68030>, B<68040>, B<68060> and B<cpu32>.  ColdFire
architectures are selected according to Freescale's ISA classification
and the permissible values are: B<isaa>, B<isaaplus>,
B<isab> and B<isac>.

gcc defines a macro B<__mcf>I<arch>B<__> whenever it is generating
code for a ColdFire target.  The I<arch> in this macro is one of the
B<-march> arguments given above.

When used together, B<-march> and B<-mtune> select code
that runs on a family of similar processors but that is optimized
for a particular microarchitecture.


=item B<-mcpu=>I<cpu>

Generate code for a specific M680x0 or ColdFire processor.
The M680x0 I<cpu>s are: B<68000>, B<68010>, B<68020>,
B<68030>, B<68040>, B<68060>, B<68302>, B<68332>
and B<cpu32>.  The ColdFire I<cpu>s are given by the table
below, which also classifies the CPUs into families:


=over 4


=item Family : B<-mcpu> arguments


=item B<51> : B<51> B<51ac> B<51cn> B<51em> B<51qe>


=item B<5206> : B<5202> B<5204> B<5206>


=item B<5206e> : B<5206e>


=item B<5208> : B<5207> B<5208>


=item B<5211a> : B<5210a> B<5211a>


=item B<5213> : B<5211> B<5212> B<5213>


=item B<5216> : B<5214> B<5216>


=item B<52235> : B<52230> B<52231> B<52232> B<52233> B<52234> B<52235>


=item B<5225> : B<5224> B<5225>


=item B<52259> : B<52252> B<52254> B<52255> B<52256> B<52258> B<52259>


=item B<5235> : B<5232> B<5233> B<5234> B<5235> B<523x>


=item B<5249> : B<5249>


=item B<5250> : B<5250>


=item B<5271> : B<5270> B<5271>


=item B<5272> : B<5272>


=item B<5275> : B<5274> B<5275>


=item B<5282> : B<5280> B<5281> B<5282> B<528x>


=item B<53017> : B<53011> B<53012> B<53013> B<53014> B<53015> B<53016> B<53017>


=item B<5307> : B<5307>


=item B<5329> : B<5327> B<5328> B<5329> B<532x>


=item B<5373> : B<5372> B<5373> B<537x>


=item B<5407> : B<5407>


=item B<5475> : B<5470> B<5471> B<5472> B<5473> B<5474> B<5475> B<547x> B<5480> B<5481> B<5482> B<5483> B<5484> B<5485>


=back


B<-mcpu=>I<cpu> overrides B<-march=>I<arch> if
I<arch> is compatible with I<cpu>.  Other combinations of
B<-mcpu> and B<-march> are rejected.

gcc defines the macro B<__mcf_cpu_>I<cpu> when ColdFire target
I<cpu> is selected.  It also defines B<__mcf_family_>I<family>,
where the value of I<family> is given by the table above.


=item B<-mtune=>I<tune>

Tune the code for a particular microarchitecture, within the
constraints set by B<-march> and B<-mcpu>.
The M680x0 microarchitectures are: B<68000>, B<68010>,
B<68020>, B<68030>, B<68040>, B<68060>
and B<cpu32>.  The ColdFire microarchitectures
are: B<cfv1>, B<cfv2>, B<cfv3>, B<cfv4> and B<cfv4e>.

You can also use B<-mtune=68020-40> for code that needs
to run relatively well on 68020, 68030 and 68040 targets.
B<-mtune=68020-60> is similar but includes 68060 targets
as well.  These two options select the same tuning decisions as
B<-m68020-40> and B<-m68020-60> respectively.

gcc defines the macros B<__mc>I<arch> and B<__mc>I<arch>B<__>
when tuning for 680x0 architecture I<arch>.  It also defines
B<mc>I<arch> unless either B<-ansi> or a non-GNU B<-std>
option is used.  If gcc is tuning for a range of architectures,
as selected by B<-mtune=68020-40> or B<-mtune=68020-60>,
it defines the macros for every architecture in the range.

gcc also defines the macro B<__m>I<uarch>B<__> when tuning for
ColdFire microarchitecture I<uarch>, where I<uarch> is one
of the arguments given above.


=item B<-m68000>


=item B<-mc68000>

Generate output for a 68000.  This is the default
when the compiler is configured for 68000-based systems.
It is equivalent to B<-march=68000>.

Use this option for microcontrollers with a 68000 or EC000 core,
including the 68008, 68302, 68306, 68307, 68322, 68328 and 68356.


=item B<-m68010>

Generate output for a 68010.  This is the default
when the compiler is configured for 68010-based systems.
It is equivalent to B<-march=68010>.


=item B<-m68020>


=item B<-mc68020>

Generate output for a 68020.  This is the default
when the compiler is configured for 68020-based systems.
It is equivalent to B<-march=68020>.


=item B<-m68030>

Generate output for a 68030.  This is the default when the compiler is
configured for 68030-based systems.  It is equivalent to
B<-march=68030>.


=item B<-m68040>

Generate output for a 68040.  This is the default when the compiler is
configured for 68040-based systems.  It is equivalent to
B<-march=68040>.

This option inhibits the use of 68881/68882 instructions that have to be
emulated by software on the 68040.  Use this option if your 68040 does not
have code to emulate those instructions.


=item B<-m68060>

Generate output for a 68060.  This is the default when the compiler is
configured for 68060-based systems.  It is equivalent to
B<-march=68060>.

This option inhibits the use of 68020 and 68881/68882 instructions that
have to be emulated by software on the 68060.  Use this option if your 68060
does not have code to emulate those instructions.


=item B<-mcpu32>

Generate output for a CPU32.  This is the default
when the compiler is configured for CPU32-based systems.
It is equivalent to B<-march=cpu32>.

Use this option for microcontrollers with a
CPU32 or CPU32+ core, including the 68330, 68331, 68332, 68333, 68334,
68336, 68340, 68341, 68349 and 68360.


=item B<-m5200>

Generate output for a 520X ColdFire CPU.  This is the default
when the compiler is configured for 520X-based systems.
It is equivalent to B<-mcpu=5206>, and is now deprecated
in favor of that option.

Use this option for microcontroller with a 5200 core, including
the MCF5202, MCF5203, MCF5204 and MCF5206.


=item B<-m5206e>

Generate output for a 5206e ColdFire CPU.  The option is now
deprecated in favor of the equivalent B<-mcpu=5206e>.


=item B<-m528x>

Generate output for a member of the ColdFire 528X family.
The option is now deprecated in favor of the equivalent
B<-mcpu=528x>.


=item B<-m5307>

Generate output for a ColdFire 5307 CPU.  The option is now deprecated
in favor of the equivalent B<-mcpu=5307>.


=item B<-m5407>

Generate output for a ColdFire 5407 CPU.  The option is now deprecated
in favor of the equivalent B<-mcpu=5407>.


=item B<-mcfv4e>

Generate output for a ColdFire V4e family CPU (e.g. 547x/548x).
This includes use of hardware floating point instructions.
The option is equivalent to B<-mcpu=547x>, and is now
deprecated in favor of that option.


=item B<-m68020-40>

Generate output for a 68040, without using any of the new instructions.
This results in code which can run relatively efficiently on either a
68020/68881 or a 68030 or a 68040.  The generated code does use the
68881 instructions that are emulated on the 68040.

The option is equivalent to B<-march=68020> B<-mtune=68020-40>.


=item B<-m68020-60>

Generate output for a 68060, without using any of the new instructions.
This results in code which can run relatively efficiently on either a
68020/68881 or a 68030 or a 68040.  The generated code does use the
68881 instructions that are emulated on the 68060.

The option is equivalent to B<-march=68020> B<-mtune=68020-60>.


=item B<-mhard-float>


=item B<-m68881>

Generate floating-point instructions.  This is the default for 68020
and above, and for ColdFire devices that have an FPU.  It defines the
macro B<__HAVE_68881__> on M680x0 targets and B<__mcffpu__>
on ColdFire targets.


=item B<-msoft-float>

Do not generate floating-point instructions; use library calls instead.
This is the default for 68000, 68010, and 68832 targets.  It is also
the default for ColdFire devices that have no FPU.


=item B<-mdiv>


=item B<-mno-div>

Generate (do not generate) ColdFire hardware divide and remainder
instructions.  If B<-march> is used without B<-mcpu>,
the default is "on" for ColdFire architectures and "off" for M680x0
architectures.  Otherwise, the default is taken from the target CPU
(either the default CPU, or the one specified by B<-mcpu>).  For
example, the default is "off" for B<-mcpu=5206> and "on" for
B<-mcpu=5206e>.

gcc defines the macro B<__mcfhwdiv__> when this option is enabled.


=item B<-mshort>

Consider type C<int> to be 16 bits wide, like C<short int>.
Additionally, parameters passed on the stack are also aligned to a
16-bit boundary even on targets whose API mandates promotion to 32-bit.


=item B<-mno-short>

Do not consider type C<int> to be 16 bits wide.  This is the default.


=item B<-mnobitfield>


=item B<-mno-bitfield>

Do not use the bit-field instructions.  The B<-m68000>, B<-mcpu32>
and B<-m5200> options imply S<B<-mnobitfield>>.


=item B<-mbitfield>

Do use the bit-field instructions.  The B<-m68020> option implies
B<-mbitfield>.  This is the default if you use a configuration
designed for a 68020.


=item B<-mrtd>

Use a different function-calling convention, in which functions
that take a fixed number of arguments return with the C<rtd>
instruction, which pops their arguments while returning.  This
saves one instruction in the caller since there is no need to pop
the arguments there.

This calling convention is incompatible with the one normally
used on Unix, so you cannot use it if you need to call libraries
compiled with the Unix compiler.

Also, you must provide function prototypes for all functions that
take variable numbers of arguments (including C<printf>);
otherwise incorrect code will be generated for calls to those
functions.

In addition, seriously incorrect code will result if you call a
function with too many arguments.  (Normally, extra arguments are
harmlessly ignored.)

The C<rtd> instruction is supported by the 68010, 68020, 68030,
68040, 68060 and CPU32 processors, but not by the 68000 or 5200.


=item B<-mno-rtd>

Do not use the calling conventions selected by B<-mrtd>.
This is the default.


=item B<-malign-int>


=item B<-mno-align-int>

Control whether GCC aligns C<int>, C<long>, C<long long>,
C<float>, C<double>, and C<long double> variables on a 32-bit
boundary (B<-malign-int>) or a 16-bit boundary (B<-mno-align-int>).
Aligning variables on 32-bit boundaries produces code that runs somewhat
faster on processors with 32-bit busses at the expense of more memory.

B<Warning:> if you use the B<-malign-int> switch, GCC will
align structures containing the above types  differently than
most published application binary interface specifications for the m68k.


=item B<-mpcrel>

Use the pc-relative addressing mode of the 68000 directly, instead of
using a global offset table.  At present, this option implies B<-fpic>,
allowing at most a 16-bit offset for pc-relative addressing.  B<-fPIC> is
not presently supported with B<-mpcrel>, though this could be supported for
68020 and higher processors.


=item B<-mno-strict-align>


=item B<-mstrict-align>

Do not (do) assume that unaligned memory references will be handled by
the system.


=item B<-msep-data>

Generate code that allows the data segment to be located in a different
area of memory from the text segment.  This allows for execute in place in
an environment without virtual memory management.  This option implies
B<-fPIC>.


=item B<-mno-sep-data>

Generate code that assumes that the data segment follows the text segment.
This is the default.


=item B<-mid-shared-library>

Generate code that supports shared libraries via the library ID method.
This allows for execute in place and shared libraries in an environment
without virtual memory management.  This option implies B<-fPIC>.


=item B<-mno-id-shared-library>

Generate code that doesn't assume ID based shared libraries are being used.
This is the default.


=item B<-mshared-library-id=n>

Specified the identification number of the ID based shared library being
compiled.  Specifying a value of 0 will generate more compact code, specifying
other values will force the allocation of that number to the current
library but is no more space or time efficient than omitting this option.


=item B<-mxgot>


=item B<-mno-xgot>

When generating position-independent code for ColdFire, generate code
that works if the GOT has more than 8192 entries.  This code is
larger and slower than code generated without this option.  On M680x0
processors, this option is not needed; B<-fPIC> suffices.

GCC normally uses a single instruction to load values from the GOT.
While this is relatively efficient, it only works if the GOT
is smaller than about 64k.  Anything larger causes the linker
to report an error such as:

	
	relocation truncated to fit: R_68K_GOT16O foobar


If this happens, you should recompile your code with B<-mxgot>.
It should then work with very large GOTs.  However, code generated with
B<-mxgot> is less efficient, since it takes 4 instructions to fetch
the value of a global symbol.

Note that some linkers, including newer versions of the GNU linker,
can create multiple GOTs and sort GOT entries.  If you have such a linker,
you should only need to use B<-mxgot> when compiling a single
object file that accesses more than 8192 GOT entries.  Very few do.

These options have no effect unless GCC is generating
position-independent code.


=back



=head3 M68hc1x Options


These are the B<-m> options defined for the 68hc11 and 68hc12
microcontrollers.  The default values for these options depends on
which style of microcontroller was selected when the compiler was configured;
the defaults for the most common choices are given below.


=over 4


=item B<-m6811>


=item B<-m68hc11>

Generate output for a 68HC11.  This is the default
when the compiler is configured for 68HC11-based systems.


=item B<-m6812>


=item B<-m68hc12>

Generate output for a 68HC12.  This is the default
when the compiler is configured for 68HC12-based systems.


=item B<-m68S12>


=item B<-m68hcs12>

Generate output for a 68HCS12.


=item B<-mauto-incdec>

Enable the use of 68HC12 pre and post auto-increment and auto-decrement
addressing modes.


=item B<-minmax>


=item B<-mnominmax>

Enable the use of 68HC12 min and max instructions.


=item B<-mlong-calls>


=item B<-mno-long-calls>

Treat all calls as being far away (near).  If calls are assumed to be
far away, the compiler will use the C<call> instruction to
call a function and the C<rtc> instruction for returning.


=item B<-mshort>

Consider type C<int> to be 16 bits wide, like C<short int>.


=item B<-msoft-reg-count=>I<count>

Specify the number of pseudo-soft registers which are used for the
code generation.  The maximum number is 32.  Using more pseudo-soft
register may or may not result in better code depending on the program.
The default is 4 for 68HC11 and 2 for 68HC12.


=back



=head3 MCore Options


These are the B<-m> options defined for the Motorola M*Core
processors.


=over 4



=item B<-mhardlit>


=item B<-mno-hardlit>

Inline constants into the code stream if it can be done in two
instructions or less.


=item B<-mdiv>


=item B<-mno-div>

Use the divide instruction.  (Enabled by default).


=item B<-mrelax-immediate>


=item B<-mno-relax-immediate>

Allow arbitrary sized immediates in bit operations.


=item B<-mwide-bitfields>


=item B<-mno-wide-bitfields>

Always treat bit-fields as int-sized.


=item B<-m4byte-functions>


=item B<-mno-4byte-functions>

Force all functions to be aligned to a four byte boundary.


=item B<-mcallgraph-data>


=item B<-mno-callgraph-data>

Emit callgraph information.


=item B<-mslow-bytes>


=item B<-mno-slow-bytes>

Prefer word access when reading byte quantities.


=item B<-mlittle-endian>


=item B<-mbig-endian>

Generate code for a little endian target.


=item B<-m210>


=item B<-m340>

Generate code for the 210 processor.


=item B<-mno-lsim>

Assume that run-time support has been provided and so omit the
simulator library (F<libsim.a)> from the linker command line.


=item B<-mstack-increment=>I<size>

Set the maximum amount for a single stack increment operation.  Large
values can increase the speed of programs which contain functions
that need a large amount of stack space, but they can also trigger a
segmentation fault if the stack is extended too much.  The default
value is 0x1000.


=back



=head3 MeP Options



=over 4



=item B<-mabsdiff>

Enables the C<abs> instruction, which is the absolute difference
between two registers.


=item B<-mall-opts>

Enables all the optional instructions - average, multiply, divide, bit
operations, leading zero, absolute difference, min/max, clip, and
saturation.



=item B<-maverage>

Enables the C<ave> instruction, which computes the average of two
registers.


=item B<-mbased=>I<n>

Variables of size I<n> bytes or smaller will be placed in the
C<.based> section by default.  Based variables use the C<$tp>
register as a base register, and there is a 128 byte limit to the
C<.based> section.


=item B<-mbitops>

Enables the bit operation instructions - bit test (C<btstm>), set
(C<bsetm>), clear (C<bclrm>), invert (C<bnotm>), and
test-and-set (C<tas>).


=item B<-mc=>I<name>

Selects which section constant data will be placed in.  I<name> may
be C<tiny>, C<near>, or C<far>.


=item B<-mclip>

Enables the C<clip> instruction.  Note that C<-mclip> is not
useful unless you also provide C<-mminmax>.


=item B<-mconfig=>I<name>

Selects one of the build-in core configurations.  Each MeP chip has
one or more modules in it; each module has a core CPU and a variety of
coprocessors, optional instructions, and peripherals.  The
C<MeP-Integrator> tool, not part of GCC, provides these
configurations through this option; using this option is the same as
using all the corresponding command line options.  The default
configuration is C<default>.


=item B<-mcop>

Enables the coprocessor instructions.  By default, this is a 32-bit
coprocessor.  Note that the coprocessor is normally enabled via the
C<-mconfig=> option.


=item B<-mcop32>

Enables the 32-bit coprocessor's instructions.


=item B<-mcop64>

Enables the 64-bit coprocessor's instructions.


=item B<-mivc2>

Enables IVC2 scheduling.  IVC2 is a 64-bit VLIW coprocessor.


=item B<-mdc>

Causes constant variables to be placed in the C<.near> section.


=item B<-mdiv>

Enables the C<div> and C<divu> instructions.


=item B<-meb>

Generate big-endian code.


=item B<-mel>

Generate little-endian code.


=item B<-mio-volatile>

Tells the compiler that any variable marked with the C<io>
attribute is to be considered volatile.


=item B<-ml>

Causes variables to be assigned to the C<.far> section by default.


=item B<-mleadz>

Enables the C<leadz> (leading zero) instruction.


=item B<-mm>

Causes variables to be assigned to the C<.near> section by default.


=item B<-mminmax>

Enables the C<min> and C<max> instructions.


=item B<-mmult>

Enables the multiplication and multiply-accumulate instructions.


=item B<-mno-opts>

Disables all the optional instructions enabled by C<-mall-opts>.


=item B<-mrepeat>

Enables the C<repeat> and C<erepeat> instructions, used for
low-overhead looping.


=item B<-ms>

Causes all variables to default to the C<.tiny> section.  Note
that there is a 65536 byte limit to this section.  Accesses to these
variables use the C<%gp> base register.


=item B<-msatur>

Enables the saturation instructions.  Note that the compiler does not
currently generate these itself, but this option is included for
compatibility with other tools, like C<as>.


=item B<-msdram>

Link the SDRAM-based runtime instead of the default ROM-based runtime.


=item B<-msim>

Link the simulator runtime libraries.


=item B<-msimnovec>

Link the simulator runtime libraries, excluding built-in support
for reset and exception vectors and tables.


=item B<-mtf>

Causes all functions to default to the C<.far> section.  Without
this option, functions default to the C<.near> section.


=item B<-mtiny=>I<n>

Variables that are I<n> bytes or smaller will be allocated to the
C<.tiny> section.  These variables use the C<$gp> base
register.  The default for this option is 4, but note that there's a
65536 byte limit to the C<.tiny> section.


=back



=head3 MicroBlaze Options



=over 4



=item B<-msoft-float>

Use software emulation for floating point (default).


=item B<-mhard-float>

Use hardware floating point instructions.


=item B<-mmemcpy>

Do not optimize block moves, use C<memcpy>.


=item B<-mno-clearbss>

This option is deprecated.  Use B<-fno-zero-initialized-in-bss> instead.


=item B<-mcpu=>I<cpu-type>

Use features of and schedule code for given CPU.
Supported values are in the format B<v>I<X>B<.>I<YY>B<.>I<Z>, 
where I<X> is a major version, I<YY> is the minor version, and 
I<Z> is compatibility code.  Example values are B<v3.00.a>,
B<v4.00.b>, B<v5.00.a>, B<v5.00.b>, B<v5.00.b>, B<v6.00.a>. 


=item B<-mxl-soft-mul>

Use software multiply emulation (default).


=item B<-mxl-soft-div>

Use software emulation for divides (default).


=item B<-mxl-barrel-shift>

Use the hardware barrel shifter.


=item B<-mxl-pattern-compare>

Use pattern compare instructions.


=item B<-msmall-divides>

Use table lookup optimization for small signed integer divisions.


=item B<-mxl-stack-check>

This option is deprecated.  Use -fstack-check instead.


=item B<-mxl-gp-opt>

Use GP relative sdata/sbss sections.


=item B<-mxl-multiply-high>

Use multiply high instructions for high part of 32x32 multiply.


=item B<-mxl-float-convert>

Use hardware floating point conversion instructions.


=item B<-mxl-float-sqrt>

Use hardware floating point square root instruction.


=item B<-mxl-mode->I<app-model>

Select application model I<app-model>.  Valid models are 

=over 4


=item B<executable>

normal executable (default), uses startup code F<crt0.o>.


=item B<xmdstub>

for use with Xilinx Microprocessor Debugger (XMD) based 
software intrusive debug agent called xmdstub. This uses startup file 
F<crt1.o> and sets the start address of the program to be 0x800.


=item B<bootstrap>

for applications that are loaded using a bootloader.
This model uses startup file F<crt2.o> which does not contain a processor 
reset vector handler. This is suitable for transferring control on a 
processor reset to the bootloader rather than the application.


=item B<novectors>

for applications that do not require any of the 
MicroBlaze vectors. This option may be useful for applications running
within a monitoring application. This model uses F<crt3.o> as a startup file.

=back


Option B<-xl-mode->I<app-model> is a deprecated alias for 
B<-mxl-mode->I<app-model>.


=back



=head3 MIPS Options



=over 4



=item B<-EB>

Generate big-endian code.


=item B<-EL>

Generate little-endian code.  This is the default for B<mips*el-*-*>
configurations.


=item B<-march=>I<arch>

Generate code that will run on I<arch>, which can be the name of a
generic MIPS ISA, or the name of a particular processor.
The ISA names are:
B<mips1>, B<mips2>, B<mips3>, B<mips4>,
B<mips32>, B<mips32r2>, B<mips64> and B<mips64r2>.
The processor names are:
B<4kc>, B<4km>, B<4kp>, B<4ksc>,
B<4kec>, B<4kem>, B<4kep>, B<4ksd>,
B<5kc>, B<5kf>,
B<20kc>,
B<24kc>, B<24kf2_1>, B<24kf1_1>,
B<24kec>, B<24kef2_1>, B<24kef1_1>,
B<34kc>, B<34kf2_1>, B<34kf1_1>,
B<74kc>, B<74kf2_1>, B<74kf1_1>, B<74kf3_2>,
B<1004kc>, B<1004kf2_1>, B<1004kf1_1>,
B<loongson2e>, B<loongson2f>, B<loongson3a>,
B<m4k>,
B<octeon>,
B<orion>,
B<r2000>, B<r3000>, B<r3900>, B<r4000>, B<r4400>,
B<r4600>, B<r4650>, B<r6000>, B<r8000>,
B<rm7000>, B<rm9000>,
B<r10000>, B<r12000>, B<r14000>, B<r16000>,
B<sb1>,
B<sr71000>,
B<vr4100>, B<vr4111>, B<vr4120>, B<vr4130>, B<vr4300>,
B<vr5000>, B<vr5400>, B<vr5500>
and B<xlr>.
The special value B<from-abi> selects the
most compatible architecture for the selected ABI (that is,
B<mips1> for 32-bit ABIs and B<mips3> for 64-bit ABIs).

Native Linux/GNU toolchains also support the value B<native>,
which selects the best architecture option for the host processor.
B<-march=native> has no effect if GCC does not recognize
the processor.

In processor names, a final B<000> can be abbreviated as B<k>
(for example, B<-march=r2k>).  Prefixes are optional, and
B<vr> may be written B<r>.

Names of the form I<n>B<f2_1> refer to processors with
FPUs clocked at half the rate of the core, names of the form
I<n>B<f1_1> refer to processors with FPUs clocked at the same
rate as the core, and names of the form I<n>B<f3_2> refer to
processors with FPUs clocked a ratio of 3:2 with respect to the core.
For compatibility reasons, I<n>B<f> is accepted as a synonym
for I<n>B<f2_1> while I<n>B<x> and I<b>B<fx> are
accepted as synonyms for I<n>B<f1_1>.

GCC defines two macros based on the value of this option.  The first
is B<_MIPS_ARCH>, which gives the name of target architecture, as
a string.  The second has the form B<_MIPS_ARCH_>I<foo>,
where I<foo> is the capitalized value of B<_MIPS_ARCH>.
For example, B<-march=r2000> will set B<_MIPS_ARCH>
to B<"r2000"> and define the macro B<_MIPS_ARCH_R2000>.

Note that the B<_MIPS_ARCH> macro uses the processor names given
above.  In other words, it will have the full prefix and will not
abbreviate B<000> as B<k>.  In the case of B<from-abi>,
the macro names the resolved architecture (either B<"mips1"> or
B<"mips3">).  It names the default architecture when no
B<-march> option is given.


=item B<-mtune=>I<arch>

Optimize for I<arch>.  Among other things, this option controls
the way instructions are scheduled, and the perceived cost of arithmetic
operations.  The list of I<arch> values is the same as for
B<-march>.

When this option is not used, GCC will optimize for the processor
specified by B<-march>.  By using B<-march> and
B<-mtune> together, it is possible to generate code that will
run on a family of processors, but optimize the code for one
particular member of that family.

B<-mtune> defines the macros B<_MIPS_TUNE> and
B<_MIPS_TUNE_>I<foo>, which work in the same way as the
B<-march> ones described above.


=item B<-mips1>

Equivalent to B<-march=mips1>.


=item B<-mips2>

Equivalent to B<-march=mips2>.


=item B<-mips3>

Equivalent to B<-march=mips3>.


=item B<-mips4>

Equivalent to B<-march=mips4>.


=item B<-mips32>

Equivalent to B<-march=mips32>.


=item B<-mips32r2>

Equivalent to B<-march=mips32r2>.


=item B<-mips64>

Equivalent to B<-march=mips64>.


=item B<-mips64r2>

Equivalent to B<-march=mips64r2>.


=item B<-mips16>


=item B<-mno-mips16>

Generate (do not generate) MIPS16 code.  If GCC is targetting a
MIPS32 or MIPS64 architecture, it will make use of the MIPS16e ASE.

MIPS16 code generation can also be controlled on a per-function basis
by means of C<mips16> and C<nomips16> attributes.



=item B<-mflip-mips16>

Generate MIPS16 code on alternating functions.  This option is provided
for regression testing of mixed MIPS16/non-MIPS16 code generation, and is
not intended for ordinary use in compiling user code.


=item B<-minterlink-mips16>


=item B<-mno-interlink-mips16>

Require (do not require) that non-MIPS16 code be link-compatible with
MIPS16 code.

For example, non-MIPS16 code cannot jump directly to MIPS16 code;
it must either use a call or an indirect jump.  B<-minterlink-mips16>
therefore disables direct jumps unless GCC knows that the target of the
jump is not MIPS16.


=item B<-mabi=32>


=item B<-mabi=o64>


=item B<-mabi=n32>


=item B<-mabi=64>


=item B<-mabi=eabi>

Generate code for the given ABI.

Note that the EABI has a 32-bit and a 64-bit variant.  GCC normally
generates 64-bit code when you select a 64-bit architecture, but you
can use B<-mgp32> to get 32-bit code instead.

For information about the O64 ABI, see
E<lt>B<http://gcc.gnu.org/projects/mipso64-abi.html>E<gt>.

GCC supports a variant of the o32 ABI in which floating-point registers
are 64 rather than 32 bits wide.  You can select this combination with
B<-mabi=32> B<-mfp64>.  This ABI relies on the B<mthc1>
and B<mfhc1> instructions and is therefore only supported for
MIPS32R2 processors.

The register assignments for arguments and return values remain the
same, but each scalar value is passed in a single 64-bit register
rather than a pair of 32-bit registers.  For example, scalar
floating-point values are returned in B<$f0> only, not a
B<$f0>/B<$f1> pair.  The set of call-saved registers also
remains the same, but all 64 bits are saved.


=item B<-mabicalls>


=item B<-mno-abicalls>

Generate (do not generate) code that is suitable for SVR4-style
dynamic objects.  B<-mabicalls> is the default for SVR4-based
systems.


=item B<-mshared>


=item B<-mno-shared>

Generate (do not generate) code that is fully position-independent,
and that can therefore be linked into shared libraries.  This option
only affects B<-mabicalls>.

All B<-mabicalls> code has traditionally been position-independent,
regardless of options like B<-fPIC> and B<-fpic>.  However,
as an extension, the GNU toolchain allows executables to use absolute
accesses for locally-binding symbols.  It can also use shorter GP
initialization sequences and generate direct calls to locally-defined
functions.  This mode is selected by B<-mno-shared>.

B<-mno-shared> depends on binutils 2.16 or higher and generates
objects that can only be linked by the GNU linker.  However, the option
does not affect the ABI of the final executable; it only affects the ABI
of relocatable objects.  Using B<-mno-shared> will generally make
executables both smaller and quicker.

B<-mshared> is the default.


=item B<-mplt>


=item B<-mno-plt>

Assume (do not assume) that the static and dynamic linkers
support PLTs and copy relocations.  This option only affects
B<-mno-shared -mabicalls>.  For the n64 ABI, this option
has no effect without B<-msym32>.

You can make B<-mplt> the default by configuring
GCC with B<--with-mips-plt>.  The default is
B<-mno-plt> otherwise.


=item B<-mxgot>


=item B<-mno-xgot>

Lift (do not lift) the usual restrictions on the size of the global
offset table.

GCC normally uses a single instruction to load values from the GOT.
While this is relatively efficient, it will only work if the GOT
is smaller than about 64k.  Anything larger will cause the linker
to report an error such as:

	
	relocation truncated to fit: R_MIPS_GOT16 foobar


If this happens, you should recompile your code with B<-mxgot>.
It should then work with very large GOTs, although it will also be
less efficient, since it will take three instructions to fetch the
value of a global symbol.

Note that some linkers can create multiple GOTs.  If you have such a
linker, you should only need to use B<-mxgot> when a single object
file accesses more than 64k's worth of GOT entries.  Very few do.

These options have no effect unless GCC is generating position
independent code.


=item B<-mgp32>

Assume that general-purpose registers are 32 bits wide.


=item B<-mgp64>

Assume that general-purpose registers are 64 bits wide.


=item B<-mfp32>

Assume that floating-point registers are 32 bits wide.


=item B<-mfp64>

Assume that floating-point registers are 64 bits wide.


=item B<-mhard-float>

Use floating-point coprocessor instructions.


=item B<-msoft-float>

Do not use floating-point coprocessor instructions.  Implement
floating-point calculations using library calls instead.


=item B<-msingle-float>

Assume that the floating-point coprocessor only supports single-precision
operations.


=item B<-mdouble-float>

Assume that the floating-point coprocessor supports double-precision
operations.  This is the default.


=item B<-mllsc>


=item B<-mno-llsc>

Use (do not use) B<ll>, B<sc>, and B<sync> instructions to
implement atomic memory built-in functions.  When neither option is
specified, GCC will use the instructions if the target architecture
supports them.

B<-mllsc> is useful if the runtime environment can emulate the
instructions and B<-mno-llsc> can be useful when compiling for
nonstandard ISAs.  You can make either option the default by
configuring GCC with B<--with-llsc> and B<--without-llsc>
respectively.  B<--with-llsc> is the default for some
configurations; see the installation documentation for details.


=item B<-mdsp>


=item B<-mno-dsp>

Use (do not use) revision 1 of the MIPS DSP ASE.
  This option defines the
preprocessor macro B<__mips_dsp>.  It also defines
B<__mips_dsp_rev> to 1.


=item B<-mdspr2>


=item B<-mno-dspr2>

Use (do not use) revision 2 of the MIPS DSP ASE.
  This option defines the
preprocessor macros B<__mips_dsp> and B<__mips_dspr2>.
It also defines B<__mips_dsp_rev> to 2.


=item B<-msmartmips>


=item B<-mno-smartmips>

Use (do not use) the MIPS SmartMIPS ASE.


=item B<-mpaired-single>


=item B<-mno-paired-single>

Use (do not use) paired-single floating-point instructions.
  This option requires
hardware floating-point support to be enabled.


=item B<-mdmx>


=item B<-mno-mdmx>

Use (do not use) MIPS Digital Media Extension instructions.
This option can only be used when generating 64-bit code and requires
hardware floating-point support to be enabled.


=item B<-mips3d>


=item B<-mno-mips3d>

Use (do not use) the MIPS-3D ASE.
