The option B<-mips3d> implies B<-mpaired-single>.


=item B<-mmt>


=item B<-mno-mt>

Use (do not use) MT Multithreading instructions.


=item B<-mlong64>

Force C<long> types to be 64 bits wide.  See B<-mlong32> for
an explanation of the default and the way that the pointer size is
determined.


=item B<-mlong32>

Force C<long>, C<int>, and pointer types to be 32 bits wide.

The default size of C<int>s, C<long>s and pointers depends on
the ABI.  All the supported ABIs use 32-bit C<int>s.  The n64 ABI
uses 64-bit C<long>s, as does the 64-bit EABI; the others use
32-bit C<long>s.  Pointers are the same size as C<long>s,
or the same size as integer registers, whichever is smaller.


=item B<-msym32>


=item B<-mno-sym32>

Assume (do not assume) that all symbols have 32-bit values, regardless
of the selected ABI.  This option is useful in combination with
B<-mabi=64> and B<-mno-abicalls> because it allows GCC
to generate shorter and faster references to symbolic addresses.


=item B<-G> I<num>

Put definitions of externally-visible data in a small data section
if that data is no bigger than I<num> bytes.  GCC can then access
the data more efficiently; see B<-mgpopt> for details.

The default B<-G> option depends on the configuration.


=item B<-mlocal-sdata>


=item B<-mno-local-sdata>

Extend (do not extend) the B<-G> behavior to local data too,
such as to static variables in C.  B<-mlocal-sdata> is the
default for all configurations.

If the linker complains that an application is using too much small data,
you might want to try rebuilding the less performance-critical parts with
B<-mno-local-sdata>.  You might also want to build large
libraries with B<-mno-local-sdata>, so that the libraries leave
more room for the main program.


=item B<-mextern-sdata>


=item B<-mno-extern-sdata>

Assume (do not assume) that externally-defined data will be in
a small data section if that data is within the B<-G> limit.
B<-mextern-sdata> is the default for all configurations.

If you compile a module I<Mod> with B<-mextern-sdata> B<-G>
I<num> B<-mgpopt>, and I<Mod> references a variable I<Var>
that is no bigger than I<num> bytes, you must make sure that I<Var>
is placed in a small data section.  If I<Var> is defined by another
module, you must either compile that module with a high-enough
B<-G> setting or attach a C<section> attribute to I<Var>'s
definition.  If I<Var> is common, you must link the application
with a high-enough B<-G> setting.

The easiest way of satisfying these restrictions is to compile
and link every module with the same B<-G> option.  However,
you may wish to build a library that supports several different
small data limits.  You can do this by compiling the library with
the highest supported B<-G> setting and additionally using
B<-mno-extern-sdata> to stop the library from making assumptions
about externally-defined data.


=item B<-mgpopt>


=item B<-mno-gpopt>

Use (do not use) GP-relative accesses for symbols that are known to be
in a small data section; see B<-G>, B<-mlocal-sdata> and
B<-mextern-sdata>.  B<-mgpopt> is the default for all
configurations.

B<-mno-gpopt> is useful for cases where the C<$gp> register
might not hold the value of C<_gp>.  For example, if the code is
part of a library that might be used in a boot monitor, programs that
call boot monitor routines will pass an unknown value in C<$gp>.
(In such situations, the boot monitor itself would usually be compiled
with B<-G0>.)

B<-mno-gpopt> implies B<-mno-local-sdata> and
B<-mno-extern-sdata>.


=item B<-membedded-data>


=item B<-mno-embedded-data>

Allocate variables to the read-only data section first if possible, then
next in the small data section if possible, otherwise in data.  This gives
slightly slower code than the default, but reduces the amount of RAM required
when executing, and thus may be preferred for some embedded systems.


=item B<-muninit-const-in-rodata>


=item B<-mno-uninit-const-in-rodata>

Put uninitialized C<const> variables in the read-only data section.
This option is only meaningful in conjunction with B<-membedded-data>.


=item B<-mcode-readable=>I<setting>

Specify whether GCC may generate code that reads from executable sections.
There are three possible settings:


=over 4


=item B<-mcode-readable=yes>

Instructions may freely access executable sections.  This is the
default setting.


=item B<-mcode-readable=pcrel>

MIPS16 PC-relative load instructions can access executable sections,
but other instructions must not do so.  This option is useful on 4KSc
and 4KSd processors when the code TLBs have the Read Inhibit bit set.
It is also useful on processors that can be configured to have a dual
instruction/data SRAM interface and that, like the M4K, automatically
redirect PC-relative loads to the instruction RAM.


=item B<-mcode-readable=no>

Instructions must not access executable sections.  This option can be
useful on targets that are configured to have a dual instruction/data
SRAM interface but that (unlike the M4K) do not automatically redirect
PC-relative loads to the instruction RAM.

=back



=item B<-msplit-addresses>


=item B<-mno-split-addresses>

Enable (disable) use of the C<%hi()> and C<%lo()> assembler
relocation operators.  This option has been superseded by
B<-mexplicit-relocs> but is retained for backwards compatibility.


=item B<-mexplicit-relocs>


=item B<-mno-explicit-relocs>

Use (do not use) assembler relocation operators when dealing with symbolic
addresses.  The alternative, selected by B<-mno-explicit-relocs>,
is to use assembler macros instead.

B<-mexplicit-relocs> is the default if GCC was configured
to use an assembler that supports relocation operators.


=item B<-mcheck-zero-division>


=item B<-mno-check-zero-division>

Trap (do not trap) on integer division by zero.

The default is B<-mcheck-zero-division>.


=item B<-mdivide-traps>


=item B<-mdivide-breaks>

MIPS systems check for division by zero by generating either a
conditional trap or a break instruction.  Using traps results in
smaller code, but is only supported on MIPS II and later.  Also, some
versions of the Linux kernel have a bug that prevents trap from
generating the proper signal (C<SIGFPE>).  Use B<-mdivide-traps> to
allow conditional traps on architectures that support them and
B<-mdivide-breaks> to force the use of breaks.

The default is usually B<-mdivide-traps>, but this can be
overridden at configure time using B<--with-divide=breaks>.
Divide-by-zero checks can be completely disabled using
B<-mno-check-zero-division>.


=item B<-mmemcpy>


=item B<-mno-memcpy>

Force (do not force) the use of C<memcpy()> for non-trivial block
moves.  The default is B<-mno-memcpy>, which allows GCC to inline
most constant-sized copies.


=item B<-mlong-calls>


=item B<-mno-long-calls>

Disable (do not disable) use of the C<jal> instruction.  Calling
functions using C<jal> is more efficient but requires the caller
and callee to be in the same 256 megabyte segment.

This option has no effect on abicalls code.  The default is
B<-mno-long-calls>.


=item B<-mmad>


=item B<-mno-mad>

Enable (disable) use of the C<mad>, C<madu> and C<mul>
instructions, as provided by the R4650 ISA.


=item B<-mfused-madd>


=item B<-mno-fused-madd>

Enable (disable) use of the floating point multiply-accumulate
instructions, when they are available.  The default is
B<-mfused-madd>.

When multiply-accumulate instructions are used, the intermediate
product is calculated to infinite precision and is not subject to
the FCSR Flush to Zero bit.  This may be undesirable in some
circumstances.


=item B<-nocpp>

Tell the MIPS assembler to not run its preprocessor over user
assembler files (with a B<.s> suffix) when assembling them.


=item B<-mfix-r4000>


=item B<-mno-fix-r4000>

Work around certain R4000 CPU errata:

=over 4


=item -

A double-word or a variable shift may give an incorrect result if executed
immediately after starting an integer division.

=item -

A double-word or a variable shift may give an incorrect result if executed
while an integer multiplication is in progress.

=item -

An integer division may give an incorrect result if started in a delay slot
of a taken branch or a jump.

=back



=item B<-mfix-r4400>


=item B<-mno-fix-r4400>

Work around certain R4400 CPU errata:

=over 4


=item -

A double-word or a variable shift may give an incorrect result if executed
immediately after starting an integer division.

=back



=item B<-mfix-r10000>


=item B<-mno-fix-r10000>

Work around certain R10000 errata:

=over 4


=item -

C<ll>/C<sc> sequences may not behave atomically on revisions
prior to 3.0.  They may deadlock on revisions 2.6 and earlier.

=back


This option can only be used if the target architecture supports
branch-likely instructions.  B<-mfix-r10000> is the default when
B<-march=r10000> is used; B<-mno-fix-r10000> is the default
otherwise.


=item B<-mfix-vr4120>


=item B<-mno-fix-vr4120>

Work around certain VR4120 errata:

=over 4


=item -

C<dmultu> does not always produce the correct result.

=item -

C<div> and C<ddiv> do not always produce the correct result if one
of the operands is negative.

=back

The workarounds for the division errata rely on special functions in
F<libgcc.a>.  At present, these functions are only provided by
the C<mips64vr*-elf> configurations.

Other VR4120 errata require a nop to be inserted between certain pairs of
instructions.  These errata are handled by the assembler, not by GCC itself.


=item B<-mfix-vr4130>

Work around the VR4130 C<mflo>/C<mfhi> errata.  The
workarounds are implemented by the assembler rather than by GCC,
although GCC will avoid using C<mflo> and C<mfhi> if the
VR4130 C<macc>, C<macchi>, C<dmacc> and C<dmacchi>
instructions are available instead.


=item B<-mfix-sb1>


=item B<-mno-fix-sb1>

Work around certain SB-1 CPU core errata.
(This flag currently works around the SB-1 revision 2
"F1" and "F2" floating point errata.)


=item B<-mr10k-cache-barrier=>I<setting>

Specify whether GCC should insert cache barriers to avoid the
side-effects of speculation on R10K processors.

In common with many processors, the R10K tries to predict the outcome
of a conditional branch and speculatively executes instructions from
the "taken" branch.  It later aborts these instructions if the
predicted outcome was wrong.  However, on the R10K, even aborted
instructions can have side effects.

This problem only affects kernel stores and, depending on the system,
kernel loads.  As an example, a speculatively-executed store may load
the target memory into cache and mark the cache line as dirty, even if
the store itself is later aborted.  If a DMA operation writes to the
same area of memory before the "dirty" line is flushed, the cached
data will overwrite the DMA-ed data.  See the R10K processor manual
for a full description, including other potential problems.

One workaround is to insert cache barrier instructions before every memory
access that might be speculatively executed and that might have side
effects even if aborted.  B<-mr10k-cache-barrier=>I<setting>
controls GCC's implementation of this workaround.  It assumes that
aborted accesses to any byte in the following regions will not have
side effects:


=over 4


=item 1.

the memory occupied by the current function's stack frame;


=item 2.

the memory occupied by an incoming stack argument;


=item 3.

the memory occupied by an object with a link-time-constant address.

=back


It is the kernel's responsibility to ensure that speculative
accesses to these regions are indeed safe.

If the input program contains a function declaration such as:

	
	void foo (void);


then the implementation of C<foo> must allow C<j foo> and
C<jal foo> to be executed speculatively.  GCC honors this
restriction for functions it compiles itself.  It expects non-GCC
functions (such as hand-written assembly code) to do the same.

The option has three forms:


=over 4


=item B<-mr10k-cache-barrier=load-store>

Insert a cache barrier before a load or store that might be
speculatively executed and that might have side effects even
if aborted.


=item B<-mr10k-cache-barrier=store>

Insert a cache barrier before a store that might be speculatively
executed and that might have side effects even if aborted.


=item B<-mr10k-cache-barrier=none>

Disable the insertion of cache barriers.  This is the default setting.

=back



=item B<-mflush-func=>I<func>


=item B<-mno-flush-func>

Specifies the function to call to flush the I and D caches, or to not
call any such function.  If called, the function must take the same
arguments as the common C<_flush_func()>, that is, the address of the
memory range for which the cache is being flushed, the size of the
memory range, and the number 3 (to flush both caches).  The default
depends on the target GCC was configured for, but commonly is either
B<_flush_func> or B<__cpu_flush>.


=item B<mbranch-cost=>I<num>

Set the cost of branches to roughly I<num> "simple" instructions.
This cost is only a heuristic and is not guaranteed to produce
consistent results across releases.  A zero cost redundantly selects
the default, which is based on the B<-mtune> setting.


=item B<-mbranch-likely>


=item B<-mno-branch-likely>

Enable or disable use of Branch Likely instructions, regardless of the
default for the selected architecture.  By default, Branch Likely
instructions may be generated if they are supported by the selected
architecture.  An exception is for the MIPS32 and MIPS64 architectures
and processors which implement those architectures; for those, Branch
Likely instructions will not be generated by default because the MIPS32
and MIPS64 architectures specifically deprecate their use.


=item B<-mfp-exceptions>


=item B<-mno-fp-exceptions>

Specifies whether FP exceptions are enabled.  This affects how we schedule
FP instructions for some processors.  The default is that FP exceptions are
enabled.

For instance, on the SB-1, if FP exceptions are disabled, and we are emitting
64-bit code, then we can use both FP pipes.  Otherwise, we can only use one
FP pipe.


=item B<-mvr4130-align>


=item B<-mno-vr4130-align>

The VR4130 pipeline is two-way superscalar, but can only issue two
instructions together if the first one is 8-byte aligned.  When this
option is enabled, GCC will align pairs of instructions that it
thinks should execute in parallel.

This option only has an effect when optimizing for the VR4130.
It normally makes code faster, but at the expense of making it bigger.
It is enabled by default at optimization level B<-O3>.


=item B<-msynci>


=item B<-mno-synci>

Enable (disable) generation of C<synci> instructions on
architectures that support it.  The C<synci> instructions (if
enabled) will be generated when C<__builtin___clear_cache()> is
compiled.

This option defaults to C<-mno-synci>, but the default can be
overridden by configuring with C<--with-synci>.

When compiling code for single processor systems, it is generally safe
to use C<synci>.  However, on many multi-core (SMP) systems, it
will not invalidate the instruction caches on all cores and may lead
to undefined behavior.


=item B<-mrelax-pic-calls>


=item B<-mno-relax-pic-calls>

Try to turn PIC calls that are normally dispatched via register
C<$25> into direct calls.  This is only possible if the linker can
resolve the destination at link-time and if the destination is within
range for a direct call.

B<-mrelax-pic-calls> is the default if GCC was configured to use
an assembler and a linker that supports the C<.reloc> assembly
directive and C<-mexplicit-relocs> is in effect.  With
C<-mno-explicit-relocs>, this optimization can be performed by the
assembler and the linker alone without help from the compiler.


=item B<-mmcount-ra-address>


=item B<-mno-mcount-ra-address>

Emit (do not emit) code that allows C<_mcount> to modify the
calling function's return address.  When enabled, this option extends
the usual C<_mcount> interface with a new I<ra-address>
parameter, which has type C<intptr_t *> and is passed in register
C<$12>.  C<_mcount> can then modify the return address by
doing both of the following:

=over 4


=item *

Returning the new address in register C<$31>.

=item *

Storing the new address in C<*I<ra-address>>,
if I<ra-address> is nonnull.

=back


The default is B<-mno-mcount-ra-address>.


=back



=head3 MMIX Options


These options are defined for the MMIX:


=over 4


=item B<-mlibfuncs>


=item B<-mno-libfuncs>

Specify that intrinsic library functions are being compiled, passing all
values in registers, no matter the size.


=item B<-mepsilon>


=item B<-mno-epsilon>

Generate floating-point comparison instructions that compare with respect
to the C<rE> epsilon register.


=item B<-mabi=mmixware>


=item B<-mabi=gnu>

Generate code that passes function parameters and return values that (in
the called function) are seen as registers C<$0> and up, as opposed to
the GNU ABI which uses global registers C<$231> and up.


=item B<-mzero-extend>


=item B<-mno-zero-extend>

When reading data from memory in sizes shorter than 64 bits, use (do not
use) zero-extending load instructions by default, rather than
sign-extending ones.


=item B<-mknuthdiv>


=item B<-mno-knuthdiv>

Make the result of a division yielding a remainder have the same sign as
the divisor.  With the default, B<-mno-knuthdiv>, the sign of the
remainder follows the sign of the dividend.  Both methods are
arithmetically valid, the latter being almost exclusively used.


=item B<-mtoplevel-symbols>


=item B<-mno-toplevel-symbols>

Prepend (do not prepend) a B<:> to all global symbols, so the assembly
code can be used with the C<PREFIX> assembly directive.


=item B<-melf>

Generate an executable in the ELF format, rather than the default
B<mmo> format used by the B<mmix> simulator.


=item B<-mbranch-predict>


=item B<-mno-branch-predict>

Use (do not use) the probable-branch instructions, when static branch
prediction indicates a probable branch.


=item B<-mbase-addresses>


=item B<-mno-base-addresses>

Generate (do not generate) code that uses I<base addresses>.  Using a
base address automatically generates a request (handled by the assembler
and the linker) for a constant to be set up in a global register.  The
register is used for one or more base address requests within the range 0
to 255 from the value held in the register.  The generally leads to short
and fast code, but the number of different data items that can be
addressed is limited.  This means that a program that uses lots of static
data may require B<-mno-base-addresses>.


=item B<-msingle-exit>


=item B<-mno-single-exit>

Force (do not force) generated code to have a single exit point in each
function.

=back



=head3 MN10300 Options


These B<-m> options are defined for Matsushita MN10300 architectures:


=over 4


=item B<-mmult-bug>

Generate code to avoid bugs in the multiply instructions for the MN10300
processors.  This is the default.


=item B<-mno-mult-bug>

Do not generate code to avoid bugs in the multiply instructions for the
MN10300 processors.


=item B<-mam33>

Generate code which uses features specific to the AM33 processor.


=item B<-mno-am33>

Do not generate code which uses features specific to the AM33 processor.  This
is the default.


=item B<-mam33-2>

Generate code which uses features specific to the AM33/2.0 processor.


=item B<-mam34>

Generate code which uses features specific to the AM34 processor.


=item B<-mtune=>I<cpu-type>

Use the timing characteristics of the indicated CPU type when
scheduling instructions.  This does not change the targeted processor
type.  The CPU type must be one of B<mn10300>, B<am33>,
B<am33-2> or B<am34>.


=item B<-mreturn-pointer-on-d0>

When generating a function which returns a pointer, return the pointer
in both C<a0> and C<d0>.  Otherwise, the pointer is returned
only in a0, and attempts to call such functions without a prototype
would result in errors.  Note that this option is on by default; use
B<-mno-return-pointer-on-d0> to disable it.


=item B<-mno-crt0>

Do not link in the C run-time initialization object file.


=item B<-mrelax>

Indicate to the linker that it should perform a relaxation optimization pass
to shorten branches, calls and absolute memory addresses.  This option only
has an effect when used on the command line for the final link step.

This option makes symbolic debugging impossible.


=item B<-mliw>

Allow the compiler to generate I<Long Instruction Word>
instructions if the target is the B<AM33> or later.  This is the
default.  This option defines the preprocessor macro B<__LIW__>.


=item B<-mnoliw>

Do not allow the compiler to generate I<Long Instruction Word>
instructions.  This option defines the preprocessor macro
B<__NO_LIW__>.


=back



=head3 PDP-11 Options


These options are defined for the PDP-11:


=over 4


=item B<-mfpu>

Use hardware FPP floating point.  This is the default.  (FIS floating
point on the PDP-11/40 is not supported.)


=item B<-msoft-float>

Do not use hardware floating point.


=item B<-mac0>

Return floating-point results in ac0 (fr0 in Unix assembler syntax).


=item B<-mno-ac0>

Return floating-point results in memory.  This is the default.


=item B<-m40>

Generate code for a PDP-11/40.


=item B<-m45>

Generate code for a PDP-11/45.  This is the default.


=item B<-m10>

Generate code for a PDP-11/10.


=item B<-mbcopy-builtin>

Use inline C<movmemhi> patterns for copying memory.  This is the
default.


=item B<-mbcopy>

Do not use inline C<movmemhi> patterns for copying memory.


=item B<-mint16>


=item B<-mno-int32>

Use 16-bit C<int>.  This is the default.


=item B<-mint32>


=item B<-mno-int16>

Use 32-bit C<int>.


=item B<-mfloat64>


=item B<-mno-float32>

Use 64-bit C<float>.  This is the default.


=item B<-mfloat32>


=item B<-mno-float64>

Use 32-bit C<float>.


=item B<-mabshi>

Use C<abshi2> pattern.  This is the default.


=item B<-mno-abshi>

Do not use C<abshi2> pattern.


=item B<-mbranch-expensive>

Pretend that branches are expensive.  This is for experimenting with
code generation only.


=item B<-mbranch-cheap>

Do not pretend that branches are expensive.  This is the default.


=item B<-munix-asm>

Use Unix assembler syntax.  This is the default when configured for
B<pdp11-*-bsd>.


=item B<-mdec-asm>

Use DEC assembler syntax.  This is the default when configured for any
PDP-11 target other than B<pdp11-*-bsd>.

=back



=head3 picoChip Options


These B<-m> options are defined for picoChip implementations:


=over 4



=item B<-mae=>I<ae_type>

Set the instruction set, register set, and instruction scheduling
parameters for array element type I<ae_type>.  Supported values
for I<ae_type> are B<ANY>, B<MUL>, and B<MAC>.

B<-mae=ANY> selects a completely generic AE type.  Code
generated with this option will run on any of the other AE types.  The
code will not be as efficient as it would be if compiled for a specific
AE type, and some types of operation (e.g., multiplication) will not
work properly on all types of AE.

B<-mae=MUL> selects a MUL AE type.  This is the most useful AE type
for compiled code, and is the default.

B<-mae=MAC> selects a DSP-style MAC AE.  Code compiled with this
option may suffer from poor performance of byte (char) manipulation,
since the DSP AE does not provide hardware support for byte load/stores.


=item B<-msymbol-as-address>

Enable the compiler to directly use a symbol name as an address in a
load/store instruction, without first loading it into a
register.  Typically, the use of this option will generate larger
programs, which run faster than when the option isn't used.  However, the
results vary from program to program, so it is left as a user option,
rather than being permanently enabled.


=item B<-mno-inefficient-warnings>

Disables warnings about the generation of inefficient code.  These
warnings can be generated, for example, when compiling code which
performs byte-level memory operations on the MAC AE type.  The MAC AE has
no hardware support for byte-level memory operations, so all byte
load/stores must be synthesized from word load/store operations.  This is
inefficient and a warning will be generated indicating to the programmer
that they should rewrite the code to avoid byte operations, or to target
an AE type which has the necessary hardware support.  This option enables
the warning to be turned off.


=back



=head3 PowerPC Options


These are listed under 


=head3 IBM RS/6000 and PowerPC Options


These B<-m> options are defined for the IBM RS/6000 and PowerPC:

=over 4


=item B<-mpower>


=item B<-mno-power>


=item B<-mpower2>


=item B<-mno-power2>


=item B<-mpowerpc>


=item B<-mno-powerpc>


=item B<-mpowerpc-gpopt>


=item B<-mno-powerpc-gpopt>


=item B<-mpowerpc-gfxopt>


=item B<-mno-powerpc-gfxopt>


=item B<-mpowerpc64>


=item B<-mno-powerpc64>


=item B<-mmfcrf>


=item B<-mno-mfcrf>


=item B<-mpopcntb>


=item B<-mno-popcntb>


=item B<-mpopcntd>


=item B<-mno-popcntd>


=item B<-mfprnd>


=item B<-mno-fprnd>


=item B<-mcmpb>


=item B<-mno-cmpb>


=item B<-mmfpgpr>


=item B<-mno-mfpgpr>


=item B<-mhard-dfp>


=item B<-mno-hard-dfp>

GCC supports two related instruction set architectures for the
RS/6000 and PowerPC.  The I<POWER> instruction set are those
instructions supported by the B<rios> chip set used in the original
RS/6000 systems and the I<PowerPC> instruction set is the
architecture of the Freescale MPC5xx, MPC6xx, MPC8xx microprocessors, and
the IBM 4xx, 6xx, and follow-on microprocessors.

Neither architecture is a subset of the other.  However there is a
large common subset of instructions supported by both.  An MQ
register is included in processors supporting the POWER architecture.

You use these options to specify which instructions are available on the
processor you are using.  The default value of these options is
determined when configuring GCC.  Specifying the
B<-mcpu=>I<cpu_type> overrides the specification of these
options.  We recommend you use the B<-mcpu=>I<cpu_type> option
rather than the options listed above.

The B<-mpower> option allows GCC to generate instructions that
are found only in the POWER architecture and to use the MQ register.
Specifying B<-mpower2> implies B<-power> and also allows GCC
to generate instructions that are present in the POWER2 architecture but
not the original POWER architecture.

The B<-mpowerpc> option allows GCC to generate instructions that
are found only in the 32-bit subset of the PowerPC architecture.
Specifying B<-mpowerpc-gpopt> implies B<-mpowerpc> and also allows
GCC to use the optional PowerPC architecture instructions in the
General Purpose group, including floating-point square root.  Specifying
B<-mpowerpc-gfxopt> implies B<-mpowerpc> and also allows GCC to
use the optional PowerPC architecture instructions in the Graphics
group, including floating-point select.

The B<-mmfcrf> option allows GCC to generate the move from
condition register field instruction implemented on the POWER4
processor and other processors that support the PowerPC V2.01
architecture.
The B<-mpopcntb> option allows GCC to generate the popcount and
double precision FP reciprocal estimate instruction implemented on the
POWER5 processor and other processors that support the PowerPC V2.02
architecture.
The B<-mpopcntd> option allows GCC to generate the popcount
instruction implemented on the POWER7 processor and other processors
that support the PowerPC V2.06 architecture.
The B<-mfprnd> option allows GCC to generate the FP round to
integer instructions implemented on the POWER5+ processor and other
processors that support the PowerPC V2.03 architecture.
The B<-mcmpb> option allows GCC to generate the compare bytes
instruction implemented on the POWER6 processor and other processors
that support the PowerPC V2.05 architecture.
The B<-mmfpgpr> option allows GCC to generate the FP move to/from
general purpose register instructions implemented on the POWER6X
processor and other processors that support the extended PowerPC V2.05
architecture.
The B<-mhard-dfp> option allows GCC to generate the decimal floating
point instructions implemented on some POWER processors.

The B<-mpowerpc64> option allows GCC to generate the additional
64-bit instructions that are found in the full PowerPC64 architecture
and to treat GPRs as 64-bit, doubleword quantities.  GCC defaults to
B<-mno-powerpc64>.

If you specify both B<-mno-power> and B<-mno-powerpc>, GCC
will use only the instructions in the common subset of both
architectures plus some special AIX common-mode calls, and will not use
the MQ register.  Specifying both B<-mpower> and B<-mpowerpc>
permits GCC to use any instruction from either architecture and to
allow use of the MQ register; specify this for the Motorola MPC601.


=item B<-mnew-mnemonics>


=item B<-mold-mnemonics>

Select which mnemonics to use in the generated assembler code.  With
B<-mnew-mnemonics>, GCC uses the assembler mnemonics defined for
the PowerPC architecture.  With B<-mold-mnemonics> it uses the
assembler mnemonics defined for the POWER architecture.  Instructions
defined in only one architecture have only one mnemonic; GCC uses that
mnemonic irrespective of which of these options is specified.

GCC defaults to the mnemonics appropriate for the architecture in
use.  Specifying B<-mcpu=>I<cpu_type> sometimes overrides the
value of these option.  Unless you are building a cross-compiler, you
should normally not specify either B<-mnew-mnemonics> or
B<-mold-mnemonics>, but should instead accept the default.


=item B<-mcpu=>I<cpu_type>

Set architecture type, register usage, choice of mnemonics, and
instruction scheduling parameters for machine type I<cpu_type>.
Supported values for I<cpu_type> are B<401>, B<403>,
B<405>, B<405fp>, B<440>, B<440fp>, B<464>, B<464fp>,
B<476>, B<476fp>, B<505>, B<601>, B<602>, B<603>,
B<603e>, B<604>, B<604e>, B<620>, B<630>, B<740>,
B<7400>, B<7450>, B<750>, B<801>, B<821>, B<823>,
B<860>, B<970>, B<8540>, B<a2>, B<e300c2>,
B<e300c3>, B<e500mc>, B<e500mc64>, B<ec603e>, B<G3>,
B<G4>, B<G5>, B<titan>, B<power>, B<power2>, B<power3>,
B<power4>, B<power5>, B<power5+>, B<power6>, B<power6x>,
B<power7>, B<common>, B<powerpc>, B<powerpc64>, B<rios>,
B<rios1>, B<rios2>, B<rsc>, and B<rs64>.

B<-mcpu=common> selects a completely generic processor.  Code
generated under this option will run on any POWER or PowerPC processor.
GCC will use only the instructions in the common subset of both
architectures, and will not use the MQ register.  GCC assumes a generic
processor model for scheduling purposes.

B<-mcpu=power>, B<-mcpu=power2>, B<-mcpu=powerpc>, and
B<-mcpu=powerpc64> specify generic POWER, POWER2, pure 32-bit
PowerPC (i.e., not MPC601), and 64-bit PowerPC architecture machine
types, with an appropriate, generic processor model assumed for
scheduling purposes.

The other options specify a specific processor.  Code generated under
those options will run best on that processor, and may not run at all on
others.

The B<-mcpu> options automatically enable or disable the
following options:

B<-maltivec  -mfprnd  -mhard-float  -mmfcrf  -mmultiple 
-mnew-mnemonics  -mpopcntb -mpopcntd  -mpower  -mpower2  -mpowerpc64 
-mpowerpc-gpopt  -mpowerpc-gfxopt  -msingle-float -mdouble-float 
-msimple-fpu -mstring  -mmulhw  -mdlmzb  -mmfpgpr -mvsx>

The particular options set for any particular CPU will vary between
compiler versions, depending on what setting seems to produce optimal
code for that CPU; it doesn't necessarily reflect the actual hardware's
capabilities.  If you wish to set an individual option to a particular
value, you may specify it after the B<-mcpu> option, like
B<-mcpu=970 -mno-altivec>.

On AIX, the B<-maltivec> and B<-mpowerpc64> options are
not enabled or disabled by the B<-mcpu> option at present because
AIX does not have full support for these options.  You may still
enable or disable them individually if you're sure it'll work in your
environment.


=item B<-mtune=>I<cpu_type>

Set the instruction scheduling parameters for machine type
I<cpu_type>, but do not set the architecture type, register usage, or
choice of mnemonics, as B<-mcpu=>I<cpu_type> would.  The same
values for I<cpu_type> are used for B<-mtune> as for
B<-mcpu>.  If both are specified, the code generated will use the
architecture, registers, and mnemonics set by B<-mcpu>, but the
scheduling parameters set by B<-mtune>.


=item B<-mcmodel=small>

Generate PowerPC64 code for the small model: The TOC is limited to
64k.


=item B<-mcmodel=medium>

Generate PowerPC64 code for the medium model: The TOC and other static
data may be up to a total of 4G in size.


=item B<-mcmodel=large>

Generate PowerPC64 code for the large model: The TOC may be up to 4G
in size.  Other data and code is only limited by the 64-bit address
space.


=item B<-maltivec>


=item B<-mno-altivec>

Generate code that uses (does not use) AltiVec instructions, and also
enable the use of built-in functions that allow more direct access to
the AltiVec instruction set.  You may also need to set
B<-mabi=altivec> to adjust the current ABI with AltiVec ABI
enhancements.


=item B<-mvrsave>


=item B<-mno-vrsave>

Generate VRSAVE instructions when generating AltiVec code.


=item B<-mgen-cell-microcode>

Generate Cell microcode instructions


=item B<-mwarn-cell-microcode>

Warning when a Cell microcode instruction is going to emitted.  An example
of a Cell microcode instruction is a variable shift.


=item B<-msecure-plt>

Generate code that allows ld and ld.so to build executables and shared
libraries with non-exec .plt and .got sections.  This is a PowerPC
32-bit SYSV ABI option.


=item B<-mbss-plt>

Generate code that uses a BSS .plt section that ld.so fills in, and
requires .plt and .got sections that are both writable and executable.
This is a PowerPC 32-bit SYSV ABI option.


=item B<-misel>


=item B<-mno-isel>

This switch enables or disables the generation of ISEL instructions.


=item B<-misel=>I<yes/no>

This switch has been deprecated.  Use B<-misel> and
B<-mno-isel> instead.


=item B<-mspe>


=item B<-mno-spe>

This switch enables or disables the generation of SPE simd
instructions.


=item B<-mpaired>


=item B<-mno-paired>

This switch enables or disables the generation of PAIRED simd
instructions.


=item B<-mspe=>I<yes/no>

This option has been deprecated.  Use B<-mspe> and
B<-mno-spe> instead.


=item B<-mvsx>


=item B<-mno-vsx>

Generate code that uses (does not use) vector/scalar (VSX)
instructions, and also enable the use of built-in functions that allow
more direct access to the VSX instruction set.


=item B<-mfloat-gprs=>I<yes/single/double/no>


=item B<-mfloat-gprs>

This switch enables or disables the generation of floating point
operations on the general purpose registers for architectures that
support it.

The argument I<yes> or I<single> enables the use of
single-precision floating point operations.

The argument I<double> enables the use of single and
double-precision floating point operations.

The argument I<no> disables floating point operations on the
general purpose registers.

This option is currently only available on the MPC854x.


=item B<-m32>


=item B<-m64>

Generate code for 32-bit or 64-bit environments of Darwin and SVR4
targets (including GNU/Linux).  The 32-bit environment sets int, long
and pointer to 32 bits and generates code that runs on any PowerPC
variant.  The 64-bit environment sets int to 32 bits and long and
pointer to 64 bits, and generates code for PowerPC64, as for
B<-mpowerpc64>.


=item B<-mfull-toc>


=item B<-mno-fp-in-toc>


=item B<-mno-sum-in-toc>


=item B<-mminimal-toc>

Modify generation of the TOC (Table Of Contents), which is created for
every executable file.  The B<-mfull-toc> option is selected by
default.  In that case, GCC will allocate at least one TOC entry for
each unique non-automatic variable reference in your program.  GCC
will also place floating-point constants in the TOC.  However, only
16,384 entries are available in the TOC.

If you receive a linker error message that saying you have overflowed
the available TOC space, you can reduce the amount of TOC space used
with the B<-mno-fp-in-toc> and B<-mno-sum-in-toc> options.
B<-mno-fp-in-toc> prevents GCC from putting floating-point
constants in the TOC and B<-mno-sum-in-toc> forces GCC to
generate code to calculate the sum of an address and a constant at
run-time instead of putting that sum into the TOC.  You may specify one
or both of these options.  Each causes GCC to produce very slightly
slower and larger code at the expense of conserving TOC space.

If you still run out of space in the TOC even when you specify both of
these options, specify B<-mminimal-toc> instead.  This option causes
GCC to make only one TOC entry for every file.  When you specify this
option, GCC will produce code that is slower and larger but which
uses extremely little TOC space.  You may wish to use this option
only on files that contain less frequently executed code.


=item B<-maix64>


=item B<-maix32>

Enable 64-bit AIX ABI and calling convention: 64-bit pointers, 64-bit
C<long> type, and the infrastructure needed to support them.
Specifying B<-maix64> implies B<-mpowerpc64> and
B<-mpowerpc>, while B<-maix32> disables the 64-bit ABI and
implies B<-mno-powerpc64>.  GCC defaults to B<-maix32>.


=item B<-mxl-compat>


=item B<-mno-xl-compat>

Produce code that conforms more closely to IBM XL compiler semantics
when using AIX-compatible ABI.  Pass floating-point arguments to
prototyped functions beyond the register save area (RSA) on the stack
in addition to argument FPRs.  Do not assume that most significant
double in 128-bit long double value is properly rounded when comparing
values and converting to double.  Use XL symbol names for long double
support routines.

The AIX calling convention was extended but not initially documented to
handle an obscure K&R C case of calling a function that takes the
address of its arguments with fewer arguments than declared.  IBM XL
compilers access floating point arguments which do not fit in the
RSA from the stack when a subroutine is compiled without
optimization.  Because always storing floating-point arguments on the
stack is inefficient and rarely needed, this option is not enabled by
default and only is necessary when calling subroutines compiled by IBM
XL compilers without optimization.


=item B<-mpe>

Support I<IBM RS/6000 SP> I<Parallel Environment> (PE).  Link an
application written to use message passing with special startup code to
enable the application to run.  The system must have PE installed in the
standard location (F</usr/lpp/ppe.poe/>), or the F<specs> file
must be overridden with the B<-specs=> option to specify the
appropriate directory location.  The Parallel Environment does not
support threads, so the B<-mpe> option and the B<-pthread>
option are incompatible.


=item B<-malign-natural>


=item B<-malign-power>

On AIX, 32-bit Darwin, and 64-bit PowerPC GNU/Linux, the option
B<-malign-natural> overrides the ABI-defined alignment of larger
types, such as floating-point doubles, on their natural size-based boundary.
The option B<-malign-power> instructs GCC to follow the ABI-specified
alignment rules.  GCC defaults to the standard alignment defined in the ABI.

On 64-bit Darwin, natural alignment is the default, and B<-malign-power>
is not supported.


=item B<-msoft-float>


=item B<-mhard-float>

Generate code that does not use (uses) the floating-point register set.
Software floating point emulation is provided if you use the
B<-msoft-float> option, and pass the option to GCC when linking.


=item B<-msingle-float>


=item B<-mdouble-float>

Generate code for single or double-precision floating point operations.
B<-mdouble-float> implies B<-msingle-float>.


=item B<-msimple-fpu>

Do not generate sqrt and div instructions for hardware floating point unit.


=item B<-mfpu>

Specify type of floating point unit.  Valid values are I<sp_lite>
(equivalent to -msingle-float -msimple-fpu), I<dp_lite> (equivalent
to -mdouble-float -msimple-fpu), I<sp_full> (equivalent to -msingle-float),
and I<dp_full> (equivalent to -mdouble-float).


=item B<-mxilinx-fpu>

Perform optimizations for floating point unit on Xilinx PPC 405/440.


=item B<-mmultiple>


=item B<-mno-multiple>

Generate code that uses (does not use) the load multiple word
instructions and the store multiple word instructions.  These
instructions are generated by default on POWER systems, and not
generated on PowerPC systems.  Do not use B<-mmultiple> on little
endian PowerPC systems, since those instructions do not work when the
processor is in little endian mode.  The exceptions are PPC740 and
PPC750 which permit the instructions usage in little endian mode.


=item B<-mstring>


=item B<-mno-string>

Generate code that uses (does not use) the load string instructions
and the store string word instructions to save multiple registers and
do small block moves.  These instructions are generated by default on
POWER systems, and not generated on PowerPC systems.  Do not use
B<-mstring> on little endian PowerPC systems, since those
instructions do not work when the processor is in little endian mode.
The exceptions are PPC740 and PPC750 which permit the instructions
usage in little endian mode.


=item B<-mupdate>


=item B<-mno-update>

Generate code that uses (does not use) the load or store instructions
that update the base register to the address of the calculated memory
location.  These instructions are generated by default.  If you use
B<-mno-update>, there is a small window between the time that the
stack pointer is updated and the address of the previous frame is
stored, which means code that walks the stack frame across interrupts or
signals may get corrupted data.


=item B<-mavoid-indexed-addresses>


=item B<-mno-avoid-indexed-addresses>

Generate code that tries to avoid (not avoid) the use of indexed load
or store instructions. These instructions can incur a performance
penalty on Power6 processors in certain situations, such as when
stepping through large arrays that cross a 16M boundary.  This option
is enabled by default when targetting Power6 and disabled otherwise.


=item B<-mfused-madd>


=item B<-mno-fused-madd>

Generate code that uses (does not use) the floating point multiply and
accumulate instructions.  These instructions are generated by default
if hardware floating point is used.  The machine dependent
B<-mfused-madd> option is now mapped to the machine independent
B<-ffp-contract=fast> option, and B<-mno-fused-madd> is
mapped to B<-ffp-contract=off>.


=item B<-mmulhw>


=item B<-mno-mulhw>

Generate code that uses (does not use) the half-word multiply and
multiply-accumulate instructions on the IBM 405, 440, 464 and 476 processors.
These instructions are generated by default when targetting those
processors.


=item B<-mdlmzb>


=item B<-mno-dlmzb>

Generate code that uses (does not use) the string-search B<dlmzb>
instruction on the IBM 405, 440, 464 and 476 processors.  This instruction is
generated by default when targetting those processors.


=item B<-mno-bit-align>


=item B<-mbit-align>

On System V.4 and embedded PowerPC systems do not (do) force structures
and unions that contain bit-fields to be aligned to the base type of the
bit-field.

For example, by default a structure containing nothing but 8
C<unsigned> bit-fields of length 1 would be aligned to a 4 byte
boundary and have a size of 4 bytes.  By using B<-mno-bit-align>,
the structure would be aligned to a 1 byte boundary and be one byte in
size.


=item B<-mno-strict-align>


=item B<-mstrict-align>

On System V.4 and embedded PowerPC systems do not (do) assume that
unaligned memory references will be handled by the system.


=item B<-mrelocatable>


=item B<-mno-relocatable>

Generate code that allows (does not allow) a static executable to be
relocated to a different address at runtime.  A simple embedded
PowerPC system loader should relocate the entire contents of
C<.got2> and 4-byte locations listed in the C<.fixup> section,
a table of 32-bit addresses generated by this option.  For this to
work, all objects linked together must be compiled with
B<-mrelocatable> or B<-mrelocatable-lib>.
B<-mrelocatable> code aligns the stack to an 8 byte boundary.


=item B<-mrelocatable-lib>


=item B<-mno-relocatable-lib>

Like B<-mrelocatable>, B<-mrelocatable-lib> generates a
C<.fixup> section to allow static executables to be relocated at
runtime, but B<-mrelocatable-lib> does not use the smaller stack
alignment of B<-mrelocatable>.  Objects compiled with
B<-mrelocatable-lib> may be linked with objects compiled with
any combination of the B<-mrelocatable> options.


=item B<-mno-toc>


=item B<-mtoc>

On System V.4 and embedded PowerPC systems do not (do) assume that
register 2 contains a pointer to a global area pointing to the addresses
used in the program.


=item B<-mlittle>


=item B<-mlittle-endian>

On System V.4 and embedded PowerPC systems compile code for the
processor in little endian mode.  The B<-mlittle-endian> option is
the same as B<-mlittle>.


=item B<-mbig>


=item B<-mbig-endian>

On System V.4 and embedded PowerPC systems compile code for the
processor in big endian mode.  The B<-mbig-endian> option is
the same as B<-mbig>.


=item B<-mdynamic-no-pic>

On Darwin and Mac OS X systems, compile code so that it is not
relocatable, but that its external references are relocatable.  The
resulting code is suitable for applications, but not shared
libraries.


=item B<-msingle-pic-base>

Treat the register used for PIC addressing as read-only, rather than
loading it in the prologue for each function.  The run-time system is
responsible for initializing this register with an appropriate value
before execution begins.


=item B<-mprioritize-restricted-insns=>I<priority>

This option controls the priority that is assigned to
dispatch-slot restricted instructions during the second scheduling
pass.  The argument I<priority> takes the value I<0/1/2> to assign
I<no/highest/second-highest> priority to dispatch slot restricted
instructions.


=item B<-msched-costly-dep=>I<dependence_type>

This option controls which dependences are considered costly
by the target during instruction scheduling.  The argument
I<dependence_type> takes one of the following values:
I<no>: no dependence is costly,
I<all>: all dependences are costly,
I<true_store_to_load>: a true dependence from store to load is costly,
I<store_to_load>: any dependence from store to load is costly,
I<number>: any dependence which latency E<gt>= I<number> is costly.


=item B<-minsert-sched-nops=>I<scheme>

This option controls which nop insertion scheme will be used during
the second scheduling pass.  The argument I<scheme> takes one of the
following values:
I<no>: Don't insert nops.
I<pad>: Pad with nops any dispatch group which has vacant issue slots,
according to the scheduler's grouping.
I<regroup_exact>: Insert nops to force costly dependent insns into
separate groups.  Insert exactly as many nops as needed to force an insn
to a new group, according to the estimated processor grouping.
I<number>: Insert nops to force costly dependent insns into
separate groups.  Insert I<number> nops to force an insn to a new group.


=item B<-mcall-sysv>

On System V.4 and embedded PowerPC systems compile code using calling
conventions that adheres to the March 1995 draft of the System V
Application Binary Interface, PowerPC processor supplement.  This is the
default unless you configured GCC using B<powerpc-*-eabiaix>.


=item B<-mcall-sysv-eabi>


=item B<-mcall-eabi>

Specify both B<-mcall-sysv> and B<-meabi> options.


=item B<-mcall-sysv-noeabi>

Specify both B<-mcall-sysv> and B<-mno-eabi> options.


=item B<-mcall-aixdesc>

On System V.4 and embedded PowerPC systems compile code for the AIX
operating system.


=item B<-mcall-linux>

On System V.4 and embedded PowerPC systems compile code for the
Linux-based GNU system.


=item B<-mcall-gnu>

On System V.4 and embedded PowerPC systems compile code for the
Hurd-based GNU system.


=item B<-mcall-freebsd>

On System V.4 and embedded PowerPC systems compile code for the
FreeBSD operating system.


=item B<-mcall-netbsd>

On System V.4 and embedded PowerPC systems compile code for the
NetBSD operating system.


=item B<-mcall-openbsd>

On System V.4 and embedded PowerPC systems compile code for the
OpenBSD operating system.


=item B<-maix-struct-return>

Return all structures in memory (as specified by the AIX ABI).


=item B<-msvr4-struct-return>

Return structures smaller than 8 bytes in registers (as specified by the
SVR4 ABI).


=item B<-mabi=>I<abi-type>

Extend the current ABI with a particular extension, or remove such extension.
Valid values are I<altivec>, I<no-altivec>, I<spe>,
I<no-spe>, I<ibmlongdouble>, I<ieeelongdouble>.


=item B<-mabi=spe>

Extend the current ABI with SPE ABI extensions.  This does not change
the default ABI, instead it adds the SPE ABI extensions to the current
ABI.


=item B<-mabi=no-spe>

Disable Booke SPE ABI extensions for the current ABI.


=item B<-mabi=ibmlongdouble>

Change the current ABI to use IBM extended precision long double.
This is a PowerPC 32-bit SYSV ABI option.


=item B<-mabi=ieeelongdouble>

Change the current ABI to use IEEE extended precision long double.
This is a PowerPC 32-bit Linux ABI option.


=item B<-mprototype>


=item B<-mno-prototype>

On System V.4 and embedded PowerPC systems assume that all calls to
variable argument functions are properly prototyped.  Otherwise, the
compiler must insert an instruction before every non prototyped call to
set or clear bit 6 of the condition code register (I<CR>) to
indicate whether floating point values were passed in the floating point
registers in case the function takes a variable arguments.  With
B<-mprototype>, only calls to prototyped variable argument functions
will set or clear the bit.


=item B<-msim>

On embedded PowerPC systems, assume that the startup module is called
F<sim-crt0.o> and that the standard C libraries are F<libsim.a> and
F<libc.a>.  This is the default for B<powerpc-*-eabisim>
configurations.


=item B<-mmvme>

On embedded PowerPC systems, assume that the startup module is called
F<crt0.o> and the standard C libraries are F<libmvme.a> and
F<libc.a>.


=item B<-mads>

On embedded PowerPC systems, assume that the startup module is called
F<crt0.o> and the standard C libraries are F<libads.a> and
F<libc.a>.


=item B<-myellowknife>

On embedded PowerPC systems, assume that the startup module is called
F<crt0.o> and the standard C libraries are F<libyk.a> and
F<libc.a>.


=item B<-mvxworks>

On System V.4 and embedded PowerPC systems, specify that you are
compiling for a VxWorks system.


=item B<-memb>

On embedded PowerPC systems, set the I<PPC_EMB> bit in the ELF flags
header to indicate that B<eabi> extended relocations are used.


=item B<-meabi>


=item B<-mno-eabi>

On System V.4 and embedded PowerPC systems do (do not) adhere to the
Embedded Applications Binary Interface (eabi) which is a set of
modifications to the System V.4 specifications.  Selecting B<-meabi>
means that the stack is aligned to an 8 byte boundary, a function
C<__eabi> is called to from C<main> to set up the eabi
environment, and the B<-msdata> option can use both C<r2> and
C<r13> to point to two separate small data areas.  Selecting
B<-mno-eabi> means that the stack is aligned to a 16 byte boundary,
do not call an initialization function from C<main>, and the
B<-msdata> option will only use C<r13> to point to a single
small data area.  The B<-meabi> option is on by default if you
configured GCC using one of the B<powerpc*-*-eabi*> options.


=item B<-msdata=eabi>

On System V.4 and embedded PowerPC systems, put small initialized
C<const> global and static data in the B<.sdata2> section, which
is pointed to by register C<r2>.  Put small initialized
non-C<const> global and static data in the B<.sdata> section,
which is pointed to by register C<r13>.  Put small uninitialized
global and static data in the B<.sbss> section, which is adjacent to
the B<.sdata> section.  The B<-msdata=eabi> option is
incompatible with the B<-mrelocatable> option.  The
B<-msdata=eabi> option also sets the B<-memb> option.


=item B<-msdata=sysv>

On System V.4 and embedded PowerPC systems, put small global and static
data in the B<.sdata> section, which is pointed to by register
C<r13>.  Put small uninitialized global and static data in the
B<.sbss> section, which is adjacent to the B<.sdata> section.
The B<-msdata=sysv> option is incompatible with the
B<-mrelocatable> option.


=item B<-msdata=default>


=item B<-msdata>

On System V.4 and embedded PowerPC systems, if B<-meabi> is used,
compile code the same as B<-msdata=eabi>, otherwise compile code the
same as B<-msdata=sysv>.


=item B<-msdata=data>

On System V.4 and embedded PowerPC systems, put small global
data in the B<.sdata> section.  Put small uninitialized global
data in the B<.sbss> section.  Do not use register C<r13>
to address small data however.  This is the default behavior unless
other B<-msdata> options are used.


=item B<-msdata=none>


=item B<-mno-sdata>

On embedded PowerPC systems, put all initialized global and static data
in the B<.data> section, and all uninitialized data in the
B<.bss> section.


=item B<-mblock-move-inline-limit=>I<num>

Inline all block moves (such as calls to C<memcpy> or structure
copies) less than or equal to I<num> bytes.  The minimum value for
I<num> is 32 bytes on 32-bit targets and 64 bytes on 64-bit
targets.  The default value is target-specific.


=item B<-G> I<num>

On embedded PowerPC systems, put global and static items less than or
equal to I<num> bytes into the small data or bss sections instead of
the normal data or bss section.  By default, I<num> is 8.  The
B<-G> I<num> switch is also passed to the linker.
All modules should be compiled with the same B<-G> I<num> value.


=item B<-mregnames>


=item B<-mno-regnames>

On System V.4 and embedded PowerPC systems do (do not) emit register
names in the assembly language output using symbolic forms.


=item B<-mlongcall>


=item B<-mno-longcall>

By default assume that all calls are far away so that a longer more
expensive calling sequence is required.  This is required for calls
further than 32 megabytes (33,554,432 bytes) from the current location.
A short call will be generated if the compiler knows
the call cannot be that far away.  This setting can be overridden by
the C<shortcall> function attribute, or by C<#pragma
longcall(0)>.

Some linkers are capable of detecting out-of-range calls and generating
glue code on the fly.  On these systems, long calls are unnecessary and
generate slower code.  As of this writing, the AIX linker can do this,
as can the GNU linker for PowerPC/64.  It is planned to add this feature
to the GNU linker for 32-bit PowerPC systems as well.

On Darwin/PPC systems, C<#pragma longcall> will generate "jbsr
callee, L42", plus a "branch island" (glue code).  The two target
addresses represent the callee and the "branch island".  The
Darwin/PPC linker will prefer the first address and generate a "bl
callee" if the PPC "bl" instruction will reach the callee directly;
otherwise, the linker will generate "bl L42" to call the "branch
island".  The "branch island" is appended to the body of the
calling function; it computes the full 32-bit address of the callee
and jumps to it.

On Mach-O (Darwin) systems, this option directs the compiler emit to
the glue for every direct call, and the Darwin linker decides whether
to use or discard it.

In the future, we may cause GCC to ignore all longcall specifications
when the linker is known to generate glue.


=item B<-mtls-markers>


=item B<-mno-tls-markers>

Mark (do not mark) calls to C<__tls_get_addr> with a relocation
specifying the function argument.  The relocation allows ld to
reliably associate function call with argument setup instructions for
TLS optimization, which in turn allows gcc to better schedule the
sequence.


=item B<-pthread>

Adds support for multithreading with the I<pthreads> library.
This option sets flags for both the preprocessor and linker.


=item B<-mrecip>


=item B<-mno-recip>

This option will enable GCC to use the reciprocal estimate and
reciprocal square root estimate instructions with additional
Newton-Raphson steps to increase precision instead of doing a divide or
square root and divide for floating point arguments.  You should use
the B<-ffast-math> option when using B<-mrecip> (or at
least B<-funsafe-math-optimizations>,
B<-finite-math-only>, B<-freciprocal-math> and
B<-fno-trapping-math>).  Note that while the throughput of the
sequence is generally higher than the throughput of the non-reciprocal
instruction, the precision of the sequence can be decreased by up to 2
ulp (i.e. the inverse of 1.0 equals 0.99999994) for reciprocal square
roots.


=item B<-mrecip=>I<opt>

This option allows to control which reciprocal estimate instructions
may be used.  I<opt> is a comma separated list of options, that may
be preceded by a C<!> to invert the option:
C<all>: enable all estimate instructions,
C<default>: enable the default instructions, equivalent to B<-mrecip>,
C<none>: disable all estimate instructions, equivalent to B<-mno-recip>;
C<div>: enable the reciprocal approximation instructions for both single and double precision;
C<divf>: enable the single precision reciprocal approximation instructions;
C<divd>: enable the double precision reciprocal approximation instructions;
C<rsqrt>: enable the reciprocal square root approximation instructions for both single and double precision;
C<rsqrtf>: enable the single precision reciprocal square root approximation instructions;
C<rsqrtd>: enable the double precision reciprocal square root approximation instructions;

So for example, B<-mrecip=all,!rsqrtd> would enable the
all of the reciprocal estimate instructions, except for the
C<FRSQRTE>, C<XSRSQRTEDP>, and C<XVRSQRTEDP> instructions
which handle the double precision reciprocal square root calculations.


=item B<-mrecip-precision>


=item B<-mno-recip-precision>

Assume (do not assume) that the reciprocal estimate instructions
provide higher precision estimates than is mandated by the powerpc
ABI.  Selecting B<-mcpu=power6> or B<-mcpu=power7>
automatically selects B<-mrecip-precision>.  The double
precision square root estimate instructions are not generated by
default on low precision machines, since they do not provide an
estimate that converges after three steps.


=item B<-mveclibabi=>I<type>

Specifies the ABI type to use for vectorizing intrinsics using an
external library.  The only type supported at present is C<mass>,
which specifies to use IBM's Mathematical Acceleration Subsystem
(MASS) libraries for vectorizing intrinsics using external libraries.
GCC will currently emit calls to C<acosd2>, C<acosf4>,
C<acoshd2>, C<acoshf4>, C<asind2>, C<asinf4>,
C<asinhd2>, C<asinhf4>, C<atan2d2>, C<atan2f4>,
C<atand2>, C<atanf4>, C<atanhd2>, C<atanhf4>,
C<cbrtd2>, C<cbrtf4>, C<cosd2>, C<cosf4>,
C<coshd2>, C<coshf4>, C<erfcd2>, C<erfcf4>,
C<erfd2>, C<erff4>, C<exp2d2>, C<exp2f4>,
C<expd2>, C<expf4>, C<expm1d2>, C<expm1f4>,
C<hypotd2>, C<hypotf4>, C<lgammad2>, C<lgammaf4>,
C<log10d2>, C<log10f4>, C<log1pd2>, C<log1pf4>,
C<log2d2>, C<log2f4>, C<logd2>, C<logf4>,
C<powd2>, C<powf4>, C<sind2>, C<sinf4>, C<sinhd2>,
C<sinhf4>, C<sqrtd2>, C<sqrtf4>, C<tand2>,
C<tanf4>, C<tanhd2>, and C<tanhf4> when generating code
for power7.  Both B<-ftree-vectorize> and
B<-funsafe-math-optimizations> have to be enabled.  The MASS
libraries will have to be specified at link time.


=item B<-mfriz>


=item B<-mno-friz>

Generate (do not generate) the C<friz> instruction when the
B<-funsafe-math-optimizations> option is used to optimize
rounding a floating point value to 64-bit integer and back to floating
point.  The C<friz> instruction does not return the same value if
the floating point number is too large to fit in an integer.

=back



=head3 RX Options


These command line options are defined for RX targets:


=over 4


=item B<-m64bit-doubles>


=item B<-m32bit-doubles>

Make the C<double> data type be 64-bits (B<-m64bit-doubles>)
or 32-bits (B<-m32bit-doubles>) in size.  The default is
B<-m32bit-doubles>.  I<Note> RX floating point hardware only
works on 32-bit values, which is why the default is
B<-m32bit-doubles>.


=item B<-fpu>


=item B<-nofpu>

Enables (B<-fpu>) or disables (B<-nofpu>) the use of RX
floating point hardware.  The default is enabled for the I<RX600>
series and disabled for the I<RX200> series.

Floating point instructions will only be generated for 32-bit floating
point values however, so if the B<-m64bit-doubles> option is in
use then the FPU hardware will not be used for doubles.

I<Note> If the B<-fpu> option is enabled then
B<-funsafe-math-optimizations> is also enabled automatically.
This is because the RX FPU instructions are themselves unsafe.


=item B<-mcpu=>I<name>

Selects the type of RX CPU to be targeted.  Currently three types are
supported, the generic I<RX600> and I<RX200> series hardware and
the specific I<RX610> CPU.  The default is I<RX600>.

The only difference between I<RX600> and I<RX610> is that the
I<RX610> does not support the C<MVTIPL> instruction.

The I<RX200> series does not have a hardware floating point unit
and so B<-nofpu> is enabled by default when this type is
selected.


=item B<-mbig-endian-data>


=item B<-mlittle-endian-data>

Store data (but not code) in the big-endian format.  The default is
B<-mlittle-endian-data>, i.e. to store data in the little endian
format.


=item B<-msmall-data-limit=>I<N>

Specifies the maximum size in bytes of global and static variables
which can be placed into the small data area.  Using the small data
area can lead to smaller and faster code, but the size of area is
limited and it is up to the programmer to ensure that the area does
not overflow.  Also when the small data area is used one of the RX's
registers (C<r13>) is reserved for use pointing to this area, so
it is no longer available for use by the compiler.  This could result
in slower and/or larger code if variables which once could have been
held in C<r13> are now pushed onto the stack.

Note, common variables (variables which have not been initialised) and
constants are not placed into the small data area as they are assigned
to other sections in the output executable.

The default value is zero, which disables this feature.  Note, this
feature is not enabled by default with higher optimization levels
(B<-O2> etc) because of the potentially detrimental effects of
reserving register C<r13>.  It is up to the programmer to
experiment and discover whether this feature is of benefit to their
program.


=item B<-msim>


=item B<-mno-sim>

Use the simulator runtime.  The default is to use the libgloss board
specific runtime.


=item B<-mas100-syntax>


=item B<-mno-as100-syntax>

When generating assembler output use a syntax that is compatible with
Renesas's AS100 assembler.  This syntax can also be handled by the GAS
assembler but it has some restrictions so generating it is not the
default option.


=item B<-mmax-constant-size=>I<N>

Specifies the maximum size, in bytes, of a constant that can be used as
an operand in a RX instruction.  Although the RX instruction set does
allow constants of up to 4 bytes in length to be used in instructions,
a longer value equates to a longer instruction.  Thus in some
circumstances it can be beneficial to restrict the size of constants
that are used in instructions.  Constants that are too big are instead
placed into a constant pool and referenced via register indirection.

The value I<N> can be between 0 and 4.  A value of 0 (the default)
or 4 means that constants of any size are allowed.


=item B<-mrelax>

Enable linker relaxation.  Linker relaxation is a process whereby the
linker will attempt to reduce the size of a program by finding shorter
versions of various instructions.  Disabled by default.


=item B<-mint-register=>I<N>

Specify the number of registers to reserve for fast interrupt handler
functions.  The value I<N> can be between 0 and 4.  A value of 1
means that register C<r13> will be reserved for the exclusive use
of fast interrupt handlers.  A value of 2 reserves C<r13> and
C<r12>.  A value of 3 reserves C<r13>, C<r12> and
C<r11>, and a value of 4 reserves C<r13> through C<r10>.
A value of 0, the default, does not reserve any registers.


=item B<-msave-acc-in-interrupts>

Specifies that interrupt handler functions should preserve the
accumulator register.  This is only necessary if normal code might use
the accumulator register, for example because it performs 64-bit
multiplications.  The default is to ignore the accumulator as this
makes the interrupt handlers faster.


=back


I<Note:> The generic GCC command line B<-ffixed->I<reg>
has special significance to the RX port when used with the
C<interrupt> function attribute.  This attribute indicates a
function intended to process fast interrupts.  GCC will will ensure
that it only uses the registers C<r10>, C<r11>, C<r12>
and/or C<r13> and only provided that the normal use of the
corresponding registers have been restricted via the
B<-ffixed->I<reg> or B<-mint-register> command line
options.


=head3 S/390 and zSeries Options


These are the B<-m> options defined for the S/390 and zSeries architecture.


=over 4


=item B<-mhard-float>


=item B<-msoft-float>

Use (do not use) the hardware floating-point instructions and registers
for floating-point operations.  When B<-msoft-float> is specified,
functions in F<libgcc.a> will be used to perform floating-point
operations.  When B<-mhard-float> is specified, the compiler
generates IEEE floating-point instructions.  This is the default.


=item B<-mhard-dfp>


=item B<-mno-hard-dfp>

Use (do not use) the hardware decimal-floating-point instructions for
decimal-floating-point operations.  When B<-mno-hard-dfp> is
specified, functions in F<libgcc.a> will be used to perform
decimal-floating-point operations.  When B<-mhard-dfp> is
specified, the compiler generates decimal-floating-point hardware
instructions.  This is the default for B<-march=z9-ec> or higher.


=item B<-mlong-double-64>


=item B<-mlong-double-128>

These switches control the size of C<long double> type. A size
of 64bit makes the C<long double> type equivalent to the C<double>
type. This is the default.


=item B<-mbackchain>


=item B<-mno-backchain>

Store (do not store) the address of the caller's frame as backchain pointer
into the callee's stack frame.
A backchain may be needed to allow debugging using tools that do not understand
DWARF-2 call frame information.
When B<-mno-packed-stack> is in effect, the backchain pointer is stored
at the bottom of the stack frame; when B<-mpacked-stack> is in effect,
the backchain is placed into the topmost word of the 96/160 byte register
save area.

In general, code compiled with B<-mbackchain> is call-compatible with
code compiled with B<-mmo-backchain>; however, use of the backchain
for debugging purposes usually requires that the whole binary is built with
B<-mbackchain>.  Note that the combination of B<-mbackchain>,
B<-mpacked-stack> and B<-mhard-float> is not supported.  In order
to build a linux kernel use B<-msoft-float>.

The default is to not maintain the backchain.


=item B<-mpacked-stack>


=item B<-mno-packed-stack>

Use (do not use) the packed stack layout.  When B<-mno-packed-stack> is
specified, the compiler uses the all fields of the 96/160 byte register save
area only for their default purpose; unused fields still take up stack space.
When B<-mpacked-stack> is specified, register save slots are densely
packed at the top of the register save area; unused space is reused for other
purposes, allowing for more efficient use of the available stack space.
However, when B<-mbackchain> is also in effect, the topmost word of
the save area is always used to store the backchain, and the return address
register is always saved two words below the backchain.

As long as the stack frame backchain is not used, code generated with
B<-mpacked-stack> is call-compatible with code generated with
B<-mno-packed-stack>.  Note that some non-FSF releases of GCC 2.95 for
S/390 or zSeries generated code that uses the stack frame backchain at run
time, not just for debugging purposes.  Such code is not call-compatible
with code compiled with B<-mpacked-stack>.  Also, note that the
combination of B<-mbackchain>,
B<-mpacked-stack> and B<-mhard-float> is not supported.  In order
to build a linux kernel use B<-msoft-float>.

The default is to not use the packed stack layout.


=item B<-msmall-exec>


=item B<-mno-small-exec>

Generate (or do not generate) code using the C<bras> instruction
to do subroutine calls.
This only works reliably if the total executable size does not
exceed 64k.  The default is to use the C<basr> instruction instead,
which does not have this limitation.


=item B<-m64>


=item B<-m31>

When B<-m31> is specified, generate code compliant to the
GNU/Linux for S/390 ABI.  When B<-m64> is specified, generate
code compliant to the GNU/Linux for zSeries ABI.  This allows GCC in
particular to generate 64-bit instructions.  For the B<s390>
targets, the default is B<-m31>, while the B<s390x>
targets default to B<-m64>.


=item B<-mzarch>


=item B<-mesa>

When B<-mzarch> is specified, generate code using the
instructions available on z/Architecture.
When B<-mesa> is specified, generate code using the
instructions available on ESA/390.  Note that B<-mesa> is
not possible with B<-m64>.
When generating code compliant to the GNU/Linux for S/390 ABI,
the default is B<-mesa>.  When generating code compliant
to the GNU/Linux for zSeries ABI, the default is B<-mzarch>.


=item B<-mmvcle>


=item B<-mno-mvcle>

Generate (or do not generate) code using the C<mvcle> instruction
to perform block moves.  When B<-mno-mvcle> is specified,
use a C<mvc> loop instead.  This is the default unless optimizing for
size.


=item B<-mdebug>


=item B<-mno-debug>

Print (or do not print) additional debug information when compiling.
The default is to not print debug information.


=item B<-march=>I<cpu-type>

Generate code that will run on I<cpu-type>, which is the name of a system
representing a certain processor type.  Possible values for
I<cpu-type> are B<g5>, B<g6>, B<z900>, B<z990>,
B<z9-109>, B<z9-ec> and B<z10>.
When generating code using the instructions available on z/Architecture,
the default is B<-march=z900>.  Otherwise, the default is
B<-march=g5>.


=item B<-mtune=>I<cpu-type>

Tune to I<cpu-type> everything applicable about the generated code,
except for the ABI and the set of available instructions.
The list of I<cpu-type> values is the same as for B<-march>.
The default is the value used for B<-march>.


=item B<-mtpf-trace>


=item B<-mno-tpf-trace>

Generate code that adds (does not add) in TPF OS specific branches to trace
routines in the operating system.  This option is off by default, even
when compiling for the TPF OS.


=item B<-mfused-madd>


=item B<-mno-fused-madd>

Generate code that uses (does not use) the floating point multiply and
accumulate instructions.  These instructions are generated by default if
hardware floating point is used.


=item B<-mwarn-framesize=>I<framesize>

Emit a warning if the current function exceeds the given frame size.  Because
this is a compile time check it doesn't need to be a real problem when the program
runs.  It is intended to identify functions which most probably cause
a stack overflow.  It is useful to be used in an environment with limited stack
size e.g. the linux kernel.


=item B<-mwarn-dynamicstack>

Emit a warning if the function calls alloca or uses dynamically
sized arrays.  This is generally a bad idea with a limited stack size.


=item B<-mstack-guard=>I<stack-guard>


=item B<-mstack-size=>I<stack-size>

If these options are provided the s390 back end emits additional instructions in
the function prologue which trigger a trap if the stack size is I<stack-guard>
bytes above the I<stack-size> (remember that the stack on s390 grows downward).
If the I<stack-guard> option is omitted the smallest power of 2 larger than
the frame size of the compiled function is chosen.
These options are intended to be used to help debugging stack overflow problems.
The additionally emitted code causes only little overhead and hence can also be
used in production like systems without greater performance degradation.  The given
values have to be exact powers of 2 and I<stack-size> has to be greater than
I<stack-guard> without exceeding 64k.
In order to be efficient the extra code makes the assumption that the stack starts
at an address aligned to the value given by I<stack-size>.
The I<stack-guard> option can only be used in conjunction with I<stack-size>.

=back



=head3 Score Options


These options are defined for Score implementations:


=over 4


=item B<-meb>

Compile code for big endian mode.  This is the default.


=item B<-mel>

Compile code for little endian mode.


=item B<-mnhwloop>

Disable generate bcnz instruction.


=item B<-muls>

Enable generate unaligned load and store instruction.


=item B<-mmac>

Enable the use of multiply-accumulate instructions. Disabled by default.


=item B<-mscore5>

Specify the SCORE5 as the target architecture.


=item B<-mscore5u>

Specify the SCORE5U of the target architecture.


=item B<-mscore7>

Specify the SCORE7 as the target architecture. This is the default.


=item B<-mscore7d>

Specify the SCORE7D as the target architecture.

=back



=head3 SH Options


These B<-m> options are defined for the SH implementations:


=over 4


=item B<-m1>

Generate code for the SH1.


=item B<-m2>

Generate code for the SH2.


=item B<-m2e>

Generate code for the SH2e.


=item B<-m2a-nofpu>

Generate code for the SH2a without FPU, or for a SH2a-FPU in such a way
that the floating-point unit is not used.


=item B<-m2a-single-only>

Generate code for the SH2a-FPU, in such a way that no double-precision
floating point operations are used.


=item B<-m2a-single>

Generate code for the SH2a-FPU assuming the floating-point unit is in
single-precision mode by default.


=item B<-m2a>

Generate code for the SH2a-FPU assuming the floating-point unit is in
double-precision mode by default.


=item B<-m3>

Generate code for the SH3.


=item B<-m3e>

Generate code for the SH3e.


=item B<-m4-nofpu>

Generate code for the SH4 without a floating-point unit.


=item B<-m4-single-only>

Generate code for the SH4 with a floating-point unit that only
supports single-precision arithmetic.


=item B<-m4-single>

Generate code for the SH4 assuming the floating-point unit is in
single-precision mode by default.


=item B<-m4>

Generate code for the SH4.


=item B<-m4a-nofpu>

Generate code for the SH4al-dsp, or for a SH4a in such a way that the
floating-point unit is not used.


=item B<-m4a-single-only>

Generate code for the SH4a, in such a way that no double-precision
floating point operations are used.


=item B<-m4a-single>

Generate code for the SH4a assuming the floating-point unit is in
single-precision mode by default.


=item B<-m4a>

Generate code for the SH4a.


=item B<-m4al>

Same as B<-m4a-nofpu>, except that it implicitly passes
B<-dsp> to the assembler.  GCC doesn't generate any DSP
instructions at the moment.


=item B<-mb>

Compile code for the processor in big endian mode.


=item B<-ml>

Compile code for the processor in little endian mode.


=item B<-mdalign>

Align doubles at 64-bit boundaries.  Note that this changes the calling
conventions, and thus some functions from the standard C library will
not work unless you recompile it first with B<-mdalign>.


=item B<-mrelax>

Shorten some address references at link time, when possible; uses the
linker option B<-relax>.


=item B<-mbigtable>

Use 32-bit offsets in C<switch> tables.  The default is to use
16-bit offsets.


=item B<-mbitops>

Enable the use of bit manipulation instructions on SH2A.


=item B<-mfmovd>

Enable the use of the instruction C<fmovd>.  Check B<-mdalign> for
alignment constraints.


=item B<-mhitachi>

Comply with the calling conventions defined by Renesas.


=item B<-mrenesas>

Comply with the calling conventions defined by Renesas.


=item B<-mno-renesas>

Comply with the calling conventions defined for GCC before the Renesas
conventions were available.  This option is the default for all
targets of the SH toolchain except for B<sh-symbianelf>.


=item B<-mnomacsave>

Mark the C<MAC> register as call-clobbered, even if
B<-mhitachi> is given.


=item B<-mieee>

Increase IEEE-compliance of floating-point code.
At the moment, this is equivalent to B<-fno-finite-math-only>.
When generating 16 bit SH opcodes, getting IEEE-conforming results for
comparisons of NANs / infinities incurs extra overhead in every
floating point comparison, therefore the default is set to
B<-ffinite-math-only>.


=item B<-minline-ic_invalidate>

Inline code to invalidate instruction cache entries after setting up
nested function trampolines.
This option has no effect if -musermode is in effect and the selected
code generation option (e.g. -m4) does not allow the use of the icbi
instruction.
If the selected code generation option does not allow the use of the icbi
instruction, and -musermode is not in effect, the inlined code will
manipulate the instruction cache address array directly with an associative
write.  This not only requires privileged mode, but it will also
fail if the cache line had been mapped via the TLB and has become unmapped.


=item B<-misize>

Dump instruction size and location in the assembly code.


=item B<-mpadstruct>

This option is deprecated.  It pads structures to multiple of 4 bytes,
which is incompatible with the SH ABI.


=item B<-mspace>

Optimize for space instead of speed.  Implied by B<-Os>.


=item B<-mprefergot>

When generating position-independent code, emit function calls using
the Global Offset Table instead of the Procedure Linkage Table.


=item B<-musermode>

Don't generate privileged mode only code; implies -mno-inline-ic_invalidate
if the inlined code would not work in user mode.
This is the default when the target is C<sh-*-linux*>.


=item B<-multcost=>I<number>

Set the cost to assume for a multiply insn.


=item B<-mdiv=>I<strategy>

Set the division strategy to use for SHmedia code.  I<strategy> must be
one of: call, call2, fp, inv, inv:minlat, inv20u, inv20l, inv:call,
inv:call2, inv:fp .
"fp" performs the operation in floating point.  This has a very high latency,
but needs only a few instructions, so it might be a good choice if
your code has enough easily exploitable ILP to allow the compiler to
schedule the floating point instructions together with other instructions.
Division by zero causes a floating point exception.
"inv" uses integer operations to calculate the inverse of the divisor,
and then multiplies the dividend with the inverse.  This strategy allows
cse and hoisting of the inverse calculation.  Division by zero calculates
an unspecified result, but does not trap.
"inv:minlat" is a variant of "inv" where if no cse / hoisting opportunities
have been found, or if the entire operation has been hoisted to the same
place, the last stages of the inverse calculation are intertwined with the
final multiply to reduce the overall latency, at the expense of using a few
more instructions, and thus offering fewer scheduling opportunities with
other code.
"call" calls a library function that usually implements the inv:minlat
strategy.
